Honey Rose, aged 35, was found not guilty of the criminal charge of gross negligence manslaughter in August 2021. This verdict came following the death of Vincent Barker in July 2012 due to fluid accumulation on his brain. The court found that Rose had missed a condition during a routine eye test, which led to the boy's demise. The Barker family expressed their disappointment, stating they felt the appeal court ruling had left them "devastated." Although the judges acknowledged a "serious breach of duty" by Rose, they emphasized that such matters should be addressed by regulatory bodies rather than the courts. During her trial last year, it was revealed that Rose, a mother of three from Newham, east London, had conducted all necessary tests during Vincent's eye examination at the Ipswich branch of Boots on 15 February 2012. However, she failed to detect the boy's swollen optic discs, an indicator of hydrocephalus - fluid on the brain. Vincent, affectionately known as Vinnie, passed away in July 2012, approximately five months after the eye test. Sir Brian Leveson, sitting with two other judges at The Court of Appeal, stated, "This decision does not, in any sense, condone the negligence that the jury must have found to have been established at a high level in relation to the way that Ms Rose examined Vincent and failed to identify the defect which ultimately led to his death. 'That serious breach of duty is a matter for her regulator; in the context of this case, however, it does not constitute the crime of gross negligence manslaughter." In a statement issued after Monday's ruling, the Barker family said, "We are understandably devastated. We feel that the conviction of manslaughter by gross negligence should have been upheld. 'We remain in no doubt that if Honey Rose had not breached her duty of care to our son he would still be with us today." The family further added, "Not only was Vinnie 'let down' by his optometrist, but 'he has also been failed by the legal system'."
East Lindsey District Council intends to transfer the management of the So Festival, Embassy Theatre in Skegness, and Meridian Leisure Centre in Louth to a charitable trust. This move, officials assert, would enable these facilities to tap into new sources of funding and save the council at least £1 million over a five-year period without significant job losses. The council has faced a reduction of £1.9 million (13.9%) in central government funding this financial year, following a £1.2 million cut the previous year. The Meridian Leisure Centre, which was only recently opened in 2010, currently necessitates an annual subsidy of £250,000. Approximately £350,000 is spent on its events program, which encompasses the So Festival. Councillor Adam Grist, who oversees culture, leisure, and tourism at the authority, emphasized that action was necessary. "Given the current situation, maintaining the status quo is not an option," he said. "We can't simply stand still and expect the problem to resolve itself. Every service will be thoroughly reviewed, and we believe that by being proactive and taking this step, we are safeguarding the services we currently offer." Besides access to grants, charitable status would also provide business rate and VAT relief. Mr. Grist elaborated that the proposal entailed the council paying a decreasing management fee to the trust, while retaining ownership of the buildings and ensuring guarantees for employees' pay and conditions. The plans will be presented to the full council next month.

Over the span of fifteen years, the Cormorant, initially known as AirMule and valued at approximately $14 million (Â£11 million), has been designed for both rescue and military operations. Capable of carrying up to 500kg (1,100lb) at speeds of 185km/h (115mph), Urban Aeronautics claims its internal rotors make it safer than traditional helicopters. Not only can it take off and land vertically, but it can also navigate between buildings and under power lines. Urban Aeronautics CEO, Rafi Yoeli, stated, "The development of Cormorant signifies its progression from prototype to near-term production, and eventually commercialization of this groundbreaking technology for diverse applications and markets." The commercial drone sector is rapidly growing, with Amazon unveiling Prime Air, a drone-based delivery service, in the summer. In June, the passenger drone 184, produced by Chinese company Ehang, received approval for test flights in Nevada. Dr. Mirko Kovac, director of the Aerial Robotics Lab at Imperial College London, commented, "This proposed vehicle indicates a real potential for personal drones, not just for delivery and environmental monitoring, but also for transportation. While there is still much technological development required to ensure safety and user-friendliness, if this project reaches the market, it could disrupt urban mobility and make swift air travel accessible to the general public." Drone expert Dr. Ravi Vaidyanathan, from Imperial's department of mechanical engineering, referred to the recent flight of the Israeli drone as a "significant" moment in low-altitude navigation, and commended the initial focus on humanitarian rescue and military applications. "Identifying a niche market and establishing a safety record is a wise strategy," he told the BBC. However, while civilian drone use is expected to increase, the timeline remains uncertain due to regulatory hurdles such as takeoff and landing locations, altitude restrictions, and safety concerns. "There are numerous regulatory challenges that need to be addressed," he said.

Under the proposed changes, George Osborne stated that approximately 98% of homeowners in England and Wales would pay less in stamp duty than under the current system. This reduction would not affect those purchasing homes valued above £937,000, who would see an increase in their tax. Osborne referred to the current stamp duty as a "tax on aspiration" that he aims to reform. Currently, stamp duty is a tax applied throughout the UK, charging progressively higher rates based on the entire purchase price. This structure has often been criticized as a "slab tax" due to the sudden increases in stamp duty when the price surpasses the next threshold. For instance, a homebuyer purchasing a property for £250,000 would pay £2,500, or 1%, in stamp duty. If the price were £1 more, they would pay an additional £5,000, as they would then be charged 3% on the entire purchase price. Starting from midnight on December 3rd, the new stamp duty rates will only apply to the portion of the purchase price that falls within the particular duty band, making it more similar to income tax. For example, a homebuyer purchasing a house for £200,000 would pay nothing on the first £125,000 and then 2% of the next £75,000, resulting in a bill of £1,500. Previously, they would have paid 1% on the total purchase price, resulting in a bill of £2,000. Although the percentage rates appear higher in some cases, the overall charge will mostly be lower. A homebuyer purchasing an average-priced family home in England and Wales will now pay £4,500 less in stamp duty. The new system will also eliminate the steps or sudden jumps in existing stamp duty thresholds. From April 1st, 2015, stamp duty in Scotland will be replaced by Land and Buildings Transaction Tax (LBTT). Until that date, the new stamp duty rates will apply in Scotland as well. The new system in England and Wales will be similar to LBTT, with buyers only charged the higher rates on the portion of the property price that falls within that band. Under Scotland's LBTT, home

Commissioner Burn explained that a group of individuals had conspired to commit a terrorist act and had begun preparing for it. The suspected targets in this plot are said to include the Wolloomooloo Navy base, as well as the offices of the Australian Federal Police and NSW Police. The men in question, Abdullah Salihy, aged 24, and Mohammed Almouie, aged 20, have been identified in Australian media. Previous raids on the families of both men were reportedly part of Operation Appleby, according to media outlets. Operation Appleby is an ongoing police initiative aimed at a group of young men whom authorities suspect were planning a terrorist attack in Australia. To date, a total of 13 individuals have been arrested as part of this operation.
The International Paralympic Committee (IPC) taskforce, in a consensus decision, advises that the suspension of Russia, imposed in August, should remain. This suspension was a result of Russia's exclusion from the Rio Paralympics last September, due to evidence of systematic doping. The taskforce's report expresses apprehension about Russia's insufficient action prior to the 2018 Winter Olympics. Sir Philip Craven, IPC president, recently hinted to the BBC that Russia's athletes could still be barred from the 2019 Games. The suspension was imposed following investigations by the World Anti-Doping Agency, led by Richard McLaren, which revealed widespread drug use and alleged cover-ups involving government officials. However, the Russian authorities have not explicitly acknowledged or refuted McLaren's findings, according to the IPC taskforce report, which has been collaborating with the Russian Paralympic Committee (RPC). Andy Parkinson, chairman of the taskforce and CEO of British Rowing, stated, "As long as the issues leading to the suspension are not fully understood and resolved, there cannot be any significant cultural change." He also reported some positive developments since the taskforce's initiation, such as attempts to establish an effective short-term testing program. In a similar move, the athletics' world governing body, the IAAF, recently voted to prolong Russia's ban from international competition.
Abubakar Shekau, the longtime leader of Boko Haram, denounced the Islamic State's (IS) declaration that Abu Musab al-Barnawi had taken over as leader. Shekau accused al-Barnawi of attempting a coup against him. Boko Haram, an extremist group, seeks to overthrow Nigeria's government and establish an Islamic State in the north. In the past 18 months, the group has lost most of the territory it controlled due to a joint offensive by Nigerian forces and neighboring countries. Shekau was last heard from in an audio message last August, affirming his survival and denying being replaced, a claim also made in an IS video released in April. In a 10-minute audio message delivered in both Arabic and Hausa, Shekau seemed to distance Boko Haram from IS, yet he still recognized Abu Bakr al-Baghdadi as caliph. He claimed that some within Boko Haram had prevented him from communicating with al-Baghdadi. "I was asked to send my ideology in writing to the caliph, but it was manipulated by some people to serve their own selfish interests," he added, suggesting a coup attempt against him. He stated that he had sent eight different letters to IS leaders, but they failed to act on them, only to learn later that he had been replaced. He then labeled al-Barnawi and his followers as polytheist. Boko Haram has experienced splits before, but this latest division is the most serious to date. Shekau's outburst indicates deep-seated disagreements, which could potentially lead to clashes between the loyalists of the two leaders. It also signifies the group's weakness, possibly foreshadowing its eventual collapse. Military officials view the split as an indication that the group is nearing its end. However, some security analysts warn that the internal strife could make the group more dangerous and unpredictable. Nigeria and other regional forces will now need to capitalize on this factionalism to their advantage. Shekau assumed leadership of Boko Haram after its founder, Muhammad Yusuf, died in Nigerian police custody in July 2009. Under his leadership, Boko Haram became more radical, carried out more killings, and pledged allegiance to IS

Wenger lamented the strain on players like Ramsey and the difficult situation of integrating players back from the Euros without adequate preparation time. Arsenal's next fixture is against defending champions Leicester City next Saturday. In a thrilling encounter, Theo Walcott scored the opening goal for Wenger's team shortly after missing a penalty, only for Liverpool to respond with two goals from Philippe Coutinho and one each from Adam Lallana and new signing Sadio Mane. Liverpool manager Jurgen Klopp praised Mane's goal, before Arsenal's Alex Oxlade-Chamberlain and Calum Chambers scored to level the game. However, Klopp acknowledged that his team had momentarily lost focus, and took responsibility for the late lapse. "I have a significant role to play in the last half-hour," Klopp admitted. "It's unacceptable for a manager to celebrate a goal in such a situation, and I immediately realized my mistake." The St James Theatre, situated in Palace Street, Victoria, has been erected on the grounds that once housed the Westminster Theatre, which met its unfortunate demise due to a fire in 2002. The owners envision that the £7m complex will entice new spectators and facilitate the growth of commercial theatre beyond the West End. Kicking off the inaugural season is Bully Boy, a play penned by comedienne Sandi Toksvig. Originally constructed as a chapel in 1766, the building underwent several transformations, becoming a cinema in 1924 and subsequently a theatre in 1931. For numerous years, it was under the ownership of the Moral Re-Armament movement, which utilized it to stage shows imbued with moral or spiritual messages. The new complex comprises a 312-seat main auditorium and a studio space accommodating up to 150 individuals, along with apartments. The theatre will be equipped with multimedia broadcast facilities, which will be utilized to broadcast performances on its own BSkyB satellite channel.
In the two weeks following June 24, annuity rates have decreased by 3.5%, as stated by Tom McPhail, head of retirement policy at Hargreaves Lansdown. Before the Brexit vote, a 65-year-old with £100,000 in savings could have purchased an annual income of £5,069. However, the value of that pension has now dropped to £4,890, marking a new record low. Prior to the referendum, experts had anticipated this decline. Interestingly, a majority of individuals over the age of 60 voted for the UK's exit from the European Union. McPhail commented, "Annuity rates are plummeting to the bottom of the chart." He further explained that six months ago, a 60-year-old could secure better terms than what is currently being offered to a 65-year-old. Despite the historically low rates, McPhail cautions that there is no guarantee when or if they will increase. The decline in annuity rates is a long-term trend, driven by increasing life expectancy. However, the drop has been accelerated by the fall in bond yields since the Brexit vote. McPhail advises anyone considering the purchase of an annuity not to delay their decision due to the current lower rates. In his words, "If the question is, 'should I buy an annuity today?', then the answer is not to postpone the decision because today's rates are lower than in the past."

The authenticity of the test remains uncertain, and if it indeed occurred, its success would hold significance due to the difficulty in detecting submarine-launched ballistic missiles (SLBMs). This test takes place as North Korea prepares for a significant party congress next month. Under UN sanctions from 2006, North Korea is prohibited from nuclear tests and activities involving ballistic missile technology. According to a South Korean defense ministry spokesman, North Korea launched a projectile believed to be an SLBM around 6:30 pm (0930 GMT) in the East Sea (Sea of Japan) near the north-eastern port of Sinpo. South Korea is monitoring the North Korean military closely and maintaining a full defense posture. North Korea has yet to report the test in its official media, and doubts have been cast on past claims of similar tests. The US suggests that photographs showing a December launch were manipulated, while others believe the North has fired missiles from submerged platforms, but not from submarines. South Korea's Joint Chiefs of Staff stated that the missile traveled approximately 30km (19 miles), whereas a typical SLBM can travel at least 300km (186 miles). To date, North Korea has conducted four nuclear tests, with the first in October 2006 and the latest in January this year. The UN Security Council responded to the latest test by imposing its strongest sanctions to date over North Korea's nuclear weapons program. Last month, North Korea claimed to have developed nuclear warheads small enough to fit on ballistic missiles, although experts question the validity of these claims. Analysts believe the North may be preparing for a fifth test as a display of strength prior to the North Korean Workers' Party Congress, the first since 1980.
In Siyazan, at least one individual required hospitalization, and several others sustained injuries when police employed tear gas and rubber bullets to disperse protesting crowds. The demonstrators were incensed by deteriorating economic conditions, a consequence of the plummeting oil prices. Azerbaijan's economy is heavily reliant on oil, with almost half its GDP in 2014 attributed to the oil sector. Over the past 15 months, oil prices have dropped by 70%, reaching $31 per barrel in international markets. Consequently, Azerbaijan's currency, the manat, has significantly depreciated in value. The interior ministry claimed the protests were orchestrated by the opposition and religious extremists. In response to the crisis, the government has reduced the price of flour starting from Friday, according to Reuters. Additionally, they have waived VAT on wheat imports and the sale of bread and flour. To support the faltering manat, Azerbaijan's central bank has prohibited the sale of foreign exchange in commercial banks' bureaux de change. Azerbaijan's economy, heavily reliant on oil revenue, has been destabilized by the decline in global oil prices. Small businesses have been severely impacted in recent years due to alleged bribes demanded by officials. Corruption has been a significant obstacle for businesses in Azerbaijan, according to the International Monetary Fund. At the start of last year, the manat was nearly equivalent to the euro, but it was devalued against the dollar in February 2015. In December 2015, the Central Bank unpegged the manat from the dollar, which led to an immediate price hike. This adversely affected low-income families and those with substantial bank loans. Azerbaijan imports most basic consumer goods, although some agricultural products are locally produced, with raw materials for production imported from abroad. Despite repeated warnings from international and local analysts about the anticipated effects of oil dependency, the Azerbaijani government has taken minimal action to bolster other industries. In a country where media are under stringent control, dissenting voices are met with force. He claims that the Conservatives are being propped up by the DUP to maintain power after the recent election, and that this constitutes blatant bribery: money for votes. McClean argues that the deal contravenes the Good Friday Agreement, which stipulates that the government should exercise its power with "rigorous impartiality" for all people, regardless of their identities and traditions. McClean is pursuing the legal challenge as an individual, not on behalf of the Green Party. McClean's solicitor is a senior partner at Edwin Coe solicitors, who represented Deir Tozetti Dos Santos, one of the claimants in the successful Brexit challenge in the Supreme Court. Greene told the BBC that there has been a "public outcry" over the Tory-DUP deal. He emphasized that the issue is not about imposing views, but rather about upholding the rule of law. "This is about a citizen's right to question actions that appear questionable and to challenge them meaningfully in court," Greene said. Alberto Costa, a former government lawyer and now MP for South Leicestershire, told the BBC that the investment given to Northern Ireland as part of the deal was not a "personal inducement" and that Prime Minister Theresa May had a constitutional duty to form a government. Next April, a gala in the city will honor the illustrious career of a 77-year-old individual, Robert Redford. The event is organized by the society that oversees the annual New York Film Festival. They acknowledge Redford's influence as challenging to quantify in various aspects. Klainberg further adds, "We've grown up watching his films, from the early '60s to his current work. I believe he's also had some of the greatest co-stars ever, from Natalie Wood to Faye Dunaway, Barbra Streisand, and, of course, his famous camaraderie with Paul Newman." Redford's performance in All is Lost, which was screened at the New York Film Festival in 2013, was met with a standing ovation at its premiere at the Cannes Film Festival last year.
The study conducted by researchers from the Karolinska Institute in Stockholm reveals a correlation between height and the risk of developing various types of cancer, including breast cancer and skin cancer (melanoma). For every additional 10 centimeters of height, the risk of cancer development increases by 18% in women and 11% in men. Taller women have a 20% higher risk of breast cancer, while taller men and women experience a 30% increased risk of skin cancer. However, it's important to note that the study does not account for numerous risk factors, and experts advise against undue concern among tall individuals. To minimize the risk of cancer, the following measures are advised: - Previous research has indicated a link between height and an increased risk of cancer development, although the underlying cause remains unclear. - The researchers tracked a large group of Swedish adults over a period of more than 50 years to arrive at these findings. - Prof Dorothy Bennett, head of the molecular cell sciences research centre at St George's, University of London, suggests that the association between height and cancer risk could be due to the fact that taller individuals have more cells in their bodies, increasing the likelihood of one cell becoming cancerous. - Sarah Williams, health information manager at Cancer Research UK, emphasizes that the study did not account for factors such as smoking or breast screening. - Regardless of height, reducing the risk of cancer can be achieved by avoiding smoking, limiting alcohol consumption, maintaining a healthy diet, regular exercise, maintaining a healthy weight, and practicing safe sun exposure.

A cyberattack impacting 51 UPS Store franchises in 24 states led to the exposure of clients' personal data, including names, addresses, email addresses, and payment card information. The company stated that the malware responsible had been eradicated, and their services were now secure. Recently, a significant US hospital chain disclosed that their systems had been compromised, with the private information of approximately 4.5 million healthcare patients from Community Health Systems facilities stolen. Last week, SuperValu, a US grocery chain, also acknowledged a breach. Founded in 1907 as a messenger company, UPS now boasts over 4,450 franchised locations within the US, each independently owned and responsible for setting up their own network. The breaches, detected due to a notification from the US government, occurred between January and August. UPS reported no instances of the stolen data being utilized for fraudulent activities, but customers are advised to closely monitor their account activity for signs of unauthorized access. Tim Davis, president of The UPS Store, advised, "We urge you to stay vigilant by reviewing your account statements and monitoring your free credit reports." He further assured, "We have identified and contained the incident, and an internal review to investigate the breach has commenced."
Andrey Melnichenko's luxury sailing yacht, the Sailing Yacht A, has been detained in British Gibraltar territorial waters since Wednesday. Melnichenko's spokesperson remains optimistic about the imminent lifting of the order. The Bermuda-registered vessel, constructed by Nobiskrug, departed from the Kiel shipyard in northern Germany two weeks prior. With a length of 143m (469ft) and three masts, the tallest reaching 100m, the superyacht boasts a gross tonnage of 12,600 and is said to have cost at least €400m. Nobiskrug claims the vessel features an underwater observation pod, hybrid diesel-electric propulsion, and advanced navigation systems. Designed by Philippe Starck, Nobiskrug is demanding an outstanding payment of €9.8m, as well as €5.5m for subcontractors and interest charges. Valla Yachts Ltd, a Bermuda-based company, is the yacht's registered owner. In a statement to the BBC, a top Gibraltar court official, Admiralty Marshal Liam Yeats, confirmed the vessel's detainment. Melnichenko's spokesperson described the situation as a "technical problem," expressing confidence that the yacht will be returned to the owner's project team in the near future. Melnichenko, an industrialist with significant investments in Russia's fertilizer, coal, and energy sectors, boasts a net worth of $13.2bn, according to Forbes. In the event of a ship's arrest, as is the case with the Sailing Yacht A, it occurs when banks or creditors seek compensation from shipowners who are unable to meet their financial obligations, such as mortgage or loan payments. Typically, arrested ships are sold in a sealed-bids auction within six to eight weeks, following proof of the claim and the issuance of a judgment. The Gibraltar Port Authority assigns 'ship keepers' to safeguard the vessel and its contents, providing the crew with essentials like fuel, food, and water. Arrangements are also made for the crew to have shore leave.

At present, students in Northern Ireland receive their maintenance loan and grant payments three times a year, typically at the start of each academic term. Student leaders argue that transitioning to monthly payments would aid students in managing their finances more effectively. The Department of Education (DEL) estimates the cost of implementing this change to be between £250,000 and £350,000. The majority of students in Northern Ireland secure loans to cover their living expenses, with many also receiving additional support grants. Unlike loans, grants are means-tested and do not require repayment. In the academic year 2013/14, over 42,000 students in Northern Ireland borrowed a combined total of £131m in maintenance loans. However, nearly 25,000 students, approximately 60%, also received a support grant to help with living costs, amounting to over £68m. The amount of grant a student receives is contingent upon household income. Students from households with an income of £19,203 or less are eligible for the maximum grant of £3,475 annually. Students from households with income between £19,203 and £41,605 receive a partial grant, with 38% of students in Northern Ireland receiving the full grant and 22% receiving a partial grant in 2013/14. Similar to maintenance loans, grants are disbursed in September, January, and April. In Scotland, a monthly payment system is already in place, with students receiving a larger initial payment in September followed by smaller monthly payments from October to June. The President of NUS-USI, Fergal McFerran, supports the idea of monthly payments, stating, "Monthly support payments, with a larger September payment, could assist students in paying for accommodation deposits, household equipment, or study materials required at the start of the academic year." However, there is no indication that the level of maintenance loan or support grant will increase. Minister Stephen Farry of DEL announced the commencement of the consultation, stating, "Although there is limited flexibility in the current financial context regarding the overall level of support available to students, there is room to explore the timing of that support." The consultation concludes on September 27. Tuition fee loans, currently £3,805 per year for a Northern

In the European Olympic qualifying event semifinals, Irish flyweight Michaela Walsh was defeated by Armenia's Narek Abgaryan. Despite this loss, Walsh still has an opportunity to qualify for Rio as three spots are up for grabs on Saturday. Irish lightweight David Oliver Joyce suffered a close loss against Britain's Joe Cordina in his semifinal bout. Walsh managed to force Abgaryan to take a late standing count in the final moments of their flyweight semifinal, but the Armenian secured a well-deserved unanimous decision in Samsun. Walsh, a silver medalist at last year's European Games, will secure a Rio berth if she defeats Bulgaria's Daniel Asenov in a box-off on Saturday. Asenov was outpointed by Britain's Muhammad Ali in Friday's other flyweight semifinal. Walsh began the match with a clever left-right combination, but Abgaryan responded with several good shots, taking the first round on all judges' cards. Abgaryan dominated the second round with a powerful right hand, notably rocking Walsh on her heels. Trailing 20-18 on all cards heading into the final round, Walsh bravely attempted a comeback, but Abgaryan primarily kept out of reach. In the final seconds, it seemed as if the bell had already sounded, causing Abgaryan to lower his guard, but a flurry of Walsh's punches led to a standing count for the Armenian. However, Abgaryan had already done enough to secure the decision. Irish lightweight Joyce appeared visibly disappointed after the three judges all gave a 29-28 verdict to Britain's Cordina in their semifinal. Cordina seemed to have the edge in the first round, but Joyce appeared to decisively win the second round, only for two judges to award the round to Cordina with a 10-9 verdict. This left the European champion in control of the bout heading into the final round, and although Joyce was given the last session by two judges, the Irishman was unable to overcome the deficit. To add to Joyce's frustration, the judge who scored the fight level after two rounds gave the final session to the Welshman, resulting in a unanimous verdict.
Between April 2016 and March 2017, South Wales Fire and Rescue reported a significant increase in incidents, with 31 compared to 12 the previous year. This rise came alongside reports of verbal and physical abuse, including instances of bricks being thrown at crews. As for North Wales and Mid and West Wales fire services, they did not have specific figures when asked, but maintained a firm stance against such attacks, adopting a zero-tolerance policy. South Wales regions with the highest number of attacks on firefighters included Blaenau Gwent (13%), Caerphilly county (16%), Cardiff (19%), and Newport (23%). In response, South Wales Fire and Rescue is collaborating with Crimestoppers and the Gwent Police and Crime Commissioner to decrease the number of attacks and bring offenders to justice. Matthew Jones, head of the fire crime unit in South Wales, emphasized their mission to "save lives and protect property." He added, "It is disheartening when firefighters, who risk their lives to keep communities safe, are subjected to a lack of respect such as this." A North Wales Fire and Rescue spokeswoman stated that attacks on their firefighters are rare, considering the number of calls they attend each year. She reiterated, "We will not tolerate abuse or attacks directed towards our staff." A Mid and West Wales Fire and Rescue spokesman confirmed their zero-tolerance approach to any attacks on firefighters, assuring that those responsible will be reported, caught, and dealt with by the police.

With the complete liberalization of the German labor market, workers from Poland and seven other EU nations that joined in 2004 can now enter the workforce. The German government had initially resisted this move, delaying the opening of its economy to workers from the former communist countries. In an effort to safeguard German workers from cheaper labor, ministers have pledged their support. Estimating a yearly influx of 100,000 individuals, Labor Minister Ursula von der Leyen informed the widely-read Bild newspaper that increased scrutiny would be applied to sectors like construction and catering, where German unions express significant concerns about wages being driven down by immigrants. She emphasized that inspectors would ensure compliance with agreed minimum wages and proper registration of newcomers. Finance Minister Wolfgang Schaeuble added that his department would deploy an additional 150 officials to monitor industries, with a focus on detecting illegal workers. Apart from Poland, workers from the Czech Republic, Hungary, Slovenia, Slovakia, Estonia, Lithuania, and Latvia are eligible to participate in the German labor market. The ministers opted to announce their commitments through popular newspapers, potentially reflecting the apprehension among ordinary Germans, even as employers argue that the economy requires external talent for skilled roles. However, employers may not publicly acknowledge, but unions are concerned that businesses also seek unskilled workers at reduced wages.

Construction on the former Cardiff bus station site commenced in 2014, with BBC Wales relocating there in 2019. HM Revenue and Customs will relocate from their Cardiff base in Llanishen, joining other government departments such as the Office for the Secretary of State for Wales at the new hub. The site, adjacent to Cardiff Central railway station, currently houses firms like Hugh James solicitors, Hodge Bank, and Motonovo Finance. Cardiff council leader Huw Thomas deemed the announcement a crucial component of "the jigsaw," with plans also underway for a new bus interchange and a redevelopment of the nearby Brains Brewery site. Thomas stated, "Cardiff is competing with major cities across the UK as we strive to attract jobs and investment." A UK government spokesman confirmed the deal was part of an initiative to reduce its buildings from 800 to 200 by 2022. Secretary of State for Wales Alun Cairns stated the move would demonstrate the government's commitment to being "visible, accessible, and working to improve people's lives." HMRC chief executive Jon Thompson pledged to collaborate with Cardiff council on the redevelopment of their current base at Ty Glas, which employs 2,800 staff, following the move.

The researchers at Imperial College London examined health survey data spanning from 1994 to 2011, which they published in The Lancet. Their findings revealed a significant increase in the number of individuals receiving treatment for high blood pressure, as well as more effective treatment methods compared to two decades prior. They suggested that further improvements could potentially save additional lives. Their study indicated that the percentage of people with high blood pressure receiving treatment nearly doubled, rising from 32% in 1994 to 58% in 2011. Simultaneously, the proportion of people successfully reducing their blood pressure to normal levels tripled, increasing from 11% to 37%. Professor Neil Poulter, one of the researchers, told the BBC, "If we had treated people in 1994 as we did in 2011, we estimate it could have prevented up to 100,000 major events (heart attacks, strokes, and deaths)." He added, "It's truly impressive, saving a significant number of lives." The study predicts that 80% of patients will be successfully managing their blood pressure by 2022 if the current rate of improvement continues. Enhanced medications, increased public awareness, and financial incentives for doctors are believed to be driving factors behind this improvement. Regrettably, heart disease remains the leading cause of death in the UK, claiming 82,000 lives annually.

However, some service booklets from the event are now being sold on eBay for up to £200. The remains of Richard III were discovered beneath a car park in Leicester in August 2012. Despite a legal challenge, he was reburied in the cathedral during a series of events that spanned a week. The cathedral hosted three primary services - one to receive the coffin, one for the reinterment, and one for the unveiling of the sealed tomb. Notable attendees at Thursday's reinterment included the Countess of Wessex and actor Benedict Cumberbatch. Dean David Monteith further emphasized this, stating, "Death necessitates acknowledging the murky aspects of individuals, which is a given. However, we had more service booklets printed than required, and all three can be purchased from the cathedral for £12.50. This price covers our costs and contributes a small amount towards the cathedral's upkeep."
In Cuba, private ownership of broadcast media is prohibited, and the government controls all mainstream media outlets. As reported by Reporters Without Borders (RSF), Cuba is the only country in the Americas that does not permit a non-state independent press. The government's media primarily serve to disseminate propaganda for the regime. The Cuban government maintains a firm grip on the media, as evidenced by the denial of a travel visa for a Cuban blogger. The United States attempts to reach Cuban audiences through media outlets such as Washington-backed Radio-TV Marti, which claims to provide "balanced, uncensored" news for Cubans. By 2014, there were approximately 3.1 million internet users in Cuba, according to Internetlivestats.com. However, the US-based NGO Freedom House states that internet access is heavily restricted, making it difficult for most Cubans to connect from their homes. Bloggers who support dissidents have faced harassment and detention, Freedom House adds. Cuba is listed as one of RSF's "Enemies of the Internet." Since the submarine cable linking Cuba to Venezuela became operational, the authorities have been unable to blame connection problems on the US embargo, as claimed by the press freedom group.

The West of England Local Enterprise Partnership (LEP) has consented to allocate £53 million from the City Deal towards the construction of a new indoor venue. Bristol City Council intends to cover the remaining £38 million, which will be recouped through rental income from the venue's operator and other sources. This 12,000-seater venue will be situated in the Temple Quarter Enterprise Zone's core. A decision on the project is scheduled for the city council's budget meeting on February 18. Mayor of Bristol, George Ferguson, expressed his optimism, stating, "It's heartening that the LEP acknowledges the significance of utilizing City Deal funding for the development of an arena, which will significantly stimulate the local economy." Colin Skellett from the LEP noted that the economic advantages of having an arena for the region are widely recognized. He further emphasized that the fund exists to stimulate job creation and economic growth in the west of England, with the Bristol Arena being one of several projects designed to achieve this. Bristol City Council will commence the search for an independent company to manage the arena next month. This project forms part of the city's broader plan to regenerate approximately 170 acres of land surrounding Bristol Temple Meads station. Bristol City Council anticipates the arena will be completed by June 2017. Under the City Deal agreement, certain powers are devolved from central government to local authorities, enabling them to assume control over decisions affecting their area, promote economic growth, and determine how public funds should be allocated.

Launched in April 2014, the survey aimed to provide an overall assessment of participants' health and well-being, and suggest improved lifestyle choices. The Conservatives asserted that only 12,939 individuals from a population of 1,177,000 over-50s in Wales had completed the survey within the subsequent 23 months. Mr. Millar, who posed the questions when he served as the shadow health minister, stated the meager participation figures would further fuel public discontent over Labour's unfulfilled manifesto promise to deliver annual GP health checks for all over-50s in Wales. He suggested that many older individuals lack the digital skills to utilize the service, while the Welsh Conservatives' current health spokesperson, Angela Burns, emphasized poor internet and phone connections in rural areas. "Individuals aged 50 and over are susceptible to a variety of health issues, such as cancer, high blood pressure, diabetes, and arthritis," said Ms. Burns. "It's evident that the program has been a dismal failure, and funds intended for its continuation should instead be directed towards initiatives aimed at recruiting and retaining GPs." A Welsh Government spokesperson defended the program as an innovative strategy, noting that initial costs were high but running costs amounted to £70,000 in 2015-16. Regarding the low usage, the spokesperson explained that other individuals were benefiting from charity and voluntary organizations using the platform as a basis for group discussions on health and lifestyle. "Research indicates that delivering targeted health information to the public is more effective than other methods," the spokesperson added. "Public Health Wales is examining its approach to public information provision with the intention of better supporting individuals to manage their own health and well-being. The lessons learned from Add to Your Life are playing a significant role in this examination."

Video footage from Sunday showed the flames and people hastily moving away. Out of the 12 people treated, eleven were hospitalized. As of Monday, eight people, including the man's 19-year-old son, were in stable condition in the hospital. Two individuals had already been discharged. The incident occurred during a burnout competition, according to a Red CentreNATS spokesperson speaking to the BBC. The event was subsequently halted following the incident, which is currently under investigation by the police. "This is a regrettable situation, but all patients are receiving the necessary care," Red CentreNATS stated. A 51-year-old individual from Westcliff is under investigation in relation to the death of Angela Millington, a 33-year-old resident of Southend. Her remains were discovered on Foulness Island by a group of hikers in June, but were only identified using DNA tests recently. The individual has been released on bail, with the investigation set to continue until November 17. Detective Chief Inspector Simon Werrett stated that some of Millington's remains were found on a specific area of Foulness Island known as New Burwood Head. Although there hasn't been a confirmed sighting of her since her visit to a housing officer in November 2013, where she withdrew money from her bank account, there have been unconfirmed reports of possible sightings in Southend in January and mid-February this year, according to Essex Police. Werrett further added that the investigation is still ongoing, with a focus on the possibility of Millington living or associating with someone in February this year. The police are eager to identify this person and are urging them to come forward.
The Family Planning Association's report, focusing on women in England and Wales, revealed that approximately 7% of women born in 1995 had at least one child before they turned 20. This is a significant decrease compared to the peak of 20% for women born in 1952. The decline in teenage motherhood has been gradual, now resembling the levels seen in the generation of women born in the early 1920s. The report also highlighted the average family size for women born in 1970, who by the age of 45 had an average of 1.91 children. This is slightly lower than their mothers' generation, represented by women born in 1943, who had an average of 2.24 children by the same age. However, the Office for National Statistics (ONS) noted that this decrease may not necessarily indicate a reduction in the number of children being born, but rather a delay in childbirth. Paul Casey from the Family Planning Association attributed this decline to teenagers having easy access to contraception and support services, and the confidence to use them. He emphasized the importance of maintaining this progress, especially considering ongoing debates about sex and relationships education and recent cuts in sexual and reproductive health services. On the other hand, Dr. Ellie Lee, Director of the Centre for Parenting Culture Studies at the University of Kent, questioned whether this decline in teenage births is truly positive. She argued that the trajectory was already heading downwards before the Teenage Pregnancy Strategy was implemented. Dr. Lee criticized the strategy for promoting long-term contraceptives such as implants and injections, suggesting that it may have limited the choice-based discussions teenagers could have about their sexual and reproductive health. She further expressed concern about negative attitudes towards teenage mothers, which she believes are unjustified. Dr. Lee argued that there is no evidence to suggest that teenage mothers are any worse than any other mother.

A previously issued report has established that a combination of errors led to the tragic deaths of 11 infants, one mother, and a nine-year span at Furness General Hospital. Mr. Hunt has expressed a desire to investigate the part played by the Nursing and Midwifery Council (NMC) in these events. The Professional Standards Authority (PSA) will oversee this review. The preventable deaths occurred between 2004 and 2013 at the hospital, which is part of the Morecambe Bay NHS Foundation Trust. It was subsequently uncovered that the maternity unit at Furness General was dysfunctional, with subpar care delivered by underqualified staff. The working relationships between doctors and midwives were also found to be severely strained, with midwives referring to themselves as "the musketeers" as they pursued normal childbirth at any cost. Mr. Hunt has already advocated for the NMC to no longer oversee the statutory supervision of midwives in the UK, suggesting a shift towards a supervision model similar to that of other health professionals. The PSA review is to be initiated "as soon as possible," according to Mr. Hunt. He further stated, "Given the NMC's significance in maintaining high standards of care in nursing, health visiting, and midwifery, this review will offer the public and the NMC itself independent confirmation that all lessons from the Morecambe Bay incident have been absorbed and acted upon." NMC CEO Jackie Smith commented, "As an organization that values openness, transparency, and continuous improvement, we welcome the PSA's input in helping us identify learning from our handling of these cases in order to establish where we could do things differently should a similar situation arise now. "We cannot undo what has already transpired; however, we must progress by identifying how we should do things differently in the future." In 2014, the NMC faced criticism from the PSA regarding its handling of disciplinary cases related to the scandal-ridden Mid-Staffordshire NHS Trust.

The construction of the pipeline, a topic of debate for seven years, has been met with opposition from environmentalists, who argue that it would cause irreversible damage. The president, however, contended that the pipeline has been exaggerated in the climate change discourse, taking on an "overblown significance." The proposed pipeline was slated to span 1,179 miles (1,897km), transporting 800,000 barrels of oil daily from Alberta, Canada, to Steele City, Nebraska. Nevertheless, the president stated that the pipeline would not provide a significant long-term economic benefit. The news was criticized by Republican presidential candidates, with Jeb Bush referring to it as an assault on the U.S. economy. In February, the Republican-led Congress voted to initiate construction immediately, but Mr. Obama vetoed the bill, citing the need for environmental reports. Despite the seven-year struggle, Barack Obama has ultimately decided to halt the pipeline. This decision will likely be celebrated by environmentalists, criticized by Republican presidential candidates, and largely disregarded by a public that was not particularly invested in the issue. The president's decision provides Democratic presidential frontrunner Hillary Clinton with a favorable position, as she recently expressed her opposition to the pipeline. The decision comes as world leaders prepare to meet in Paris at the end of the month to finalize a significant global climate agreement. The agreement, to be reached at the UN Climate Change Conference or COP 21, could be a significant part of the president's legacy. Although the project is currently halted, the pipeline controversy is unlikely to fade soon. Proponents are expected to challenge the decision in court, and if a Republican is elected president next year, construction could still be approved. The pipeline also stirred controversy beyond Washington, straining relations between the former Canadian Prime Minister Stephen Harper and the U.S. president. President Obama has reportedly spoken with newly-elected Canadian Prime Minister Justin Trudeau, and they have agreed that the issue of climate change outweighs any disagreements over the pipeline.
Simon Roberts, 40, from Cefn Mawr, and Wayne McKenzie, 40, from Manchester, admitted charges of drugs conspiracy and money laundering at Caernarfon Crown Court on Thursday. In 2013, police conducted a covert operation, bugging Roberts's business premises, as revealed during the hearing. Other members of the gang were also imprisoned. Taxi driver David Taylor, 55, from Pentre Gwyn, Wrexham, served as a courier, alongside 44-year-old Philip Burke, from Whiston, Liverpool. The court sentenced Taylor to six years and eight months, and Burke to five years and four months. Burke was found with £45,000. David Arfon Jones, 31, from Ruabon, and Jason Maddocks-Jones, 35, from Chirk, received six-year sentences. Laura Roberts, 26, from Cefn Mawr, received an 18-month suspended sentence with 300 hours of unpaid work for facilitating banking arrangements for her husband. She was the only defendant to plead not guilty. Lyndsey Bradley, 26, from Liverpool, McKenzie's partner, received a 14-month suspended sentence, also with 300 hours unpaid work, for a similar offense. Judge Niclas Parry stated that the case involved a criminal syndicate importing cocaine into north Wales, primarily around the Wrexham area, in a complex operation, leading to substantial profits. He emphasized the immeasurable impact on the community.
Keith Taylor, the Green MEP for the South East, expressed concerns that a new runway could negatively impact air quality. Both airports have asserted they would adhere to legal air quality standards. The Airports Commission's consultation on this matter concludes this Friday, with a recommendation for airport expansion to the government expected in the near future. The commission was instructed to conduct an additional air quality assessment following the Supreme Court's deadline for the government to propose strategies for reducing air pollution levels. The commission is expected to recommend one of the three shortlisted proposals - two at Heathrow and one at Gatwick. However, the Green Party, local councils in the Heathrow region, and the Gatwick Area Conservation Campaign argue that the commission has underestimated the environmental impact of airport expansion. Mr. Taylor stated, "The truth is that constructing any significant new runways would be detrimental to air quality, would rule out any possibility of meeting our climate change targets, and would result in an unparalleled increase in noise levels." Stewart Wingate, Gatwick's CEO, acknowledged that air quality is now a crucial factor in expansion decisions. He, however, questioned the feasibility of a third Heathrow runway addressing the air quality issue, stating, "Heathrow currently violates legal air quality standards, and it is illogical to believe that a third runway at Heathrow would resolve the problem. In contrast, Gatwick has never breached legal air quality standards, and its location ensures it never will." Matt Gorman, Heathrow's director of sustainability and environment, countered Mr. Wingate's argument, asserting, "The commission's air quality analysis has confirmed that an expanded Heathrow will surpass the local air quality standards."

"Just last week, I met with my consultant who suggested I consult a surgeon for an operation, which I am financially unable to undergo," he explained. "Given my struggles, this diet is a drastic measure I've resorted to." The 36-year-old from Essex, having recently defeated Tom Ford in the second round of the World Grand Prix in Llandudno, stated that his diet is so restrictive that even veteran player Peter Ebdon would find it challenging. He further elaborated, "Let me assure you, it's disheartening - it's essentially a therapeutic diet. I've always had to manage Crohn's, and I will continue to do so." The former world number two and two-time World Championship finalist is currently ranked 32 globally. "I've endured a great deal, but now that cancer is no longer a concern, I prefer to focus on the past," he added. "My goal is to live a typical, healthy life like any other snooker player on the tour, and if I achieve that, I believe my performance will improve. However, at the moment, Crohn's continues to trouble me, so I must strive to manage it effectively."

The children's football teams of Kings Heath Concorde, located in Birmingham, are currently sponsored by a nightclub and tattoo parlor. The Birmingham County FA has deemed this sponsorship inappropriate as the services provided by these establishments are age-restricted, making advertising on children's kits a violation of the rules. The club, however, claims it cannot afford to replace each team's kit, which would cost approximately £400. Manager Russell Jukes has reportedly sent out 50 letters to local businesses seeking sponsorship. The Arena nightclub in Stourbridge and Warstones Body Art agreed to sponsor the teams. However, following a complaint, the FA informed the club that the teams were banned from playing in the sponsored kit and it must be changed. A letter from the Birmingham County FA to Mr. Jukes stated, "We believe that the advertising of a tattoo parlor and a nightclub on U10 and U8 team shirts would be in violation of Regulation A8. These are age-restricted products/services and thus unsuitable for players who are significantly younger than 18." Mr. Jukes stated that the club operates on a tight budget. "I am hoping that some firms will come forward and offer sponsorship. If we don't receive sponsorship, we will have to disband the two teams," he said. Approximately 30 children who attend training and play for the teams would be left disappointed if the teams were to disband.

Mastercard, an American financial services corporation, is set to acquire Vocalink for up to £869 million. Notably, Vocalink manages the UK's ATM network, Link, and Bacs, the automated clearing house that processes direct debits. This purchase follows Softbank's £24.3 billion offer for Arm Holdings, a Cambridge-based chip designer, which raised concerns about British companies becoming takeover targets post-Brexit. Vocalink, which is partly owned by major banks such as Barclays, Lloyds, and HSBC, is chaired by Sir John Gieve, former deputy governor of the Bank of England. A spokesperson for Vocalink confirmed that discussions with Mastercard have been ongoing for eight months, and they deny any opportunistic motives due to the falling value of sterling. The new Chancellor of the Exchequer, Philip Hammond, has expressed that this deal demonstrates Britain's continued appeal to international investors. Earlier this year, the Payment Systems Regulator (PSR) recommended that the banks relinquish their control over the UK's payment systems due to the hindrance it poses to competition and innovation. In the deal, Mastercard will acquire a 92.4% stake in Vocalink, with the remaining shares to be held by the banks for at least three years. The purchase price consists of an initial £700 million, with an additional payment of up to £169 million contingent upon meeting certain performance targets. The specifics of these targets have not been disclosed. Vocalink's spokesperson has confirmed that they have been in contact with the Treasury, the Bank of England, and the PSR regarding the transaction.

On Thursday, the recovery of an eighth body was confirmed from the Amtrak wreckage by fire officials. It has been stated that all 243 passengers on the train have been accounted for. Prior to derailing on a curve where the speed limit was 50 mph, the train was traveling at 106 mph (170 km/h). Federal investigators are still unsure as to why the train was moving at such a high speed. A cadaver dog conducted a search of the area and discovered the eighth body, which was then sent to the medical examiner's office. He denies any consumption of alcohol or drugs. Mr. Nutter described Mr. Bostian's actions as "clearly reckless and irresponsible." He questioned, "What was the engineer [driver] doing? Why was the train traveling at such a high speed?" A jury had initially cleared Harbinder Khatkar of a 2011 rape charge. Six weeks following the trial, Khatkar assaulted three women on a street in Derby and later invaded the homes of three other women, all on February 1st, 2018. Khatkar, aged 37, was subsequently re-tried and found guilty of the 2011 rape under double jeopardy laws. This marked his third trial for the same attack, as jurors failed to reach a verdict during the first trial. One of the women attacked on February 1st was repeatedly raped in her home over several hours, and Khatkar also threatened to harm her children. Judge John Burgess described Khatkar, who refused to attend sentencing at Derby Crown Court, as a "highly manipulative man with no regard for others." The judge stated, "The harm he inflicted on these women is unimaginable, especially the woman who was compelled to testify three times since 2011, including at two earlier trials." Ryze in Dalkeith will reopen on Thursday at 10:00, following a temporary closure due to safety concerns. In the three weeks following its launch on 23 January, over 100 incidents were reported at the 10,000 sq ft trampoline park. These incidents included a broken neck, back, and other bones, leading Midlothian Council to serve an improvement notice on the park's operators. Ryze stated that it had agreed to suspend trading until licensing issues were resolved and that its accident figures were "better than the industry norm." The incident rate represented only 0.39% of the 25,662 jumpers over the three-week period. Midlothian Council has now granted Ryze Edinburgh an indoor sports entertainment license, with additional specific conditions imposed to ensure safety. The company has cooperated fully with the council during the closure, reviewing and enhancing its operating processes, health and safety procedures, and first aid policies. All staff members are now first aid trained, and improvements have been made to the toilet areas and parking provision to accommodate high customer numbers. Ryze has also decided to create a dedicated "kiddy court" for children under six, featuring a foam pit and two 45-degree rectangular trampolines.
A rich tapestry of Ulster life, spanning from Winston Churchill's speech to nationalists in Belfast in 1912 to a 1963 Viking re-enactment in a County Down fishing town, is beautifully depicted in previously unseen archive footage. The British Film Institute (BFI) has made accessible hundreds of these unique films as part of the From Britain On Film project, allowing the public to view them online for free. The collection includes royal visits, Lord Mayor's shows, and Churchill's address in Belfast's Celtic Park, where he expressed his support for Home Rule. There are also numerous travelogues promoting Northern Ireland as a tourist destination, which played a significant role in attracting visitors before the Troubles. In the realm of sports, the 1930 Ulster Tourist Trophy race at the Ards circuit in County Down is captured on camera, drawing thousands of spectators and capturing the attention of the motorsport world. The real treasures, however, lie in the footage of everyday people performing extraordinary feats. Scenes from spinning mills and the construction of the M1 at Stockman's Lane in Belfast showcase Northern Ireland's thriving industry. Another film captures the first World Ploughing Championships held in Northern Ireland, with a local man, Lawrence McMillan, taking the title in the 1959 competition in Armoy, County Antrim. Perhaps most enlightening is a 1938 recording of the Royal Victoria Hospital in Belfast, offering a rare glimpse into its casualty department and operating theaters, a decade before the establishment of the National Health Service. Although medicine has advanced significantly since then, some aspects of healthcare in Northern Ireland appear to remain unchanged, as the film ends with an appeal to reduce waiting lists. Francis Jones, from Northern Ireland Screen, which gathered much of the footage, stated that the project's aim was to preserve fragile film that might otherwise be lost. He added, "There is a pure nostalgia value to film. It's different to reading a textbook or history book. To actually see the film has that kind of visceral appeal and really resonates with people." The entire From Britain On Film archive can be viewed at the BFI Player.

By the end of 2017, the telecommunications companies have been mandated to ensure voice coverage across at least 90% of the UK's geographical area. Currently, these companies fulfill a pledge to provide 3G coverage to 90% of UK buildings, but this still leaves many users experiencing dropped calls outside. In December, Ofcom published a report detailing the geographic coverage of 2G, 3G, and 4G voice and data for three out of the four operators. The report stated that the watchdog could not provide a figure for Three's 3G and 4G-based voice and call geographic coverage. In response, the Culture Secretary, Sajid Javid, stated, "For far too long, too many parts of the UK have suffered from poor mobile coverage, leaving them unable to make calls or send texts." He added that the new licence conditions would improve the UK's mobile phone coverage to a world-class standard. The Department for Culture, Media and Sport (DCMS) expects the changes to reduce the number of "not-spots" by two-thirds. In November, Javid warned the operators that he might force them to transfer customers to rival networks without charge if they did not improve coverage. The networks initially objected, citing potential battery life issues and system security risks. However, they agreed to amend their licences to meet the new goal in December. The 2017 target should decrease the frequency of black spots for mobile subscribers while traveling and visiting rural areas. Richard Lloyd, executive director of the consumers association Which?, expressed support for the government's actions, stating, "We now need to see swift action from the mobile phone companies, with financial penalties if they fail to deliver their commitments, to start putting 'not-spots' to an end." Ofcom also noted that EE, Three, and Vodafone are aiming to match O2 by providing 4G coverage to the interior of 98% of UK premises by the end of 2015. EU competition regulators are yet to rule on the plan, and Three may benefit from the fact that its parent company, Hutchison Whampoa, is attempting to purchase O2 from its current owner, Telefonica.

Fairy circles, characterized by barren ground encircled by grass and ranging from 2-15 meters in diameter, have been the subject of scientific debate for many years. These circles, initially believed to be confined to the Namib desert in southern Africa, have also been observed in western Australia. The cause of these formations has been a matter of contention, with some attributing them to sand termites eating the roots of vegetation, while others suggest competition between plant species is the culprit. A new theory, published in the journal Nature, proposes a unified explanation that combines both theories. Researchers from the universities of Strathclyde and Princeton posit that the interaction between termite activity and plant competition gives rise to fairy circles. Termites, by removing vegetation above their mounds to conserve water, create bare circles essential for their survival in arid environments. The surrounding vegetation, taking advantage of the increased moisture levels, grows taller and forms the circle. Dr. Juan Bonachela from the University of Strathclyde's Department of Mathematics and Statistics explains, "Our findings reconcile the two theories and offer a possible explanation for the regular vegetation patterns observed worldwide. In the case of fairy circles, termites remove vegetation on their mounds to increase moisture, which in turn encourages the growth of surrounding vegetation, forming the circle. This behavior, driven by competition among termite colonies, impacts the entire ecosystem, enabling it to withstand harsh conditions and recover more quickly from droughts." The research was based on field data from four continents and computer simulations.
The government of Sudan granted autonomy to South Sudan, a region predominantly inhabited by Christian and Animist individuals who had long been fighting for independence from the Arab Muslim-led north. Despite this, ongoing disputes, particularly over oil revenue sharing and border demarcation, have persisted, causing friction between the two newly formed nations. Sudan has been plagued by conflict for years. Two rounds of civil war between the north and south, which claimed the lives of approximately 1.5 million people, were followed by an ongoing conflict in the western region of Darfur, displacing over two million people and resulting in the deaths of more than 200,000. Sudan's population is approximately 45.7 million, and the official languages are Arabic and English. The majority of the population practices Islam. The life expectancy is 60 years for men and 64 years for women. The Sudanese pound is the national currency, and Omar Hassan al-Bashir serves as the president. Al-Bashir has been in power for over 25 years, seizing control in a coup in 1989 and being elected president in 1996. He has been re-elected several times, most recently in 2015, extending his term for another five years. Many opposition parties chose to boycott the election. Al-Bashir is wanted by the International Criminal Court on charges of genocide, war crimes, and crimes against humanity. Sudan's broadcasting is heavily regulated, with state TV and radio reflecting government policy. The private press voices opposition views, but the government wields its power to control what is published. As of July 2014, Sudan had 9.3 million internet users, with blogging subject to scrutiny. Key historical milestones in Sudan include the emergence of the Nubian Kingdom of Kush around 2000-1500 BC, the spread of Islam in Sudan in 651 AD, the Ottoman Empire's conquest of the northern part of the country in 1820-21, Sudan's rule under joint British-Egyptian authority from 1899-1955, Sudan's independence in 1956, the introduction of Sharia Islamic

In 2012, a staggering 668 rhinos were brutally slain for their horns, marking a near 50% increase from the previous year's toll. The majority of these tragic deaths occurred within South Africa's Kruger National Park, its largest wildlife sanctuary. The escalating demand for rhino horn in Asian markets is believed to be the primary catalyst for this slaughter. South Africa is home to approximately 75% of the global rhino population, numbering around 28,000 animals. In contrast, only 13 rhinos fell victim to poachers in 2007. Since then, the killings have escalated significantly. The rise is attributed to the belief in nations such as China and Vietnam that powdered rhino horn possesses medicinal properties and can aid in the treatment of diseases like cancer. A kilogram of horn can fetch up to $65,000. The allure of such substantial profits has drawn in organized crime syndicates, who employ a variety of advanced technologies in their attempts to capture and disfigure the animals. The South African government has responded with military personnel and surveillance aircraft, yet the rising figures suggest they are struggling to stem the tide. As per a recent report from TRAFFIC, a wildlife monitoring network, South Africa's rhino population is now confronting a poaching crisis that could lead to a significant population decline. "Rhinos are being illegally killed, their horns hacked off, and the animals left to bleed to death," states Sabri Zain, TRAFFIC's director of advocacy, "all for the trivial use of their horns as a hangover remedy." Since the beginning of this year, five more rhinos have been killed according to South African authorities. However, South Africa is not the only nation grappling with the threat to its endangered rhino population. India is home to over 2,200 rhinos, primarily found in the well-protected Kaziranga reserve. Despite the deployment of around 900 armed rangers and guards, poachers have still managed to claim 18 lives last year, up from ten in 2011. Follow Matt on twitter.

The appointment of Ajit Pai to head the Federal Communications Commission (FCC) has sparked apprehension among internet rights activists, as they fear that laws upholding net neutrality may now be in jeopardy. Net neutrality is the principle that all internet traffic should be treated equally, preventing internet service providers from charging more for a "fast lane" for data-heavy services like Netflix. Proponents of net neutrality, which includes companies like Google and Facebook, argue that it is essential for maintaining fair competition among online companies. They contend that if a new video service couldn't afford to be in the "fast lane," it would face difficulties competing against established platforms like YouTube or Vimeo. Historically, Republican members of the FCC have voted against net neutrality, and with President Trump's election, the commission will have a Republican majority. In a statement, Pai, who was already an FCC commissioner, expressed his intention to "shift from playing defense at the FCC to going on offense" and to "remove those rules that are holding back investment, innovation, and job creation." Critics of Pai, such as Craig Aaron, president of communications advocacy group Free Press, have voiced concerns about his stance on net neutrality, stating that he has been on the wrong side of most issues during his tenure at the FCC. Despite these concerns, the appointment was welcomed by telecoms giant Verizon, as well as other internet service providers like Comcast and AT&T. This support could be particularly beneficial for AT&T, as Pai is said to be supportive of the mega-merger between AT&T and Time Warner, which contradicts President Trump's previous assurance that the merger would not be approved due to concerns about concentration of power. Time Warner is also the parent company of CNN, which has received significant criticism from President Trump. In a statement, AT&T expressed their support for Pai's leadership, stating that he would work with his fellow commissioners to reestablish a "commonsense regulatory framework necessary to support the president’s agenda."
In an attempt to encourage greater caution among drivers, shared spaces are designed to eliminate traffic signs, pedestrian crossings, and even curbs. A survey commissioned by Lord Holmes of Richmond, involving 600 participants, indicated that a substantial 63% found their shared space experiences unsatisfactory. Lord Holmes expressed hope that this study would shift perceptions regarding "these hazardous and expensive planning errors." The study further revealed that over a third of the respondents deliberately avoid shared spaces, and there is a significant under-reporting of accidents in such areas. The visually-impaired Lord Holmes, a former Paralympian, urged an immediate halt to all shared space projects, stating, "An immediate moratorium on all shared spaces is indispensable." He continued, "Town centers are being transformed into perilous third-world traffic free-for-alls. Shared spaces are not a safe haven; overzealous councils are jeopardizing public safety for aesthetics, resulting in confusion, chaos, unnecessary expenses, and tragedy." The Department for Transport (DfT) defines shared spaces as "streets or places designed to enhance pedestrian flow and comfort by diminishing the predominance of motor vehicles and allowing all users to share the space rather than adhere to the rigid rules implied by more traditional designs." Lord Holmes contends that the survey's results unequivocally show that these schemes do not "improve pedestrian flow and comfort" and "allow all users to share the space." The report also suggests a legal challenge to shared spaces, as solicitor Chris Fry from Unity Law represents five visually impaired individuals who are suing their local authorities for the removal of pedestrian crossings from their shared spaces. Fry stated, "They are taking legal action against their local authorities because pedestrian crossings have been removed from their shared spaces. The notion is that pedestrians will make eye contact with drivers, leading to enhanced safety. However, the visually impaired cannot do that, and local authorities are in violation of their duty as service providers under the Equality Act to make reasonable accommodations to the shared space." A DfT spokesperson responded, "It is the responsibility of local authorities to evaluate the appropriateness of implementing a shared space scheme on their roads. Samsung anticipates achieving an operating profit of 8.4 trillion won (equivalent to $7.9 billion or £4.8 billion) during the January-March quarter, marking a 4% decrease compared to the same period last year. This decline follows a 6% drop in operating profit in the preceding quarter. The decreasing profits suggest the difficulties Samsung faces in increasing its earnings due to the falling prices of smartphones. Young Park, an analyst from Hyundai Securities, attributes Samsung's reduced profits to both shrinking margins in the smartphone market and a slowdown in the sector's growth. The success of Samsung's Galaxy line of smartphones has been a significant factor in its recent growth. However, the company is facing increasing competition as other manufacturers aim to capture a larger share of the market. Companies like Apple, HTC, and Chinese manufacturers such as Lenovo, ZTE, and Huawei are all seeking to expand their market presence. This competition has led to price reductions among vendors, which puts pressure on their profit margins. Earlier this year, Samsung had predicted that competition within the sector would become even more intense. One analyst suggested that as profit margins in the sector continue to decline, Samsung may need to reduce costs to maintain its earnings growth. "For additional growth this year, Samsung needs to focus on cost reduction and cutting marketing expenses," said Greg Roh, an analyst from HMC Investment and Securities. "In essence, Samsung has no choice but to improve internal efficiencies to avoid a further decline in its earnings."

In the heart of one of India's bustling metropolises, Ahmedabad, BBC's Rupa Jha encounters five diverse individuals, each leading unique lives. Among them is Ms. Parmar, a resilient woman who lost her parents at a tender age and has spent her life scavenging for discarded items to sell. She shares her hardships, stating, "The public can be cruel when I'm out working. I face lewd comments from inebriated men, attacks from stray dogs. It's more challenging now than it was in my youth." Her anger subsides, however, as she acknowledges the futility of dwelling on the unfairness of her circumstances, "I can't help but feel frustrated, but then I realize there's no point in being angry. This is the reality I live in." Ms. Parmar resides in a modest dwelling with her jobless son and daughter-in-law. Rising food prices have added to their struggles, compelling her to rely on inexpensive chilies as a substitute for vegetables. She earns approximately $20 monthly, dividing her day into two shifts – one in the morning and the other at night. In addition to her scavenging work, Ms. Parmar supplements her income by donating blood for clinical research. Despite India's progress in various sectors, she laments that her life has deteriorated. "The city now clears away more rubbish, leaving less for us to collect," she says, reflecting on the changes in her environment.
Visitors to Disney parks in the U.S., Paris, and Hong Kong will soon be prohibited from bringing selfie sticks into the parks. A spokeswoman explained to the BBC that handheld extension poles have become a growing safety concern for both guests and cast members due to potential injuries and interference with rides. Starting Tuesday, visitors will be required to leave their selfie sticks in lockers at the park entrances. Several football clubs, the National Gallery, and the All England Lawn Tennis & Croquet Club in Wimbledon have previously banned selfie sticks over similar concerns. Apple also prohibited the use of selfie sticks at its annual WWDC developers conference held in the U.S. this month. Although St John Ambulance has not reported a surge in selfie stick-related injuries, training officer Clive James offered advice for anyone injured. He suggested sitting down, holding a cold compress against the injury, and seeking medical help if symptoms of a worsening head injury, such as dizziness, nausea, confusion, or loss of memory, occur.
