Countries including Colombia, Ecuador, El Salvador, and Jamaica have advised women to postpone pregnancies due to concerns about the mosquito-borne Zika virus. This advice comes after an outbreak in Brazil, where nearly 4,000 babies were born with suspected microcephaly since October. The US Centers for Disease Control and Prevention has also warned pregnant women to avoid traveling to over 20 countries in the Americas and beyond where Zika cases have been reported. While the link between microcephaly and Zika is not yet confirmed, a small number of babies who died had the virus in their brains, and no other plausible cause for the surge in microcephaly has been proposed. The virus is not contagious and usually presents with flu-like symptoms. In Colombia, Health Minister Alejandro Gaviria has urged women to delay pregnancies for up to eight months. Brazil's health ministry reports that 49 babies with suspected microcephaly have died, with five of these cases showing evidence of a Zika virus infection. The largest known outbreak of Zika is currently in Brazil, with most cases in the north-east, but others have also been detected in the south-east, including Rio de Janeiro and Sao Paulo. The number of Zika cases has risen sharply in several other Latin American countries, with over 13,500 cases reported in Colombia alone. The US Centers for Disease Control and Prevention issued initial travel warnings to pregnant women last week, and added eight more places to the list on Friday. London experienced a 6% decrease in overall recorded crime, with approximately 45,000 fewer incidents reported between 2012 and 2013. The Metropolitan Police attributed this decrease to proactive measures they have implemented. Notably, the launch of the Trident Gang Crime Command, which focuses on around 250 active criminal gangs in London using a combination of enforcement and preventative strategies, has had an impact. Thefts involving personal items, particularly mobile phones, increased by 17%. Former Metropolitan Police officer Mike Pannett expressed concern about the sustainability of this crime reduction, stating, "I am pleased with the news, but I am more interested in how this reduction will be maintained over the next two to three years." Offenses involving weapons also decreased, with a 20% reduction in knife crime and a 20% decrease in gun crime. However, the unit responsible for investigating firearms deaths lost its brief, as the force felt it created an "unhelpful" situation where murders were distinguished based on the weapon used, even though the same offenders could be involved in both knife and gun deaths. The number of homicides remained relatively stable, with 106 deaths compared to 103 the previous year - the lowest number in over 40 years. Victim Support suggested that the 16% increase in reported rapes might be due to people feeling more confident about reporting incidents. Jeff Gartner from the charity stated, "I believe there has been a significant effort by the Metropolitan Police to address the culture surrounding rape investigations, and I hope people are becoming more confident and are receiving adequate support when they report a rape." Mayor of London Boris Johnson praised the efforts of the police force, stating, "London is one of the safest large cities in the world, and it's wonderful news that crime rates are continuing to decline. This is a testament to the exceptional work our police are doing, and we will continue to keep police officers on the streets to combat crime."
On Tuesday, his wife, Barbara, announced that he had passed away at their home in Vermont, following a battle with leukemia. A renowned poet of his era, Kinnell authored over a dozen books throughout five decades. His 1982 collection, Selected Poems, earned him the Pulitzer Prize, and it also shared the National Book Award for Poetry with contemporary Charles Wright. Other notable works by Kinnell include The Book of Nightmares, inspired by the horror of the Vietnam War, When One Has Lived a Long Time Alone, and Mortal Acts, Mortal Words. One of his most famous poems is The Bear, which tells the story of a hunter who, after consuming the animal's blood and excrement, begins to identify with his prey. Other noteworthy poems include After Making Love We Hear Footsteps and When the Towers Fell, a reflection on the September 11 terrorist attacks in New York. Born in Providence, Rhode Island, on February 1, 1927, to Scottish and Irish immigrants, Kinnell was a quiet child, often described as almost mute. He developed a sense of isolation from others and realized that his happiness would be found in poetry. After attending Princeton University, where he shared a dorm with future U.S. poet laureate WS Merwin, who introduced him to the works of WB Yeats, Kinnell's breakthrough poem, The Avenue Bearing the Initial of Christ Into the New World, was published in 1960. This 14-part work was about Avenue C in Manhattan and the people who walked the street. Kinnell served as Vermont's poet laureate from 1989 to 1993 and moved there in 2005. He was later honored with the Wallace Stevens Award for lifetime achievement by the Academy of American Poets in 2010. Kinnell married his first wife, Spanish translator Ines Delgado de Torres, in 1965 and had two children, Fergus and Maud. However, they divorced 20 years later. In 1997, he married his second wife, Barbara.
Dr. Nihal Weerasena was determined to have committed numerous lapses in his care of five children and one adult patient during his tenure with Leeds Teaching Hospitals NHS Trust. The Medical Practitioners Tribunal Service (MPTS) imposed sanctions upon him following a finding of misconduct. The MPTS concluded that Dr. Weerasena displayed "reckless disregard for patient safety." Among the charges proven, the MPTS found that Dr. Weerasena demonstrated "substandard practice" in the treatment of a seven-year-old patient during a complex surgery in 2012. However, he was exonerated of neglecting a critical event during the operation as documented in his typed report, which was noted in his handwritten note. The panel also found that Dr. Weerasena failed to maintain an accurate record of surgery on a six-year-old patient in November 2010. An allegation concerning the care of a sixth child was not substantiated. The MPTS stated that Dr. Weerasena's misconduct, lack of self-awareness, and failure to take appropriate corrective measures were "fundamentally incompatible with continued registration." It added that "Mr. Weerasena should not practice again for public safety reasons and to safeguard the profession's reputation." Dr. Weerasena was reported to the General Medical Council in 2014 following a review of pediatric care services by the Trust, which included an examination of clinical outcomes. The review was conducted a year after children's heart operations at Leeds General Infirmary were temporarily halted at the behest of NHS England due to concerns about the safety of the unit. A subsequent report concluded that the unit did not have excess mortality rates. The surgeon did not appear at the hearing, but provided an explanation in writing and claimed to be a victim of a "cover-up."
In 2011, British artists represented 11.7% of all albums sold in the United States, according to the BPI, marking a significant increase from the 9.8% share they held in 2010. Adele's second album, 21, was the top-selling British album, moving 5.8 million units, followed by Mumford and Sons' Sigh No More with 1.4 million copies sold. A total of 30 UK albums surpassed the 100,000 copy threshold in the US. This was the highest percentage share for British artists since comparable data was first collected in 2000, and potentially the highest since the 1980s, the BPI noted. Other notable British albums of 2011 included Coldplay's Mylo Xyloto, Susan Boyle's Someone to Watch Over Me, and the two albums by Florence and the Machine. Adele's debut album, 19, sold over 900,000 copies the previous year, while Liverpool-born R&B singer Marsha Ambrosius was among the year's biggest British sellers with her solo debut, Late Nights and Early Mornings. Additionally, Jessie J, Tinie Tempah, Coldplay, Taio Cruz, and The Beatles were among the UK acts who sold more than a million single tracks in the US in 2011. BPI CEO Geoff Taylor suggested that the continued success of boybands like One Direction and The Wanted, both of whom are enjoying transatlantic chart success, would likely continue this trend. Taylor stated, "These results demonstrate that the risks UK labels take in investing in British talent... help British artists break internationally and generate significant exports that contribute positively to the UK economy."
Over the past three years, residential sales within a five-mile radius of the new railway stations have increased. Nevertheless, this growth is less significant compared to the broader local authority areas of Midlothian and the Scottish Borders. Since 2006, house prices have risen along the railway route, but the overall increase has been higher in the council areas. As of today, the volume of residential sales within five miles of the new stops has risen by 50.2% in Midlothian and 26.3% in the Borders, while the figures for the entire council areas rose by 60.6% and 30.1% respectively, and by 28.2% in Scotland as a whole. The Registers of Scotland (RoS) has reported that house prices have generally remained stable. Since the bill was approved to reopen the railway in 2006, average prices in the Midlothian catchment area have grown by 6.1% to £166,639, while houses within five miles of the new Borders stations have seen an average price increase of 4.5% to £143,283. The council-wide area figures for the same period are 13.4% in Midlothian and 9.5% in the Borders with the Scottish increase at 16.1%. Rhona Mackay, head of data at RoS, stated, "We have observed an increase in the volume of sales around the new Borders railway stations, but this is slightly lower than the volumes seen in the local authority areas as a whole. Prices are also up, but again this increase is lower in the five-mile catchment areas than the wider local authority areas where the growth is more substantial. It is anticipated that the new line will stimulate interest in the property market in the areas close to the stations, and there have been several new-build developments within the railway catchment areas in recent years. It will be intriguing to revisit these statistics once the new link is operational." The Borders Railway is set to open to the public on Sunday, connecting Tweedbank and Edinburgh with seven new stops along its length, including Newtongrange and Gorebridge in Midlothian, and Stow and Galashiels in the Borders. Glyptodonts, colossal creatures resembling armadillos with impenetrable shells, inhabited South America for millions of years, even growing to sizes comparable to cars. Previously considered close relatives of armadillos, new research now suggests they are not just cousins, but a subfamily. "Glyptodonts should be viewed as a subfamily of giant armadillos," stated Frederic Delsuc, researcher at the National Centre of Scientific Research (CNRS) and Montpellier University in France. Delsuc and his team used computational predictions to reconstruct potential DNA sequences of armadillo ancestors based on genes from living species. They then created RNA "bait" from these sequences and used it to fish for glyptodont DNA in a small museum fossil sample. This technique allows scientists to identify genuine DNA sequences from ancient species without contamination. The team successfully reconstructed the entire mitochondrial genome of a glyptodont, specifically from Doedicurus, one of the largest glyptodont species reaching up to 4m in length and weighing approximately 1.5 tonnes. These herbivorous behemoths gradually grew larger over time until their extinction at the end of the last Ice Age 10,000 years ago. They shared their era with other massive South American creatures such as elephant-sized ground sloths and giant, saber-toothed cats. Most significantly, Delsuc and his colleagues believe they have determined the position of glyptodonts in the tree of life, placing them deep within the "cingulata" order among numerous armadillo branches. "Glyptodonts represent an extinct lineage that likely originated 35 million years ago within the armadillo radiation," said co-author Hendrik Poinar from McMaster University, Canada. A key difference between this ancient group and modern armadillos is the glyptodont's large, dome-shaped shell, which was not articulated like the layered bands of modern armadillos. The researchers hypothesize that this single, rounded shield may have evolved alongside the "spectacular increase" in glyptodont size, which also explains why some older members of the family do, in fact, have banded shells.
In January, the Home Office granted posthumous pardons to numerous men who have since passed away, yet those still alive must apply to have their convictions expunged. Stonewall Cymru asserted that these men were unfairly persecuted, with many unaware they could seek exoneration. The Sexual Offences Act of 1967, which decriminalized sexual acts between men over the age of 21 in England and Wales, was enacted 50 years ago. Approximately 65,000 men were convicted under the old law, of which 15,000 are still alive. Mabli Jones, campaign, policy, and research manager at Stonewall Cymru, advocated for the removal of these convictions, stating, "The change in the law was a significant advancement, and we supported it. However, many men were unjustly persecuted by these laws and deserve to have them expunged." Some individuals remain unaware they can apply for a pardon, and Stonewall Cymru "welcomes" the possibility of automatic removal from records. A Home Office representative stated, "Convictions are not automatically deleted because the government must ensure that disregards are only granted to those who were convicted of offences involving consensual behavior and with individuals over the age of 16." The spokesperson also expressed pride in the government's record on improving equality. As of 2015, 1.6% of people in Wales identified as gay, lesbian, or bisexual, compared to 1.8% in the UK. The Aperture Spherical Telescope (FAST), with a width of 500 meters, equates to the size of approximately 30 football fields. This $180 million (£135 million) satellite project, reported by Xinhua news agency, aims to delve into space and search for potential extraterrestrial life. China's space program continues to be a significant focus for Beijing, and preparations for debugging and trials of the telescope are now underway, as confirmed by Zheng Xiaonian, the deputy head of the National Astronomical Observation under the Chinese Academy of Sciences to Xinhua. Set to replace the Arecibo Observatory in Puerto Rico, which boasts a diameter of around 300 meters, FAST will become the world's largest telescope. Although China has declared its space program to be of peaceful intent, the US Defence Department has asserted that it is pursuing activities to "prevent adversaries from utilizing space-based assets during a crisis," according to Reuters. Beijing plans to launch a "core module" for its first space station in 2018.
A survey of 250 school, youth, and health professionals in England indicates a significant number of children may not have sufficient food during school hours. The Children's Food Trust's survey revealed that 68.1% of respondents have noticed an increase in families struggling to feed their children over the past two years. The survey suggests that lunchboxes now contain less fruit and more junk food, with 47.5% of school staff reporting a change in the food children bring for lunch as household budgets tighten. One staff member described seeing "poorer quality sandwich fillings, sometimes just margarine," while another noted "fewer processed items - more leftovers or store-cupboard items." However, this staff member also acknowledged that some families only provide cold cooked rice or cold chips with fish fingers or similar, which may not be nutritiously balanced. The survey also found that 84.6% of professionals who participated had witnessed children without enough to eat during their work, with 84.8% of these respondents stating that approximately one-third of the children they work with are affected. The Children's Food Trust's CEO-designate, Linda Cregan, stated that many professionals who work with children are having to go beyond their duties to protect children from the effects of hunger and poor diet. Cregan added that while it is a parent's responsibility to ensure their child eats well, the reality is that this can be a significant challenge. She suggested that local authorities should ring-fence funding to support children's nutrition as they develop their public health plans, which could be used for training on cooking skills for local organizations, subsidizing good school food, breakfast clubs in schools, or grub clubs during holidays. Pupils at Priory School in Lewes told the BBC's School Report project that their lunches were generally good, with one 14-year-old stating that she had not seen people with chips in their lunchboxes, although the school does offer chips on Fridays. Another 13-year-old agreed that most people were quite healthy, taking a piece of fruit to school every day. The mummy known as Ta-Kesh at Maidstone Museum is set to become a more intriguing exhibit, as scientists aim to use a £78,700 grant to reconstruct her face and delve deeper into her background. believed to be a 14-year-old girl who perished around 2,700 years ago, Ta-Kush's true identity may be set to change history, according to museum staff. A CT scan on the mummy and her coffin has led to the discovery that her name has been misinterpreted for over a century. Egyptological studies in Cairo have revealed that her name is actually Ta-Kush, meaning the Kushite lady. Experts at Kent Institute of Medicine and Surgery (Kims) and Liverpool John Moore's University will use the grant to scan Ta-Kush, with the results being analyzed to digitally reconstruct her face, head, and shoulders. This process is expected to humanize Ta-Kush, allowing visitors to gain a visual understanding of her appearance. The scientific analysis should also provide definitive answers about her age and sex. Samantha Harris, the museum's collections manager, notes that while they currently believe she is a 14-year-old girl, there is a possibility that she may not be. Previous scans of mummies have revealed unexpected results, with some being found to be male. The ongoing work has already provided insights into Ta-Kush's origins, narrowing them down to the area around Thebes, as well as details about her parents and name, inscribed on her coffin. Lyn Palmer, who is managing the project, explains that the reconstruction process will take time, with the actual scan taking only five minutes, but the analysis and reconstruction requiring significant effort. Matt Travail, imaging manager at Kims, concurs, stating that the process will be different from scanning patients, with a focus on the analysis and reconstruction following the scan.
Security personnel clashed with inmates, resulting in one inmate's death and the wounding of four police officers, according to reports. The incident occurred as the inmates attempted to flee during an evacuation prompted by a fire. Authorities attributed the fire to a lightning strike. James Annan, spokesperson for the Kumasi prison, informed the AFP news agency that the inmates' cells had to be opened to save their lives. Kumasi-based journalist Erastus Asare Donkor told BBC Newsday that some inmates stole uniforms from prison officers and attempted to escape over a wall. Police reinforcements were called in to regain control as the situation seemed to be escalating. Inmates launched projectiles such as bricks, chairs, and other objects at the police, according to Mr. Annan. Officers responded by deploying tear gas, and one inmate was fatally shot when he attempted to seize a weapon. However, Mr. Donkor, who was present during the riot, stated that two inmates were killed and five officers were injured in the disturbance. Mr. Annan confirmed that no inmates managed to escape, and peace had been restored at the prison.

GP Energy has filed a planning proposal to conduct exploratory drilling beneath land adjacent to Commonwood Farm, Borras. The company maintains that the operation is solely for exploration and has no intentions of engaging in fracking, a contentious method for gas extraction from rock. However, North East Wales Against Fracking Action expresses local concerns. GP Energy's plans to drill a test borehole to investigate coal bed methane deposits underground are under review by Wrexham council. The location is approximately two miles from the River Dee, and opponents apprehend that test drilling could potentially lead to hydraulic fracturing, commonly known as fracking. Luke Ashley, spokesperson for the newly-established action group, voiced concerns that the drilling could mark the beginning of countryside industrialization to the east of Wrexham. He explained that the drilling aims to assess the gas content in coal seams spanning from Point of Ayr in Flintshire to Cheshire and Shropshire, and should full-scale extraction of coal bed methane ensue, it could involve injecting vast quantities of water and chemicals into the ground to release the gas. Ashley expressed fears that this could seep into nearby drinking water sources in the River Dee. He further stated that the group would be advocating with councillors prior to the plans being discussed by the council's planning committee, potentially later this year. A public meeting was scheduled for Tuesday evening. Fracking is a process that involves injecting water and chemicals into shale rock at high pressure to extract gas. It was temporarily prohibited in the UK following two earth tremors in Blackpool in 2011 that were attributed to fracking. Opponents argue that the water used in the fracking process will become contaminated and may contaminate domestic supplies. However, a UK government review has concluded that fracking is safe if properly monitored. Dart Energy, which owns GP Energy, recently informed the Wrexham Leader that it has no plans for fracking in the Wrexham area and stated that the well at the drilling site would be capped with cement once exploration is complete. A company representative told the newspaper, "Dart Energy confirms that the planning application is for the drilling of a coal bed methane exploration well, which is temporary in nature and will be fully cemented upon completion of the operation. A recent survey in Jersey indicates that 81% of residents endorse same-sex marriage. A petition advocating for equal rights for individuals identifying as lesbian, gay, bisexual, transgender, and questioning (LGBTQ) in the Channel Islands has amassed over a thousand signatures. Martin Gavet, chairman of the Channel Island rights group Liberate, expressed approval of the development. Deputy Nick Le Cornu, also of Reform Jersey, expressed his expectation of minimal opposition to the proposal. "I believe it's an open door," said Deputy Le Cornu. "There will always be those who are prejudiced and opposed on principle, but I think society is generally accepting of it."

Approximately 21,000 British soldiers, including around 7,000 Scots, perished in the Battle of Loos in 1915, with a significant number hailing from the 4th Black Watch, based in Dundee. The Prince and Camilla attended a service in the city to honor the fallen, with Prince Charles, as the Royal Colonel of the 3rd Battalion (The Black Watch), The Royal Regiment of Scotland, laying a wreath at a specially-commissioned memorial stone. His great uncle, Captain Fergus Bowes-Lyon, was among the casualties on the third day of fighting. The battle's first day, on September 25, 1915, claimed about 8,000 lives within the initial four hours. Commemorative events for the battle's centenary began on Friday with a beacon lighting atop the Dundee war memorial. Saturday's service featured a parade of around 250 current service personnel and 300 veterans, transforming City Square into a "cathedral" with representatives from the Scottish Episcopal, Roman Catholic Church of Scotland, Church of Scotland, and the Chaplain General to the Armed Forces. The prince, known as the Duke of Rothesay when in Scotland, expressed his admiration for the units involved, stating, "Their example is deeply humbling and is something in which the people of Scotland can take enormous and justifiable pride." In advance of the event, Ms. Sturgeon noted the impact of the battle on Scotland, stating, "The weekend of national commemorations will be a fitting tribute to those that fought, those that died, and those that were left at home." Lord Provost Bob Duncan emphasized the battle's impact on Dundee, stating, "This weekend presents an opportunity to remember their heroic sacrifice and to honor their memory." Following the service, Prince Charles and Camilla will attend a reception for forces personnel and veterans and view an exhibition on the Battle of Loos by pupils from Dundee schools.
The expenditure forms part of a novel strategy aimed at enhancing the management of Type 1 diabetes. Approximately 30,000 individuals in Scotland grapple with this condition. The government has emphasized a focus on expanding the utilization of insulin pumps, devices that can replace multiple daily injections. Currently, there are around 3,200 insulin pumps in use in Scotland. Furthermore, additional continuous glucose monitoring equipment will be made accessible. Karen Addington, CEO of the diabetes charity JDRF, expressed, "Research demonstrates that this Type 1 diabetes technology, coupled with support and training for its application, can significantly improve lives. We are pleased with the announcement of increased funding to improve access to it." Health Secretary Shona Robison stated, "We acknowledge that insulin pumps and continuous glucose monitoring kits can significantly simplify the management of Type 1 diabetes for some individuals. Proper control is crucial for improving outcomes and preventing complications from arising. This new investment will augment the provision of this equipment, making it available to a larger number of people and facilitating them to lead healthier lives."
The Sun, the largest celestial body within our Solar System, accounts for a staggering 99% of its total mass. Its diameter is approximately 109 times that of Earth, with enough space to accommodate more than a million Earths within its confines. Scientists believe that the Sun, along with the planets, originated from a solar nebula approximately 4.59 billion years ago, making it an ancient entity. The Sun's surface temperature hovers around 5,500 degrees Celsius, while its core reaches an astonishing 15.6 million degrees Celsius. Temperature variations exist within the Sun, with 'sun spots' being regions where temperatures are lower compared to other parts. Composed predominantly of hydrogen, the Sun also contains trace amounts of helium, oxygen, and even iron. Light emitted by the Sun takes just 8 minutes to reach Earth, a testament to its immense proximity. However, it's crucial to never gaze directly at the Sun, as doing so could cause irreversible damage to the eyes.
French Minister of Culture Françoise Nyssen mourned the loss of philosopher Anne Dufourmantelle, who tragically drowned at Pampelonne beach near St Tropez on Friday. The incident occurred after two children encountered difficulty in strong winds, with lifeguards subsequently rescuing them unharmed. Witnesses reported that Dufourmantelle, aged 53, was bathing approximately 50 meters from the children when an orange warning flag was changed to red, signifying dangerous conditions and a prohibition on bathing. Despite her immediate efforts to reach the children, she was swept away by a strong current. Resuscitation attempts following her recovery were unsuccessful, according to France 3 (in French). It remains uncertain if Dufourmantelle knew the children involved in the incident. Her funeral is scheduled for Tuesday in Ramatuelle, southern France. Dufourmantelle authored numerous essays emphasizing the significance of taking risks and the acceptance of potential threats as part of everyday life, including her 2011 book, Praise of Risk. Françoise Nyssen described Dufourmantelle as a "great philosopher who helped us live" on Twitter. Fellow French philosopher Raphaël Enthoven lamented her passing, praising her for her insightful commentary on dreams. In a 2015 interview with French daily Liberation, Dufourmantelle, who later worked as a columnist for the publication, expressed her views on the concept of "absolute security" and the illusion of "zero risk." She argued that fear can be manipulated as a tool for controlling freedoms and that increased protection and security measures can reinforce control and diminish life's freedoms. Dufourmantelle earned a doctoral degree in philosophy from Paris-Sorbonne in 1994 and later pursued a career in psychoanalysis. She was honored with the Raymond de Boyer Prize of Sainte-Suzanne for philosophy in 1998.

Ecometrica, based in Edinburgh, has created software for swiftly analyzing satellite imagery, enabling it to detect threats to tropical forests, such as illegal logging and encroaching agriculture. The UK Space Agency has granted a contract for this work, which will involve collaboration with experts in six additional countries to safeguard endangered ecosystems. The "Forests 2020" project seeks to enhance management and protection over 300 million hectares of tropical forests. Ecometrica will collaborate with partners in Brazil, Colombia, Ghana, Indonesia, Kenya, and Mexico, establishing earth observation laboratories to assess rainforest threats and guide conservation efforts. The Scottish company will engage experts from the University of Edinburgh, the University of Leicester, and another Edinburgh-based firm, Carbomap. Funding for this project comes from the UK Space Agency's newly launched International Partnership Programme (IPP), which unites British space knowledge and expertise to aid underdeveloped nations and developing economies. Ray Fielding, head of the IPP at the UK Space Agency, stated, "This program will discover innovative applications of space technology in this crucial area, as identified by the UN as essential for sustainable development, and we aim to make a substantial impact on the people on the ground working to protect the world's forests." Dr. Richard Tipper, executive chairman at Ecometrica, added, "We understand the critical role tropical rainforests play in the global ecosystem, but many are just starting to recognize the need for technology to ensure conservation efforts are efficient and properly directed. "The Earth Observation platforms will expedite the detection of threats like fires and illegal logging, making ground responses faster and more cost-effective."

Muse Developments has been selected to construct the £107 million redesign of the St Nicholas House district. Current demolition work is underway, with the objective of completing the project – which involves pedestrianizing a portion of Broad Street – by 2017. The blueprints will be submitted later this month. The plan encompasses office spaces for 250 vehicles, civic spaces, a 125-bed four-star hotel, and restaurants. A display of the complete Marischal Square plans and images can be viewed at Aberdeen Art Gallery on Friday and Saturday, and subsequently in the reception area of Marischal College from Monday until 17 April. Steve Turner, Muse's regional director for Scotland, commented: "The Marischal Square proposal aligns with the vision of the city's Bon Accord master plan by creating a new civic square, enhancing Marischal College's setting, and developing a dynamic mixed-use development consistent with Aberdeen's well-deserved reputation as one of the UK's premier business destinations. "Marischal Square has the potential to significantly impact the local economy, and it is crucial for the city to have a balanced blend of office and commercial space to seize the opportunities that will arise. "The new office and commercial buildings will replace the former St Nicholas House structure, adhering to the city's granite history and rising no higher than seven storeys, which is something the public expressed a desire to see reflected in Marischal Square." Deputy council leader Marie Boulton stated: "This project has now progressed to a very exciting phase, and I encourage everyone with an opinion on it to attend the exhibition at the art gallery to personally examine the plans, including the model of the scheme. "Additional viewing opportunities will be available at Marischal College after the weekend. "Regardless of your views on the Marischal Square proposals, I encourage the people of Aberdeen to voice their opinions." In 2011, over 1,000 council employees vacated the 14-story St Nicholas House building, built in 1968, for the recently renovated Marischal College building.
Since the 1970s, a total of 34 different employment programs have allegedly failed to address long-term and youth unemployment, according to Islwyn MP Chris Evans. The establishment of Jobcentre Plus in 2002 aimed to support jobseekers, yet Evans believes that successive Conservative and Labour governments have not been innovative enough in their welfare reform efforts. He criticizes previous programs such as the 1980s' Youth Training Programme (YTS) and Labour's New Deal in the 1990s, asserting they were ineffective. Evans argues that the current system is repetitive and ineffective, stating, "We're trying the same old tired solutions over and over again and getting the same results." Evans contends that Jobcentre Plus is not functional and plans to advocate for its abolition during a parliamentary debate on Tuesday. Instead, he proposes using the budget to contract services from charities and recruitment agencies with a proven track record. Evans notes that Jobcentre Plus is often seen as a place where people go to be penalized rather than to find work. In response, the Department for Work and Pensions (DWP) asserts that Jobcentre Plus offers an internationally respected service, tailored to individual and local labor market needs. The DWP claims that the service has helped over 10,000 people find work in Wales over the past year, with three quarters of those who leave Jobseeker's Allowance to take up a job still employed after eight months. Evans calls on the Labour party to take a more radical approach, stating, "For too long we opposed welfare reform rather than suggesting different solutions." He adds, "I think we lost the election because we went into the comfort zone. We have now got to talk about things we're uncomfortable about otherwise we're not going to look like a credible alternative government." Sunday Politics Wales will air on BBC1 Wales on July 5 at 11:00 BST.
Approximately 360 million account credentials, encompassing email addresses and passwords, have allegedly been exposed. Hold Security has also discovered 1.25 billion email addresses without corresponding passwords. The origin of these credentials, uncovered over the past three weeks, remains unknown. However, Hold Security indicated that they originate from major email providers. Experts find this batch exceptionally large, with online security consultant Graham Cluley describing it as "Godzilla-sized" and a "monster." He further added that while there may be some duplicates, the findings appear to be a veritable treasure trove for cybercriminals. Hold Security attributes their findings to "multiple breaches which we are independently investigating." On their website, they term the numbers "mind-boggling" and view the disclosure as a "call to action" regarding online security. According to Graham Cluley, these details could be utilized to access not only the accounts directly associated with them, but potentially others. He explains that "what normally comes out is not only spam and phishing attacks, but also that the combination of email and password can be used in multiple places because people use the same ones across different sites." If a large database of passwords is available, it can be used to determine common passwords, which can then be employed to access other accounts in the future. Reuters raised concerns that this discovery could pose a greater risk to consumers and companies than stolen credit card data, as the sets of usernames and passwords could potentially grant access to online bank accounts, corporate networks, health records, and virtually any other type of computer system. Alex Holden, chief information security officer of Hold Security, informed Reuters that the sheer volume is overwhelming. He stated that the credentials were stolen in breaches yet to be publicly reported. The affected companies may remain unaware until they are notified by third parties who have discovered evidence of hacking. Hold Security has staff working around the clock to identify the victims. Additionally, the batch includes email addresses not paired with passwords, which would be beneficial to individuals planning to launch spamming and phishing attacks.

Raynor, a 24-year-old resident of Barnsley, admitted four counts of causing death by dangerous driving. His passenger, Matthew Todd, also from Barnsley and 23 years old, received a nine-year sentence after admitting the same offenses. Ten other individuals were sentenced at Leeds Crown Court for terms ranging from 42 to 52 weeks, for their involvement in encouraging the dangerous driving that resulted in the deaths of Ryan Beal, 20, Brandon Brown, 20, Alexandra Binns, 18, and Terrie Kirby, 16. The events leading up to the fatalities were likened to the film The Fast and the Furious during the trial. In a joint statement, the families of the victims expressed, "No sentence can make up for what was done to our families on September 27, 2015. We lost sons and daughters who were just beginning their lives. We lost the opportunity to witness the people they would have become, and the families they could have had. The pain of that loss may lessen but will never disappear, and our families will always be left with the knowledge that they will never be whole again." Det Insp Richard Holmes of West Yorkshire Police described the crash as an "absolute tragedy" for the victims' families and one that had "severely impacted" the officers who handled the case. "On that fateful night, Raynor's car, with Todd as a passenger, was clocked at speeds of 140mph when it collided with the quad bike the four were on, resulting in the catastrophic injuries we sadly witnessed," he said. "Driving at this speed, on a 60mph road, was an act of unimaginable stupidity and recklessness that is hard to fathom. A recent investigation uncovered that thousands of books had been borrowed by fictitious patrons at the East Lake County library. As a result, library staff have been suspended while authorities determine if these fake borrowers influenced the library's receipt of additional state funding. The staff maintain that their actions were designed to save money by keeping popular titles that would have had to be repurchased if discarded due to lack of checkouts. According to officials from the Lake County public resources department, which oversees libraries in the Florida region, an anonymous tip led to the discovery of these fictitious readers at the East Lake County library. Lake County municipal staff's investigation found that several bogus identities were created by senior library staff, one of which was named after a retired major-league baseball player, Chuck Finley. Mr. Finley reportedly checked out over 2,600 books in nine months, with some titles being checked out and returned within an hour. The official report suggests that these fake borrowers inflated East Lake's readership numbers by nearly 4%. Readership numbers are significant because they contribute to the amount of official funding libraries receive. Last year, libraries in and around Lake County received approximately $1m (Â£820,000) from local government funds. In a statement to the Orlando Sentinel, the head librarian at East Lake claimed that the false borrowers were created to retain popular titles. He argued that following the computer system's recommendations to discard unread books would result in libraries having to repurchase titles they had just disposed of. He also alleged that many other libraries use "dummy" reader cards for the same purpose. Digital activist Cory Doctorow described the logging system as a "straitjacket" that disregarded the opinions of staff who understood reader preferences. Doctorow stated that the librarians did not manipulate the software for personal gain but rather to improve the system by teaching it to weigh circulation data based on on-the-ground intelligence.

He admitted to killing McGuigan, originally from Peebles, Scotland, and Hoare in August 2009. McGuigan, a former Royal Marine who had previously worked as a bodyguard for ex-Beatle George Harrison, was residing in Tameside at the time of his death. Kanye West will perform as the headliner on the Saturday night of this year's Glastonbury Festival, which takes place in June. The festival's organizer, Emily Eavis, shared the news via Twitter, stating, "We're delighted to announce that Kanye West will headline Saturday night on the Pyramid stage!" The Foo Fighters have been confirmed as the headliners for the Friday night, and the main act for the Sunday night remains undisclosed. Tickets for Glastonbury 2015 sold out within 30 minutes last October, with music enthusiasts paying £225 for a ticket to the event held at Worthy Farm in Somerset. In 2008, Noel Gallagher criticized the decision to have a hip-hop headliner when Jay Z performed on the Saturday. Initially, it appeared that Kanye West was unaware of the announcement, as he spent much of the day tweeting topless photos of his wife. Patients diagnosed with the disease are supposed to receive follow-up eye examinations, yet it has been discovered that nearly 500 patients have not received these tests since 2010. These examinations are crucial for detecting retinal damage caused by elevated blood sugar levels. Health officials have reached out to the affected individuals to schedule new appointments. Diabetic retinopathy is a common complication of diabetes that occurs when high blood sugar levels damage the cells at the back of the eye. If left untreated, it can lead to blindness. It has been uncovered that the system designed to ensure patients with the disease were referred for specialist eye examinations at local clinics failed to function properly. The University Hospitals of Morecambe Bay NHS Trust is currently investigating how these errors occurred. Diabetes UK has expressed concern that patients have not been receiving regular check-ups. A spokeswoman noted, "Diabetic retinopathy does not present any obvious symptoms in the early stages. By the time you notice a problem, the damage is quite severe." Sheila O'Brien of Cumbria Societies for the Blind stated, "It's unfortunate that this has occurred, but we are relieved that it has been brought to light and is being investigated. What is crucial now is to ensure that such incidents do not happen again." Approximately 160 patients in the Morecambe Bay area, 143 in North Cumbria, and nearly 200 in the Blackpool area are believed to be affected. George Nasmyth, the deputy medical director of The University Hospitals of Morecambe Bay NHS Foundation Trust, stated, "We are aware of an issue affecting a number of our diabetes patients and their annual eye screening appointments. We are collaborating with the Diabetic Eye Screening Programme, NHS England, and other NHS Trusts to determine the root cause of the problem and prevent it from recurring. Affected patients have been contacted directly and invited to attend a screening appointment. If anyone has concerns, they can contact 0845 0500 109."
The Prudential Regulation Authority (PRA) of the Bank of England and the Financial Conduct Authority (FCA) aim to curb imprudent risk-taking and short-term thinking within the financial sector. Under the revised regulations, senior executives may have their bonuses recouped for as long as ten years in instances of misconduct. Martin Wheatley, FCA's chief executive, stated these rules are integral to instilling an accountable culture in the financial district. He further emphasized that these regulations are essential for rebuilding public trust in financial services. The new rules, the result of an extended consultation process, will affect banks, building societies, and certain investment firms. Key modifications include: However, the Bank of England will not prohibit the buyout of unpaid bonuses by new employers, though the funds will not be accessible to managers any sooner than if they had remained with their previous company. Andrew Bailey, the Bank's deputy governor for prudential regulation and head of the PRA, explained, "Effective financial regulation involves establishing suitable incentives that encourage individuals to assume greater responsibility for their actions. Our objective is to ensure that those in positions of authority are rewarded for behaviors that foster a culture of effective risk management, thereby promoting the stability and reliability of individual financial institutions."
Healthcare professionals identified a 11-week old infant, referred to as "Baby D," with a broken arm and potential older rib fractures. The family, with a history of violence, had been known to Haringey children's services, who acknowledged that more could have been done to safeguard the child. A thorough review stated that risk factors were not given the necessary attention. Sir Paul Ennals, chair of the Haringey Local Safeguarding Children Board, expressed regret for several instances where improvements could have been made. He noted that numerous opportunities during the infant's first three months, and even earlier during the mother's pregnancy, were missed for intervention. Although it cannot be definitively stated that alternative actions would have prevented the abuse, the report indicates that various agencies could have and should have responded differently. The child's mother was found to be vulnerable, having been placed in care due to past neglect and violence. She was 17 when she became pregnant, and the father was 34. The baby lived with the mother's violent and criminal grandfather and a 14-year-old uncle. Despite suspicions of the family's involvement with gangs and illicit activities, social workers failed to adequately consider these risk factors. A serious case review found a culture of prioritizing targets and deadlines had led to inadequate analysis during assessments. Baby D was seen by hospital doctors on April 29, 2013, and the possible rib fractures are believed to have occurred when the baby was one month old. The infant was placed in care, and the parents were arrested for suspected abuse or neglect, but the criminal case was dismissed due to problems with the key witness. A council spokesperson confirmed that Haringey social services had accepted the findings of the review and was pleased to have found a successful care placement for the child. NHS England expressed concern over the lack of action taken to protect the child's health and safety. Haringey social services faced severe criticism in 2010 for their failure to protect Peter Connolly, also known as Baby P, who died at the age of 17 months after suffering over 50 injuries. Elite wheelchair tennis players, including Grand Slam champions and Paralympians, faced off at the inaugural Surbiton Wheelchair Tennis Tournament. This event served as a warm-up for Wimbledon, with thirteen of the world's top wheelchair players participating. The tournament marked the first opportunity for these athletes to test their grass-court skills in a competitive setting before Wimbledon, where three British players will be defending their titles. Wheelchair tennis is played on a standard-sized court using the same balls and rackets as traditional tennis. However, athletes use specialized wheelchairs, and the ball can bounce twice on each side of the net. Double Paralympic bronze medalist Lucy Shuker took up the sport after a motorbike accident left her paralyzed from the waist down. She has represented Great Britain at three Paralympics, winning a bronze medal in the doubles at both London 2012 and Rio 2016, partnering with Jordanne Whiley. Shuker recalls the initial skepticism she faced when she started playing, "When I first started, everyone said I was too disabled to compete." Despite being the most disabled player on the tour, Shuker has managed to develop a custom wheelchair and straps to aid her in competition. She notes that it presents daily challenges, but has made her stronger and more resilient in dealing with her disability. Britain's Alfie Hewett, fresh from winning the men's singles at this year's French Open, has a title to defend after winning the men's doubles at Wimbledon last year with Gordon Reid. Hewett began playing in 2005 and made his Paralympic debut in Rio, returning with two silver medals - one in the doubles with Reid, and another in the singles, after Reid defeated him. Hewett acknowledges that playing on grass requires a different approach, and he sees the Surbiton tournament as crucial for his Wimbledon preparations. On the opening day of the Surbiton tournament, Gustavo Fernandez, the reigning Australian Open champion and world number two, defeated Hewett in a reverse match, shortly after Hewett's triumph in Paris. Phil Everly, a significant figure in the Everly Brothers, one of the most influential pop acts of the 1950s and early 1960s, passed away in Burbank, Los Angeles, due to complications from lung disease. His wife, Patti, confirmed the news to the Los Angeles Times, expressing her profound sadness, "We are absolutely heartbroken." She added that his illness was a consequence of a lifetime of smoking. Don Everly, Phil's brother and partner in the Everly Brothers, expressed his deep sorrow to the Associated Press, stating, "I loved my brother very much. I always thought I'd be the one to go first. The world might be mourning an Everly Brother, but I'm mourning my brother Phil Everly." The Everly Brothers, known for their close-harmony hits like Wake Up Little Suzie, Cathy's Clown, Bye Bye Love, and All I Have To Do Is Dream, had a significant impact on the music industry, influencing acts such as the Beatles and the Beach Boys. Duane Eddy, a US rock pioneer and friend of Everly, expressed his grief to BBC Radio 5live, saying, "It's a terrible, terrible loss - for me, for everybody." Everly died on Friday from chronic obstructive pulmonary disease, as confirmed by his son Jason Everly to the Associated Press. Rolling Stone magazine hails the Everly Brothers as "the most important vocal duo in rock." The Everly Brothers, sons of Midwestern country music singers Ike and Margaret Everly, performed on their family radio show growing up. In their peak years between 1957 and 1962, they had 19 Top 40 hits, according to the Associated Press. The pair experienced an onstage breakup in 1973, leading to a decade-long estrangement, but Phil later told Time magazine their relationship had survived this. "Don and I are infamous for our split," Phil said, "but we're closer than most brothers." The Everly Brothers were inducted into the Rock 'n' Roll Hall of Fame in its first year, 1986, and they were awarded a lifetime achievement award at the Gramm

Previously, numerous individuals demonstrated outside Walsall Council House. The authority asserted that regulations must be adhered to prevent "places of remembrance from becoming disorganized and chaotic." In a statement, the council explained that part of the cemetery is a special memorial for children, and they have permitted the borders and ornaments to remain in that section. Mr. Matloob expressed, "We argue that you are discriminating against us and the broader community based on age. It should be one rule for everyone." The council defended its actions by following "the widely accepted lawn principle for new graves, which resembles war graves featuring straight rows of headstones and a uniform expanse of grass throughout the cemetery." In a statement, the council stated that the majority of people would comprehend the need for "proper management of the regulations in place." The council further stated, "It is crucial we protect the rights of our residents by maintaining the cemeteries in good condition. We have just completed a comprehensive two-month consultation period. We have made some minor adjustments to the rules and regulations, and these were confirmed by the Cabinet during its June meeting."
Senior police officials in the state declared on Wednesday that they had apprehended a militant following a firefight. Local news sources stated that the militant was subdued by two civilians he had kidnapped, who subsequently handed him over to authorities. A senior police officer asserted that the individual admitted to being a Pakistani national and had illegally entered India via the Line of Control, the unofficial boundary between the two nations. The capture of this individual has sparked comparisons to the apprehension of Qasab in 2008 by Indian media outlets. Qasab was a member of a 10-person militant group that infiltrated Mumbai by sea on November 26 and carried out attacks on several locations, including the renowned Taj Mahal hotel. Assaults on the railway station, luxury hotels, and a Jewish cultural center resulted in the deaths of 166 people. Nine militants were also killed, but Qasab was apprehended by a Mumbai police officer. He was executed in November 2012. The headlines of Indian newspapers on Thursday primarily revolved around the comparison, with some referring to the arrested militant as "Kasab II." Indian media outlets have also emphasized official statements suggesting that the arrest of the militant demonstrates Pakistan's support for "anti-India" terrorist organizations. "Intelligence officials suggest that the capture of a Pakistani terrorist following an attack on a security convoy in Jammu and Kashmir's Udhampur could yield new evidence of repeated terror attacks against India orchestrated from across the border," according to a report on the NDTV website. The Times of India views the arrest as a significant achievement for India, stating, "The captured militant represents the first significant arrest since the 26/11 Mumbai attacker, Ajmal Kasab, was taken into custody." However, an article in the Firstpost website argues that the "second Kasab's" arrest will not aid India's efforts in demonstrating Pakistan-based groups' involvement in terror attacks to the international community. "India may struggle to prove to the world that the 'second Kasab' was dispatched by the Pakistani government, much like it has failed to prove that the state was involved in sending Kasab across the border," the article
The government intends to revamp the funding system in September 2017, making students pursuing degrees in front-line health professions, such as nursing, midwifery, and physiotherapy, responsible for their own tuition fees. This move, according to the Royal College of Nursing and Unison, could deter potential NHS career seekers and exacerbate existing shortages. Currently, these students receive bursaries ranging from £4,500 to £5,500 in London, in addition to an annual grant of £1,000 and fully covered course fees. However, the government proposes to eliminate these bursaries and replace them with university fees, aligning health staff with other students. Ministers argue that this change could lead to an increase in nursing students by approximately 10,000 due to the high demand for course placements. However, unions fear that this could dissuade potential students, potentially causing a 6.5% drop in enrollments from 31,000 to 29,000 students per year. The unions contend that nursing courses are unique because students spend half their time working in the NHS and do not have extended periods for paid work like other students. RCN general secretary Janet Davies expressed concern, stating, "The fees could deter people, particularly older students who enter nursing later in life. They are crucial for workforce diversity. This seems a risky move at a time when we are short of staff." Earlier this month, a report by the Public Accounts Committee highlighted a shortage of about 50,000 front-line NHS staff. Davies has called for a pause in the government's plans, requesting a comprehensive risk assessment to evaluate the potential impact. These changes have sparked protests, with nurses and other staff planning to rally in central London. Unison head of health Christina McAnea expressed worry, stating, "This could make an already difficult situation much worse. With too few staff on the wards, the impact on patient safety could be disastrous." A Department of Health spokesman defended the changes, stating they aim to increase the number of students in training and provide them with greater financial support through loans. He also encouraged the profession to voice their concerns during the consultation process on these changes

Anne Brookes' garden in Leicester frequently hosts a group of up to eight animals as frequent visitors to her lawn. The local football and cricket teams are affectionately known as "The Foxes," and a fox also graces the Leicestershire County Council logo. Mrs. Brookes described the scene as resembling a training session unfolding in her backyard. Mrs. Brookes, a Leicester resident and supporter of the city's football team, stated that the group comprises two brothers, a mother, and five cubs. She shared, "They usually visit our garden most nights, but this time the entire family appeared, which was remarkable. It's not a common occurrence; they usually show up in small numbers. However, to see them all, and with the football match going on, it felt like The Foxes had convened a practice session in our garden, it was fantastic." She also owns a dog that barks at the guests, but "the foxes pay him no mind."
The most recent Markit/CIPS Service Sector Purchasing Managers' Index (PMI) increased from 53.3 to 54.9, signifying growth in the sector. This is the first improvement in four months for the sector's growth rate, though Markit described the pace as "relatively modest." Following the release of these figures, Sterling reached its highest point against the euro since mid-August, at 70.78 pence, and against the dollar, it rose to $1.5446. The survey indicated a five-month high in job growth among services companies, which comprise approximately three-quarters of activity in the UK's private sector. This services PMI followed strong readings from similar surveys in the manufacturing and construction sectors. Markit's chief economist, Chris Williamson, stated, "This improvement, along with the hiring revival signaled by the three surveys, may persuade more policymakers to consider raising interest rates before the end of the year." However, he also noted the absence of significant inflationary pressures in October, suggesting a lack of urgency to increase rates. Most economists anticipate that the Bank of England will not raise rates until the second quarter of next year.

Over £385,000 in fines were handed to drivers in Coppergate. However, the Traffic Penalty Tribunal determined that errors in the signage rendered the restrictions non-enforceable. The council's executive announced that those who received fines would be notified and informed of their right to request a refund. Motorists will have until 31 March 2016 to submit their claims. Council leader Chris Steward stated, "This is primarily about justice and returning money to people when something has not been correct." Fines were issued following the installation of an automatic number plate recognition system on Coppergate in August 2013, which prohibited its use during specific hours. The scheme was discontinued in 2014, along with a trial closure of Lendal Bridge, after both were deemed unenforceable. The council revealed that the revenue from fines had been set aside in the budget in anticipation of potential refunds. It is estimated that the cost of administering the refund process for Coppergate will be approximately £40,000.
An armed drone was flying in close proximity to a de-escalation zone in the southeastern part of the country, situated near the borders of Jordan and Iraq. A spokesperson for the US military announced that this was the first instance of pro-Syrian government forces attacking US-led coalition forces. No casualties among coalition forces have been reported. The incident occurred in the vicinity of the al-Tanf border crossing, which has emerged as a new hotspot in the conflict. US Colonel Ryan Dillon stated that even if the drone was only firing a warning shot, "it demonstrated a hostile intent, a hostile action, and posed a threat to our forces because this drone still carried munitions." It remains unclear which armed faction affiliated with the Syrian government was controlling the drone, according to BBC Arab affairs analyst Sebastian Usher. However, this incident represents another escalation in a region that has become a new flashpoint, potentially leading the US into direct conflict with pro-Assad forces, our correspondent notes. Last month, US-led coalition aircraft bombarded a convoy of pro-Syrian government forces near the Tanf base. Reports suggested that Syrian and Iranian-backed militiamen were advancing towards the base, which serves as a base for rebel fighters and Western special forces personnel.
Discovered at a Cambridgeshire quarry, the "best-preserved Bronze Age dwellings" in the UK date back to approximately 1000-800 BC. These dwellings, which were submerged in silt after collapsing into a river during a fire, are now undergoing excavation. The findings have been hailed as "amazing artefacts." Archaeologists have unearthed two or three circular wooden houses, built on stilts, that were part of a settlement partially destroyed by fire around 3000 years ago. The site, situated at Must Farm quarry near Whittlesey, has been deemed "unique" by David Gibson of Cambridge Archaeological Unit, who is leading the excavation. Unlike most Bronze Age sites, this one has yielded stilts, roof structure, and walls. It is believed the roof caved in during the fire, preserving the contents of the houses as they sank into the Fenland silt. At present, only a small section of one house interior is being examined. One of the initial findings was a "small, delicate wooden box that is mostly intact." Archaeologists expressed their delight at discovering such a well-preserved artefact. The contents remain undisturbed, but analysis will not commence until next week. An intact "fineware" pot and animal bones have also been found, all of which require "meticulous" cleaning and documentation. The "cluster" of fish and animal bones uncovered could represent the kitchen waste of the time. The team is halfway through an eight-month dig to uncover the site's secrets and the lives of its inhabitants. Despite being in the early stages of examining the house interior, the quality and quantity of the findings have left archaeologists "very excited." The site holds the "potential for more uncommon household objects including tools, cutlery, and even furniture." The excavation is being jointly funded by Historic England and quarry owner Forterra. Similarly to Pompeii in ancient Rome, which was preserved by a volcanic eruption in AD 79, these findings offer a unique glimpse into Bronze Age life. Boris Johnson, the mayor, has appealed the case to the Supreme Court, as it was decided that, under the Riot Damage Act of 1886, the police should bear the £60m cost. Insurers argue that all losses resulting from the damage to the Enfield building are covered by this 19th-century law. A final verdict is not anticipated for several months, according to the court. The Sony warehouse was targeted during the riots on August 8, 2011, which occurred following the death of Mark Duggan in Tottenham. Over 1.5 million CDs were destroyed in what lawyers referred to as "the largest ever arson attack in Europe". At a High Court hearing, Mr. Justice Flaux stated that the total losses exceeded £60m, including claims of £9.8m for lost profits and £1.6m for lost rent. In the High Court case, it was ruled that the police should pay for the damage but not "consequential losses" such as loss of profit and rent. However, insurers were successful in a subsequent hearing at the Court of Appeal, which found that the Metropolitan Police should cover all costs. After this ruling, BBC's legal correspondent, Clive Coleman, stated that the decision could impact other businesses that were burned and looted during the riots. Given the two previous hearings, Johnson, whose office finances the police, has requested a Supreme Court ruling.
A British athlete and advocate aims to engage with the Russian Minister of Defense to discuss the establishment of a new marine reserve surrounding the Ross Sea. This minister, Sergey Shoygu, also serves as the president of the Russian Geographical Society. Despite international tensions, Mr. Pugh believes that their dialogue could facilitate a global agreement on the proposed protected zone. The Ross Sea encompasses a portion of Antarctica claimed by New Zealand, from which expeditions led by Scott, Shackleton, and Amundsen embarked on polar explorations. The sea is considered one of the last nearly untouched regions in Earth's oceans, but there is a stalemate regarding its special designation. Speaking about his Russian meeting, Mr. Pugh told the BBC, "This is the first time Antarctica has been discussed at this level. Until now, it hasn't gone this far. They indicated an interest in dialogue and peace. 'When you remove your barriers, there are no obstacles. You can communicate person to person.'" However, Mr. Pugh, the UN Environment Programme Patron of the Oceans, cautioned that the process would be lengthy, particularly given the international disputes over Ukraine. "The relationship between the US, Russia, and the UK is currently very strained. This is the most serious crisis these parties have faced since the Cold War. There are no bilateral meetings taking place at this time." This month, as part of a series of Antarctic swims, Mr. Pugh swam further south than anyone else, breaking his own record. He described the Bay of Whales in the Ross Sea as "the most terrifying place" he had ever swum, with an average sea temperature of -1°C and an air temperature of -37°C. The proposal for the Ross Sea Marine Protected Area (MPA) has been under discussion for the past four years and has failed four times. The most recent proposal was vetoed by Russia and China. The proposed MPA would be as large as the UK, Germany, and France combined and would require the agreement of 24 countries and the EU. Matthew Sargant, aged 22, collided with a lamppost and a tree in Chandlers Ford, Hampshire, in the early hours of September 25th. He admitted to two charges of causing serious harm through reckless driving at Southampton Crown Court. Additionally, he confessed to driving under the influence of alcohol and drugs, as well as possessing cannabis with the intent to sell. The court learned that the incident occurred after Sargant offered a group of four friends a ride home following their night of drinking in Eastleigh. The accident on Templars Way left one of the passengers, a 19-year-old man, in a critical condition and unable to walk. Another 19-year-old man sustained nerve damage to his arm, while Sargant and a third passenger only suffered minor injuries. PC James Fibben stated, "A young man's life is likely to be spent in a wheelchair. It was nothing short of a miracle that no one lost their life that night." Sargant, a resident of High Street, Eastleigh, was disqualified from driving for six years and four months.
Ranveer Singh, a prominent Bollywood actor, visited Bradford to promote his upcoming film, Befikre. The event, held in City Park, marked the first time a major Bollywood star has visited the city since it hosted the Indian Film Awards in 2007. Bradford has a long history of screening Hindi language films, being one of the UK's first cities to do so regularly. Singh, with over 3.6 million followers on Twitter, expressed his visit was due to the large South Asian diaspora residing in the area. "It's been a while since anyone visited Bradford, and usually, events happen in the south," Singh said. "It's a great opportunity to come here because there are many people here who are interested in Hindi films." Accompanied by his co-star Vaani Kapoor, he also unveiled a statue of the pair based on a scene from the movie. Befikre, a romantic crime comedy set in Paris, is set to release on 9th December. Bradford's affinity for film was acknowledged by the UN in 2009, when it was named the first City of Film by Unesco. The love for Bollywood in Bradford, particularly among the South Asian diaspora, began almost 50 years ago when it became a popular venue for screenings for South Asian immigrant communities. Several cinemas opened specifically to show Bollywood movies, providing a source of entertainment for many. My father and his brother were among the first men in Bradford to show these films in cinemas after picking up reels from distributors in London. Sometimes, they even hosted visiting actors like Waheeda Rahman, Vaijayanti Mala, and Helen. My first Bollywood premiere was when I was just 6 months old. The love for Bollywood remains strong among Bradford's South Asian diaspora and has extended to other communities as well.

HP has initiated a lawsuit alleging fraud against it, with Mr. Lynch and the previous Autonomy management team planning to counter-sue HP for over £100 million, accusing HP of making "false and negligent statements." In 2011, HP, a US-based company, acquired software firm Autonomy for $11 billion. Subsequently, HP reduced the value of the acquisition by three quarters. Industry analysts speculated that there may have been issues with due diligence prior to the acquisition. HP aimed to expand its software business through the acquisition, but shortly afterward, HP asserted that it had been deceived about Autonomy's true value. Earlier this year, the Serious Fraud Office concluded its investigation into Autonomy's sale, stating that "with the information available, there is insufficient evidence for a realistic prospect of conviction." It then handed over the case to US authorities. Mr. Lynch and Mr. Hussain have consistently denied any wrongdoing. HP stated in an email that: "HP confirms that, on 30 March, a Claim Form was filed against Michael Lynch and Sushovan Hussain. The lawsuit seeks damages from them amounting to approximately $5.1 billion. HP will not comment further until the proceedings have been served on the defendants." HP filed its claim in London's Chancery Division High Court. Meanwhile, representatives for Mr. Lynch and his associates stated in a separate release: "The former management of Autonomy announces today they will file claims against HP. Former Autonomy CEO Mike Lynch's claim, which is likely to exceed £100 million, will be filed in the UK." Last summer, Autonomy filed documents in a San Francisco court accusing HP of "mismanagement" during the takeover. At the time, Autonomy's former chief financial officer, Sushovan Hussain, claimed that HP intended to "cover up its mismanagement of the Autonomy integration." HP dismissed Mr. Hussain's complaint as "preposterous" at the time.

The United States President, Donald Trump, has made another protectionist trade move, this time targeting the North American Free Trade Agreement for overhaul and imposing tariffs on foreign goods, such as steel. This action follows his withdrawal from the Trans Pacific Partnership agreement in January. The US and South Korea, a significant trade partner, exchanged approximately $144.6 billion in goods and services last year. The 2012 free trade agreement, considered the most significant in 16 years, was expected to bolster American businesses. However, exports of American goods to South Korea have decreased by nearly 3% since the deal's implementation to $42.3 billion, while imports from South Korea have risen by approximately 23%. The US trade deficit for goods and services with Korea was about $17 billion last year, although the US recorded a surplus for services exports separately. In a statement, US Trade Representative Robert Lighthizer expressed the sentiment that "we can and must do better." The administration recently wrote a letter to South Korea, labeling it as an "important ally and key trading partner" but expressing "real concerns about our significant trade imbalance." In an April interview, Trump was more straightforward, calling the deal a "horrible deal" and a "one-way street." The administration has proposed a joint session on the matter to take place within 30 days. Attempts to reach South Korean representatives for comment were unsuccessful.

At Taunton Magistrates' Court, it was revealed that Goddard's actions led to an estimated 45,000 cubic meters of water surging towards the village of Thorney. Goddard asserted that he had intended to redirect floodwaters away from his own property. The prosecution argued that Goddard's actions resulted in a rise in the water levels of Thorney, although they could not definitively link this to property damage within the village. The private prosecution was initiated by the Parrett Internal Drainage Board. By opening the sluice gate, Goddard violated a local by-law and the Land Drainage Act.
