Since 2013, the council has achieved savings of £26.7 million and aims to save an additional £9.5 million in 2017/18. The council will continue its corporate transformation program to create a more modernized and efficient council. Changes in staffing levels are inevitable, but they will be managed through handling vacancies, natural turnover, offering redeployment, and providing early retirement and voluntary severance. On Saturday, the stone situated close to the village of Serre was revealed by the Mayor of Bradford. This stone stands over the trenches where soldiers fought during the battle, and it is a replica of a stone in Bradford's Memorial Gardens. The Pals battalions, formed by friends and colleagues enlisting together, were common, particularly in northern towns. The inscription on the stone reads: "In remembrance of the soldiers from Bradford Pals and other service members of West Yorkshire who served in the Great War (1914-1918). 'And lo, a great army emerged from the North.'" The stone was mined in Bradford and shipped to France. The first Bradford Pals battalion was established at the Bradford Mechanic's Institute in 1914, and a century-old Union Jack that had flown above the institute was utilized during the unveiling. Various local dignitaries, along with representatives of veteran organizations and the Commonwealth War Graves Commission, were in attendance. Councillor Geoff Reid, the current Mayor of Bradford, commented: "We hold a significant debt of gratitude towards these brave men from Bradford who sacrificed their lives during the First World War."

Hughton further mentioned that he believed they would receive a fair decision. He added, "It seems incredibly harsh for a lad who has never been sent off before. There was nothing malicious." The red card was issued by referee Mark Haywood, and Hughton suggested post-match that Danns's reaction to the tackle may have influenced the official's decision. Haywood described the play as dangerous. Hughton acknowledged the referee's perspective, stating, "There is a difference between a player slipping and dangerous or malicious play." The team will be without Murphy for three matches, a player who has been valuable to them. Hughton expressed sympathy, stating, "We are going to be missing a player for three games who has been very good for us. We feel for him."

Rescue teams from Wales and Northern Ireland also participated in the search. At 18:15 BST on Saturday, a report was received that two speedboat drivers had not returned after setting off at 09:00. Police Scotland confirmed that the men had embarked on the speedboat for a leisure trip. Two helicopters and lifeboats conducted a search of the waters overnight on Saturday, following the boat's known and projected movements. Approximately 10 teams were involved in the search, which resumed on Sunday. A coastguard spokeswoman referred to the search as "extensive."

With profound sadness, Can's official Facebook post announced the passing of Jaki this morning, due to sudden pneumonia. He peacefully fell asleep, surrounded by his loved ones. We will deeply miss him. As pioneers of the Krautrock movement, Can combined rock, electronic, and world music. Liebezeit was credited with coining the band's name, which represented "communism, anarchism, nihilism." Throughout various lineup changes, he remained a constant member, contributing to all of their albums, including Tago Mago, Ege Bamyasi, and Future Days. Although they were prolific, releasing up to three albums per year in the 1970s, they became a cult band, influencing acts like Roxy Music, Julian Cope, The Flaming Lips, and The Horrors. They achieved an unexpected UK hit with "I Want More," reaching number 26 in 1976 and securing their sole appearance on Top Of The Pops. Liebezeit, a student of the free jazz scene in Dresden, Germany, co-founded Can in 1968 with keyboardist Irmin Schmidt, bassist Holger Czukay, and guitarist Michael Karoli. Vocalist Malcolm Mooney joined later that year. Beyond his work with Can, he collaborated with numerous other artists, including Depeche Mode, the Eurythmics, Jah Wobble, and Brian Eno. Liebezeit's dynamic drumming style was the metronomic heart of Can's music, providing a precise, hypnotic pulse that anchored their more experimental compositions. His style was later dubbed "motorik," a play on the German word for "motor skills," which evoked the repetitive, relentless experience of driving on a motorway. Liebezeit was scheduled to perform with Thurston Moore, Schmidt, and Mooney for The Can Project show at London's Barbican in April. Wobble, bassist for Public Image Limited, expressed his condolences, stating, "Wonderful person and best European drummer. King of Saxony farewell!!!" Eno paid tribute by posting links to Can's music and interviews featuring Liebezeit. Stay updated by following us on Facebook, Twitter @BBCNewsEnts, or Instagram at bbcnewsents

Leave campaigners, on the other hand, argued that SMEs are more aligned with their cause due to the perceived negative effects of regulations originating from Brussels. Google's new subsidiary, Waymo, will be under the umbrella of Alphabet, its parent company. This move signals a commitment to commercializing the autonomous driving technology Google has been developing for over a decade. At a recent media event, the company shared the story of a blind man who successfully completed a full journey using a prototype self-driving car in 2015. This was first disclosed by Google's John Krafcik on Tuesday, who wrote, "Steve Mahan rode alone in one of our prototype vehicles, navigating Austin’s suburbs. As a legally blind individual, our sensors and software served as his chauffeur." Krafcik, who will now serve as Waymo's CEO, expressed optimism about the potential impact of this technology, stating, "We believe that this technology can start to reshape the approximately ten trillion miles traveled by motor vehicles globally each year, by offering safer, more efficient, and more accessible modes of transportation." The company has also hinted at exploring applications of autonomous driving technology beyond self-driving vehicles. This indicates a growing urgency to find immediate commercial uses for the technology, which, until now, has primarily been used in testing. While companies like Tesla have already introduced elements of self-driving technology onto public roads, Google has taken a more cautious approach. Krafcik suggested potential uses for the technology in personal vehicles, ride-sharing, logistics, and addressing the "last mile" issues in public transportation. There has been no confirmation regarding rumors that Waymo may partner with established automakers instead of developing its own cars, a move that could help reduce costs for Alphabet, which is currently trying to manage expenses in its "other bets" - innovative but non-profitable areas of its business. This collision resulted in life-altering injuries for several individuals, including a woman who now requires a wheelchair due to a spinal cord injury. McGrattan, aged 25 at the time, has admitted to five counts of causing grievous bodily harm by dangerous driving. CCTV footage revealed that McGrattan was driving at an "extremely high" speed of between 52mph and 69mph in the moments leading up to the crash. His car collided with a group of up to 15 pedestrians who were filming for Irish national broadcaster RTÉ on a footpath. At the scene, McGrattan admitted to speeding and told a police officer that he thought he was going to roll over. The crash left some of the victims trapped under McGrattan's car and the fencing it had knocked down. Witnesses described the car failing to navigate a bend due to its excessive speed, and one woman stated that the car drove straight into the pedestrians, resembling a bowling ball upon impact. Another woman compared the car to a snow plow, with people being swept away. The prosecutor informed the court that some of the victims' injuries were so severe that they still required ongoing medical treatment. McGrattan's defense attorney argued that he had not driven since the crash, had been diagnosed with post-traumatic stress disorder, and experienced nightmares, during which he believed he had killed someone. McGrattan expressed deep regret for the incident and struggled to come to terms with its enormity, according to the attorney. However, the attorney also emphasized that McGrattan was not attempting to show off with his driving but had made a "major error in judgment." He added that McGrattan remained at the scene and offered assistance to the injured individuals, and provided a roadside confession to the police. Forensic evidence suggested that McGrattan's speed when he hit the curb was approximately 25mph. The Greens' Jonathan Bartley found Mr. Nuttall's comments distasteful. Describing the atrocity as a "heinous act of terrorism," Mr. Nuttall argued that the timing of the attack, occurring a year after the Brussels airport and underground system suicide bombings that claimed 32 lives, was no mere coincidence. He asserted that the attack was a matter of "when, not if." Mr. Nuttall believes that as long as there is a presence of radical Islam within society, such incidents will continue to occur, and they will happen again unless addressed. He urged the Muslim community to take responsibility, stating that they should identify and report radical individuals to the police promptly. Mr. Nuttall also advocated for the reversal of police force cuts, given the dangerous state of the world. He suggested that security measures may need to be enhanced, as the attack was relatively basic. However, Omer El-Hamdoon, deputy secretary general of the Muslim Council of Britain, called for an end to the pattern of blaming Muslims following every terrorist attack. Motorists are now required to visit the DVLA website to generate a check code, enabling companies to verify if a driver has penalty points. This system was implemented following the computerization of the paper counterpart license that contained such details. Initially, the code was active for only 72 hours; however, the checking period has been extended to 21 days due to complaints from car hire companies about long queues and drivers' concerns about internet access while abroad. For those without internet access, a phone number will also be provided, although it may not be a 24-hour service. The British Vehicle Rental and Leasing Association's CEO, Gerry Keaney, expressed satisfaction with the DVLA's response, stating, "This common sense approach will reduce queues at rental desks and give millions of renters more time to plan and arrive prepared ahead of their journey." Motoring organizations had raised concerns about the new system, as many drivers and car hire companies were unaware of it and found it challenging to use. The DVLA asserts that using the system is straightforward. Oliver Morley, the DVLA's CEO, encourages users to visit gov.uk and search for 'view driving licence' to access and share their driving record easily. Since the abolition of the paper counterpart, all records of driving endorsements and convictions will be stored online only. The approximately 8 million old-style paper licenses, which contain endorsements, will remain valid. The system does not apply to drivers in Northern Ireland.
Fernando Alonso's McLaren team announced that he underwent a comprehensive examination following his recent accident, with the results indicating completely normal findings. The accident was attributed to unexpected gusty winds, and there was no indication of any kind of mechanical failure in his car. The 33-year-old Spanish driver is currently hospitalized for additional observation, and a decision regarding his participation in the upcoming third round of pre-season testing, slated to start later this week, has yet to be made. A photo of a cheerful Alonso in his hospital bed was shared on social media by his manager, accompanied by the message: "Lunch time! Thank you for your support!" Alonso sustained a concussion after colliding with a wall on the exit of a right-hand turn at the Circuit de Catalunya. While cars can reach speeds of 150mph at that point on the track, Ferrari's Sebastian Vettel, who was following Alonso at the time of the crash, estimated his speed to be around 90mph. McLaren provided more details about Alonso's treatment following the crash on Sunday. He was driven from the accident site to the circuit's medical center, where he received initial care and, as per standard procedures, was sedated in preparation for an air-lift to the hospital. In the hospital, a comprehensive analysis of his condition was carried out, including CT scans and MRI scans, all of which came back normal. To ensure his privacy and peace during recovery, Alonso is being kept in the hospital for further observation and to recuperate from the effects of the medication used for his routine sedation. McLaren also shared insights from their detailed analysis of Alonso's damaged car. His car veered off the track at the entry of Turn Three, a fast uphill right-hand bend, onto the Astroturf lining the outside of the track. This loss of traction caused instability, causing the car to swerve back towards the inside of the track, where it struck the wall sideways. The team also addressed the misinformation circulating about Alonso allegedly losing consciousness due to an electrical fault. Their data clearly shows that he was downshifting while applying full brake pressure right up to the moment of the first impact, which would not have been possible had he been unconscious at the time.
In 1980, local authorities denied screenings of the acclaimed comedy "Life of Brian," assigning it an X rating. However, the Bournemouth Colosseum, a 19-seat venue, recently screened the film following a more lenient approach from the authorities. Cinema manager Paul Whitehouse described the historic screening as "a bit of lighthearted fun." As part of the special event, Whitehouse, who operates the cinema beneath a café, provided a three-course meal. It is believed to be the first public showing of "Life of Brian" in the town since the controversy over its certification. "The council's stance has evolved since the early '80s, and what was once considered unsuitable is now seen as acceptable," said Whitehouse. "In the past, they felt it was their duty to dictate what the residents should watch. Back then, people had to travel to Southampton to see it. 'Life of Brian' is a timeless film that has left indelible marks on British culture." Attendee Dave Harvey, a fan of the film, shared his excitement, stating, "It is one of my favorites. The Monty Python films are iconic comedies from the '60s and '70s, and I am thrilled to be part of the first public screening in Bournemouth. I never expected to be making local history tonight." Upon its release, "Life of Brian" attracted accusations of blasphemy due to its story about a man born next to Jesus Christ, who is mistaken for the Messiah. The Bournemouth council's environmental services committee initially gave the film an X rating, restricting viewings to those over 18. The distributors stipulated that it could only be shown in areas with an AA rating. Train driver Adrian Cox recently requested a change in the council's stance to host a private screening for his birthday. The screening was only approved after licensing officers agreed. Colin Robinson awarded the film a 9.5 out of 10, commenting, "I can't fathom why such a humorous film was banned. Watching it again feels like a trip down memory lane. The Monty Python squad brought a unique, carefree spirit to life."

Eurostat's preliminary estimates indicate a slight deceleration in the growth of food, alcohol, and tobacco prices compared to November. Similarly, the decrease in energy prices was less pronounced. The rate of price increase was lower than the anticipated 0.3% rise, leading to potential pressure on the European Central Bank to take further action to stimulate the underperforming European economy. Last month, the central bank's efforts to revitalize the economy fell short of market expectations. The inflation figures, though, only represent a flash estimate and do not provide a breakdown by member states. However, they do offer a broad overview of which product categories have experienced price increases or decreases. Specifically, food, alcohol, and tobacco prices are estimated to have risen by 1.2% year-on-year in December, as opposed to 1.5% in November. Energy prices, on the other hand, were declining at an annual rate of 5.9%, a decrease from 7.3% in the previous month. The price of services, meanwhile, increased by 1.1%, compared to 1.2% in the previous month.

SpaceX's Falcon 9 rocket initiated its engines, preparing for takeoff from the Cape Canaveral launch pad. However, onboard computers abruptly triggered an abort, marking the third attempt in five days to launch a satellite for SES, a Luxembourg-based operator. A fourth attempt may occur later this week, but engineers need time to analyze data and determine the cause of the abort, and the US Air Force requires preparation time for the Florida range. Elon Musk, SpaceX CEO, tweeted about the aborted launch, attributing it to a low thrust alarm caused by rising oxygen temperatures due to a boat and helium bubble. Earlier in the evening, the countdown had to be paused and reset due to a vessel moving into the restricted zone beneath the rocket's flight path. Following last year's Falcon 9 failure, SpaceX resumed operations in December with a successful multiple satellite deployment for Orbcomm. That mission also saw the historic first landing and recovery of an orbiting rocket stage, as the Falcon's lower section returned to Cape Canaveral for a controlled touchdown. In January, SpaceX launched the Jason ocean altimeter mission for US and European space and meteorological agencies. The company is currently using an upgraded, higher thrust version of its Falcon 9, achieved in part by using super-chilled liquid oxygen (LOX) as one of the propellants. However, recent countdowns have revealed additional handling challenges related to loading the liquid into the rocket and maintaining the correct temperature. Walter Newall's architectural works from the 19th century continue to stand prominently throughout Dumfries and Galloway. Photographer Margaret Elliot will be delivering a talk on Newall's life and times at Moat Brae, a structure designed by Newall in 1823, located in Dumfries. Moat Brae is believed to have influenced the creation of Peter Pan author JM Barrie, who resided in the town. Current renovations are underway at Moat Brae to transform it into a national center for children's literature. Ms. Elliot has initiated a project aimed at cataloging all the existing buildings in the region associated with Walter Newall's involvement. Born on April 3, 1780, at Doubledyke, New Abbey, Newall was a farmer's son who initially worked as a cabinet-maker before his business faltered, leading him to practice architecture. Although there is no evidence of formal training, his buildings reflect an understanding of contemporary ideas in the Greek Revival and Picturesque Gothic styles. Newall undertook a diverse array of projects, including churches, country houses, villas, and farmsteads, many of which were on Buccleuch estates. He was also instrumental in the development of George Street - the New Town of Dumfries - and the conversion of a windmill in Maxwelltown into an observatory, which became a notable landmark in the town. Unmarried, Newall passed away on Christmas Day 1863 at Craigend, New Abbey, and is buried in St Michael's Churchyard, Dumfries. Ms. Elliot expressed, "I have lived in Dumfries my entire life, and only now do I realize that few people appreciate the beauty in these buildings as they enter and exit them, including myself until now. This project has opened my eyes, and I now recognize a Walter Newall style. The history that comes with the buildings makes them even more intriguing, as are the people I have met who share their love for these buildings and grant me access to them." Her talk will take place at Moat Brae, Dumfries on Thursday, August 25, at 18:30.

Bionic legs, or micro-processor limbs, will be made available to wounded service members who have served in Iraq or Afghanistan. These advanced prosthetics will offer them enhanced stability, mobility, and a higher quality of life. Approximately 160 service members are expected to benefit from this initiative. The Help for Heroes charity has hailed this move as one that will "transform lives." Defense Secretary Philip Hammond emphasized the importance of providing the best possible care and support to troops, stating, "Our troops are heroes who have and continue to give absolutely everything for their country, and it is only right that we do everything possible to help them, especially when they suffer injury." Chancellor George Osborne, who is providing the funding from the Treasury's special reserve, added, "I am delighted that we have been able to make funding available for this cutting-edge prosthetic technology, which will go a long way to improving the lives of people who have done so much for the UK." Experts agree that these bionic legs, similar to those used by Paralympics discus thrower and former soldier Derek Derenalagi, will significantly improve the quality of life and speed of rehabilitation for amputees. The new technology offers better stability, greater mobility, and improved ability to step over obstacles, negotiate stairs, and walk backwards safely. The limbs will be fitted at the Headley Court military rehabilitation center in Surrey where they are deemed clinically appropriate. Paralympic rower Captain Nick Beighton, who lost both legs in an explosion during a foot patrol in Afghanistan in 2009, welcomed the micro processor limbs as a "big step up in technology." He expressed his excitement, saying, "To have the opportunity to try it and to use it and get the latest technology is fabulous for us because it just gives us that freedom to get out and do more things and have greater functionality and more independence." Surgeon General Air Marshal Paul Evans praised the next generation of micro processor knee, stating, "It will improve the quality of life and rehabilitation for our patients, where it is clinically suitable. Not only does it provide better stability and improved mobility but will also help reduce back pain and aid rehabilitation generally." At Mangrove Service Station on A45 London Road in Rugby, Warwickshire, three individuals needed immediate medical attention. These individuals were transported to the hospital, while the rest were taken into police custody, according to authorities. The Border Force has been notified of the discovery. The vehicle involved in this incident was described as an "overseas lorry" by Warwickshire Police. The A45 Westbound was closed between the M45 and the A4071 during the operation, but later reopened. A haulage lorry, which appeared to be foreign and potentially from Romania, was parked near the garage. Approximately 100 square boxes were seen on the sidewalk, and a team of about 10 individuals, including those in suits, police uniforms, and what seemed to be forensic investigators, were going through the boxes meticulously before placing them back onto the lorry. The three men, who appeared to be of Middle Eastern descent and showed signs of heat exhaustion, were treated at the scene by the West Midlands Ambulance Service before being transferred to University Hospital Coventry and Warwickshire. Lorry driver Will Fry reported seeing these individuals wrapped in blankets and silver foil at the service station. He stated that he was preparing to enter the garage when he witnessed a large number of police cars with flashing blue lights, ambulances, and a multitude of police personnel. The scene included a continental fridge lorry with its cargo scattered across the floor, and the ambulance crew seemed to be attending to Middle Eastern men, most of whom were bearded and wrapped in silver foil and blankets, with some not appearing to be in good health. Tunde Kosa, a hotel worker at the Travelodge Rugby Dunchurch Hotel near the service station, reported finding several police cars outside the hotel upon her arrival for work, but she assured that the incident did not affect the hotel.
The International Olympic Committee (IOC) has reported that a total of 55 stored samples from the Games, specifically, have tested positive. Of these, Russia acknowledges that 22 samples belong to their athletes, yet two were subsequently cleared when B sample tests came back negative. As stated by Mutko to Russia's Tass news agency, all samples that have been laboratory-tested should be declared invalid, and the accreditation of any laboratory found to have falsely reported a positive test result should be revoked. The IOC has been re-examining samples from the 2008 Beijing and 2012 London Games at the World Anti-Doping Agency (WADA)-accredited laboratory in Lausanne, Switzerland. The focus of these tests is on samples from athletes aiming to compete at the upcoming Olympics in Rio de Janeiro. Russian athletes have been barred from international competition by the IAAF since November, with the IOC scheduled to decide whether Russia will be permitted to participate in Rio 2016 at a meeting on June 17. The case centers around allegations of abuse of authority. A spokesperson linked this investigation to a criminal probe in France concerning the alleged extortion of athletes by former IAAF president Lamine Diack and other officials.

The proposed reform does not abolish the tax status, but individuals who have resided in the UK for 15 out of the last 20 years will forfeit the right to claim it. Known as the non-domicile rule, this regulation allows some high-income UK residents to minimize taxes on earnings earned outside the country. The chancellor stated that adjustments to the non-dom rules would generate an additional £1.5 billion in annual tax revenue. From April 2017, individuals born in the UK to UK-domiciled parents will no longer be eligible to claim non-dom status if they depart from the UK, then return and establish residency in the UK. Additionally, starting from April 2017, new regulations will be implemented to prevent individuals from evading inheritance tax on UK residential property by holding it in an offshore company. This measure aims to curb alleged abuses of the rule by some non-domiciled individuals. "It is unfair that non-domiciles with UK residential property can put it in an offshore company to avoid inheritance tax. From now on, they will pay the same tax as everyone else," said Mr. Osborne. The chancellor opted against eliminating the non-dom tax status entirely, citing potential financial losses. He emphasized that many individuals who benefit from the tax status make significant contributions to public life. However, he also acknowledged, "It is not fair that people live in this country for extended periods, utilize our public services, and yet enjoy tax rules different from those of everyone else." Separately, the chancellor announced an additional £750 million would be allocated to HM Revenue & Customs to combat tax evasion. This funding boost is expected to help HMRC officials raise an additional £7.2 billion in tax revenue by 2020. For comprehensive coverage of the July 2015 Budget, visit the BBC's online resources, including Budget Live and documents from HM Treasury. (BBC Budget Live, HM Treasury link to Budget documents)

Three ATF agents testified they were instructed to monitor the transportation of firearms, but not to make any arrests. The revelation has sparked outrage among US lawmakers regarding Operation Fast and Furious. This news follows a report suggesting that Mexican drug cartels have acquired US-sourced weapons, with approximately 70% of firearms recovered from Mexican crime scenes in 2009 and 2010 and submitted for tracing originating from the US. On Wednesday, congressional lawmakers concluded that Fast and Furious, a program intended to trace small-time gun buyers to major weapons traffickers along America's south-west border, did not lead to the apprehension of any major traffickers. The weapons monitored by ATF agents were reportedly involved in numerous murders in Mexico. Members of the House of Representatives Oversight Committee demanded answers from the Obama administration as to why no arrests were made during the investigation. ATF agent John Dodson told the committee that he wanted to seize these weapons but was ordered not to make any arrests. During a previous hearing, Senator Chuck Grassley of Iowa stated that hundreds of weapons intended for Mexican cartels were purchased in Arizona gun shops. Operation Fast and Furious was designed to track weaponry as it moved from small-time gun buyers to major traffickers, who have often evaded prosecution. In December, two US assault rifles were discovered at the site of a shootout where Customs and Border Protection agent Brian Terry was killed. At a hearing on Wednesday, Robert Heyer, the deceased agent's cousin, requested that if a government official made an error, they admit it and take responsibility for their actions. In January, the Mexican government released statistics indicating that at least 34,612 people had died in drug-related violence in Mexico since December 2006, although some suspect the figure may now exceed 40,000.

This year, proposals to introduce a non-native species for the first time were disclosed. A public consultation elicited opinions from wildlife experts, some of whom expressed concern about a potential "catastrophic outcome," while others supported the idea. The government asserted there were no "conservation reasons" to move forward. Following the consideration of all perspectives, Environment Minister Richard Ronan declared it would be "harmful," stating, "We have chosen not to advance with this at the moment." Over a hundred individuals participated in the consultation. A representative from the Manx Wildlife Trust warned of a "catastrophic impact on habitats already struggling with environmental stressors." Wildlife experts argue the red squirrel is not on the brink of extinction in the UK and see no scientific basis for establishing a sanctuary for it. Grey squirrels, originally from North America, were introduced to the UK in the 19th Century and have since posed a threat to their red counterparts. This is mainly due to competition for habitats and the transmission of the lethal parapoxvirus. Although red squirrels are not considered endangered, the Red Squirrel Survival Trust in the UK claims they could vanish within a decade without conservation efforts.
Jeremy Corbyn, the Labour leader, has stated that he would permit a second referendum on Scotland's constitutional future if both the Scottish Parliament and the Scottish people express a desire for it. However, he emphasized the necessity of waiting until the UK concludes negotiations with the EU before proceeding with such a vote. Nicola Sturgeon, on the other hand, has suggested that a vote should take place once the final terms of the Brexit deal are known. Corbyn clarified that the right to a referendum exists, as per the devolution agreement of the 1990s, but urged postponement until after Brexit negotiations are finalized due to their significance. Previously, Sturgeon had called for a second vote on Scotland's constitutional future between autumn 2018 and spring 2019. During the launch of the SNP's general election manifesto in Perth, Sturgeon argued that opposing an independence referendum would be democratically unsustainable if the SNP wins a majority of Scottish seats. The manifesto includes a call for a vote on independence to be held "at the end of the Brexit process". However, Theresa May has rejected this proposal, maintaining that "now is not the time". During a visit to a nursery in Westminster, Corbyn urged the SNP to reconsider and appealed to the Scottish people to do the same. He stressed the importance of completing the Brexit negotiations first to secure tariff-free trade access to the European market, as the manufacturing industry heavily relies on it. He also emphasized the need for good agreements with Europe regarding the management of the sea and other conditions gained from European membership. When asked if he would block a referendum if Holyrood passed a motion for it before Brexit is complete, Corbyn replied that discussions would ensue, and he would strongly urge them to delay the vote until after the Brexit talks have been concluded. He reasoned that, in Scotland, the focus should be on issues such as poverty, investment, and housing needs of the people. A Labour government, he asserted, would greatly assist the people of Scotland in these areas. The Scottish Liberal Democrats have labeled Corbyn's stance on independence as "confused and chaotic".
"Bob Dylan, a seasoned musician, voiced his opinions to Rolling Stone magazine, stating, 'Critics often whine about such matters.' He emphasized that in folk and jazz music, borrowing and reinterpretation are long-standing traditions, 'except for me, it seems.' Dylan's latest studio album, Tempest, was just released. This year, at the age of 71, he received the Medal of Freedom, the highest civilian honor in the United States, from President Obama. Previous accusations of plagiarism have been levied against him, such as borrowing from Henry Timrod, a 19th-century poet who passed away in 1867, on his 2006 album Modern Times. Another allegation involved his 2001 album Love and Theft, which was said to contain passages similar to lines from Confessions of a Yakuza, a memoir by Japanese gangster Junichi Saga. When asked about these claims, Dylan responded, 'As for Henry Timrod, have you even read him? Who's been reading him lately? And who's brought him to the forefront?' He challenged, 'If you believe quoting him can enhance your work, try it yourself and see how far you can go.' He also referenced the controversy surrounding his 1965 album Bringing It All Back Home, saying, 'They tried to label me Judas, the most reviled name in history.' He continued, 'If you've been called a bad name, try to overcome it. And for what? For playing an electric guitar?' Born Robert Allen Zimmerman in 1941, Dylan started his musical journey in 1959, performing in coffee houses in Minnesota. He adopted his stage name from the poet Dylan Thomas. Most of his renowned work stems from the 1960s, a period during which he served as an unofficial historian of America's struggles with songs like Blowin' In The Wind and The Times They Are A-Changin'. Dylan's decision to transition from traditional guitar to an electric one in the mid-1960s was met with opposition from hardcore folk fans. 'It's about songwriting,' he told Rolling Stone. 'It involves melody and rhythm, and after that, anything goes. You make it your own.'"
By 2050, crop yields are projected to increase by 40-60% according to an online tool created by the government for examining potential carbon emission reduction strategies. The Global Calculator, which has been scrutinized by international experts, explores various scenarios to meet the 2C target, a critical threshold for preventing severe climate change, as per scientists. The model, overseen by the Department of Energy and Climate Change (Decc), presents a world where living standards are preserved, yet only through extensive modifications in agriculture, transport, food, and fuel. The number of electric vehicles on the road is anticipated to reach hundreds of millions by 2050, and the carbon dioxide emission per unit of electricity must decrease by at least 90%. Consumers may also need to consider adopting diets rich in vegetables or consuming meat from animals raised under intensive farming practices. Ed Davey, the Energy and Climate Change Secretary, stated, "For the first time, this Global Calculator demonstrates that global prosperity is achievable while limiting temperature rises to 2C, thereby averting the most devastating effects of climate change. However, it is crucial that we act immediately to transform our energy and land usage if we are to achieve this sustainable growth." Dr Mike Cherrett of Climate-KIC, the EU climate initiative that co-led the project, added, "The calculator underscores that we can attain our 2C target while maintaining desirable lifestyles, but we must establish ambitious goals across all sectors and employ innovation to combat climate change." The Global Calculator is an expansion of Decc's UK calculator, published in 2010, and is being made available to other governments for use prior to the significant climate negotiations in Paris at year's end.
The real hourly wage for individuals aged 22 to 39 decreased by 11% from its peak, while workers in their 50s experienced a 5% drop and those in their 60s a 2% decrease, according to the Resolution Foundation. This organization, which aims to enhance living standards for the 15 million people in Britain with low to middle incomes, was founded in 2005. Stephen Clarke, an economic analyst at the foundation, stated, "The pay squeeze reemerged at the start of 2017 and seems likely to persist for the remainder of the year at least." He added that the recovery of wages from the previous squeeze has yet to occur, as this latest round of declining pay has set in. However, the Resolution Foundation reported that the implementation of the National Living Wage has had a positive impact since its introduction two years ago, leading to a 12% increase in wages over this period. In response to the report, a spokesperson for HM Treasury said, "More people are employed than ever before, and we are helping working individuals keep more of their earnings by reducing taxes for millions. The National Living Wage has provided a £1,400 annual pay increase for those on the lowest wages, and we are helping people's money go further by freezing fuel duty and doubling the amount of free childcare." Source: Office for National Statistics.
A 31-year-old English boxer, known as 'the Hammer' for his fervor for West Ham United, has announced his retirement from the sport. He has won 39 out of 43 fights in his career. Initially scheduled to face Finland's Edis Tatli for the European lightweight title on the 18th of March, he decided to hang up his gloves due to a lack of motivation for another grueling training regimen. With a boxing career spanning 22 years, he felt his body had endured enough rigorous training to prepare for a fight. His last fight was on the 12th of December last year, where he was stopped by Ismael Barroso in a bid for the vacant WBA interim lightweight title. The bout against Tatli was planned for the European lightweight belt.

The High Court in Chennai (Madras) dismissed multiple petitions seeking the prosecution of author Perumal Murugan. The judges commented, "Allow the author to return to his finest skill: Writing." Murugan halted his writing last year due to protests over his novel, Madhorubagan (One Part Woman). Supporters view this ruling as a triumph for free speech. Murugan has yet to respond. The court also declared that a "settlement" between Murugan and a peace council in Tamil Nadu, in which he agreed to apologize for the novel and withdraw all unsold copies, was not legally binding. Set in a century past near his hometown of Tiruchengode in southern India, Madhorubagan depicts a childless woman participating in a sex ritual during a temple festival to conceive, a scenario Murugan claims is based on historical fact. Local groups led protests against the book, alleging that the fictional extramarital sex ritual at the novel's core insulted the town, its temple, and its women. Copies of the book were burned, shops were closed, and a petition demanded the author's arrest. This led Murugan to pen a dramatic Facebook post stating, "Perumal Murugan, the writer is deceased. As he is not a deity, he will not resurrect himself. He does not believe in reincarnation. As an ordinary teacher, he will live as P Murugan. Leave him alone." He also instructed his publishers not to print or sell his work and promised to compensate them for the unsold copies. He urged his readers to burn his books and announced he would no longer attend literary festivals. Murugan is often praised by critics as one of the most accomplished writers in the Tamil language.

Seven finalists have been selected from the initial pool of fifteen contenders for the sixth edition of the event. The shortlist for the £30,000 award was unveiled at 50 Albermarle Street in London, the historical residence of John Murray, the original publisher of Scott's works. The identity of the winner will be revealed in the Borders this summer. The following seven novels have made it to the final shortlist: Upon announcing the shortlist, the chair of judges, Alistair Moffat, stated, "We received a record-breaking number of entries, resulting in our most extensive longlist and our longest shortlist since the inception of the Walter Scott Prize in 2010. This list of seven remarkable novels showcases the breadth and diversity of styles that the historical fiction genre now embraces - from the lyrical to the avant-garde, and from satire to adventure, literature set in the past can provoke, captivate, and inspire in countless ways. Our 2015 shortlist could have easily been extended, but we aim to showcase the vitality of historical writing published in the past year, as well as a wide array of global settings, from colonial India to 14th Century China, Europe during the two world wars, and 17th Century England." The winner of the prize will receive £25,000, and each of the shortlisted authors will be awarded £1000, bringing the total prize value for 2015 to £31,000.
Habit changes such as starting smoking earlier and smoking more cigarettes have been linked to a significant rise in the risk of lung cancer. These trends, reported in the New England Journal of Medicine, indicate that death rates in women have now equaled those in men. The study analyzed data from over two million women in the U.S., with the first generation of female smokers beginning in the 1950s and 60s. In these early years, women who smoked were almost three times more likely to die from lung cancer than those who had never smoked. A review of medical records from women between 2000-2010 showed they were 25 times more likely to die from lung cancer than their non-smoking counterparts. This pattern is similar for men, who reached a comparable level in the 1980s. The lead researcher, Dr. Michael Thun, stated: "The sharp increase in risk among female smokers has persisted for decades after the serious health risks from smoking were widely acknowledged, and despite the fact that women predominantly smoked cigarette brands marketed as having lower 'tar' and nicotine. "Thus, not only did the use of cigarette brands marketed as 'Light' and 'Mild' fail to prevent a significant increase in risk in women, it may have worsened the increase in deaths from chronic obstructive lung disease in male smokers, since the diluted smoke from these cigarettes is inhaled more deeply into the lungs of smokers to maintain the usual absorption of nicotine." Research published last year suggested that lifelong female smokers die a decade earlier than those who never started. However, women who quit smoking by the age of 30 almost entirely avoided the risks of early death from tobacco-related diseases, while those who quit by 40 died a year younger. Speaking after that study, Prof. Sir Richard Peto, at Oxford University, said "If women smoke like men, they die like men."

On Tuesday morning, traffic was halted northbound on the bridge so that a lorry could be safely turned around. The bridge had been closed to all traffic for nearly three weeks, reopening on December 23rd, but heavy goods vehicles will face a significant detour until at least mid-February. A representative from Police Scotland stated, "At approximately 10:40 AM on January 5th, an articulated lorry attempted to cross the Forth Road Bridge despite the ongoing restrictions. Officers temporarily halted northbound traffic to allow the lorry to be safely turned around. The driver was subsequently charged with operating a prohibited vehicle on a restricted road. We appreciate the public's patience and cooperation during this time." The bridge was closed to all traffic on December 4th after a crack was discovered in a truss beneath the carriageway. Engineers have carried out a temporary repair that allows lighter vehicles to use the bridge. Transport Minister Derek Mackay expressed hope that the crossing could reopen to all vehicles in mid-February, provided the weather remains favorable and no additional defects are detected.

MB Aerospace has acquired Centrax Turbine Components, a company based in Newton Abbot. Despite the takeover, the Gas Turbines division remains under the family-owned Centrax Group. The Devon-based firm, with a workforce of 430, has been providing engine components for approximately 70 years. It has established a reputation for its expertise in rotating components within the compressor and turbine sections of aero-engines. The company will continue to produce components that require automated machining, grinding, and robotic polishing. Centrax caters to Rolls-Royce, Snecma, and Solar engine-makers, who in turn supply Airbus, Boeing, and Embraer manufacturers. MB Aerospace, headquartered in Motherwell, North Lanarkshire, now operates plants in Connecticut, Michigan, Poland, and the UK, in addition to its existing facilities. In 2013, a majority stake in the Scottish firm was acquired by Blackstone private equity. The Centrax deal marks the first significant acquisition since the company received additional funding from this source. Robert Barr, Centrax's CEO, stated, "In MB Aerospace, we have found a guardian who shares our values, commitment to the region, and the substantial pool of high-tech manufacturing expertise we have developed over time."
Business owners have reportedly been billed repeatedly for unwanted advertisements by Wyvern Media (WM). One business owner claimed she had to close her business after being charged over £5,000, while another had over £500 in bills following adverts worth only £40. WM asserts that it never engages in unauthorized payments. Jean Wood, a dog breeder from Lincolnshire, agreed to pay for two adverts in WM's publications but was subsequently charged for adverts in other publications. She stated, "I had two invoices for the two I'd paid for, for £40. That was fine, but then I started receiving invoices marked as 'paid' for charges I hadn't asked for. I had six unauthorized payments totaling over £500." Polly Zabari, owner of a bespoke travel business, claims she was forced to shut down her company due to charges exceeding £5,000. She initially agreed to a single advertisement, but after receiving numerous phone calls and emails from various WM group companies, she discovered she had been charged over £5,000 for advertisements she had not authorized. She never received a published copy or receipt for these orders. Mrs. Zabari's bank has since reimbursed her following a successful complaint. According to WM's website, it operates 28 newspaper titles, with offices nationwide, including Coventry, Leamington Spa, and Rugby. A former employee, who chose to remain anonymous, confirmed receiving complaints from dissatisfied advertisers while selling adverts for one newspaper for several months. "I started receiving calls from customers who I knew we had sold to, claiming that certain payments had been taken out of their bank accounts without authorization," she said. "We all kept quiet, as we were afraid of losing our jobs if we spoke up." In response to these allegations, WM managing director Jonathan Rivers, based in Coleshill, West Midlands, stated that the company "has never been" in the practice of taking unauthorized payments from customers. He added that all sales calls are now recorded, and complaints have dropped to three or four a month from several thousand sales. Mr. Rivers also mentioned that if customers believe they have been charged without proper authorization, they can seek a refund through their credit cards.

The crash left Thomas with a serious brain injury, while his two passengers, a girl and a woman, sustained injuries - the girl fractured her back, and the woman suffered a head injury. The analysis of pollen discovered on pottery buried with a woman approximately 4,100 years ago, found in Achavanich, Caithness, has identified a variety of plants used for medicinal purposes. Known as "Ava," this woman was the subject of a long-term research project managed by archaeologist Maya Hoole. Ms. Hoole expressed that the presence of the pollen "raises intriguing questions." Last year, forensic artist Hew Morrison created a facial reconstruction of Ava. Recently, the results of other research have been published. The pollen analysis of a decorated beaker buried with Ava identified several plants and trees, including birch, pine (most likely Scots pine), hazel, alder, heather, grasses, meadowsweet, and St John's wort. Ms. Hoole noted, "The majority of the pollen recovered were from trees and shrubs such as birch, pine, hazel, and alder. Heather, grasses, meadowsweet, and St John's wort were also identified." The presence of meadowsweet is particularly interesting as it has also been found at other Bronze Age burials across Scotland. Ms. Hoole added, "The inclusion of both meadowsweet and St John's wort may suggest a deliberate choice of flowers in the burial. Interestingly, both of these plants are also known for their medicinal properties." She further questioned, "The presence of several different species of plants considered to have medicinal properties raises intriguing questions: was this intentional, and was it in any way related to the cause of this individual's death?" The archaeologist also mentioned that Ava's remains, along with other artifacts found with her, are now in the care of the Caithness Horizons museum in Thurso. Unusual findings include Ava being buried in a pit dug into solid rock, and her skull having an abnormal shape, which some suggest was the result of deliberate binding. It is believed Ava belonged to a wider European group known as the Beaker people. Further research is planned to shed more light on Ava and her burial.

The Russian foreign ministry expressed "indignation" over the US Department of State's annual report, which classified Russia, China, Uzbekistan, and others in its lowest tier, alongside Iran and North Korea. A Chinese foreign ministry spokeswoman, Hua Chunying, expressed concern over the report's "unilateral or arbitrary judgments" of China and called for objectivity and impartiality. The US report criticized Russia for making "ad hoc" efforts to combat trafficking, lacking a systematic approach for identifying and caring for victims. China, on the other hand, was praised for raising awareness and collaborating with international organizations, but was still criticized for ongoing issues of forced labor, sex trafficking, and a pronounced problem with trafficking among its internal migrant population. The report also highlighted the impact of China's one-child policy, which has resulted in a gender imbalance, fueling demand for prostitution and foreign brides. Russia contested the report's methodology, stating that it groups countries based on their friendliness with Washington, and asserted that it would never follow foreign instructions in combating trafficking. In response, China emphasized its commitment to fighting all forms of trafficking and urged the US to take a more objective view of its efforts.

During an interview with ABC News, President Obama acknowledged that some hacking incidents were state-sponsored by China, but he downplayed the notion of a cyber war between the two nations. His remarks came a day after US intelligence officials stated that cyber attacks have surpassed terrorism as the primary security concern. China denies these allegations and claims to be a victim of such attacks itself. When asked about accusations from US lawmakers that the scale of cyber attacks on American businesses and infrastructure amounts to a "cyber war" with China, Obama responded, "There's a significant difference between them engaging in cyber espionage or cyber attacks and a hot war. What is undeniable is that we have witnessed a steady escalation of cyber security threats. Some are state-sponsored, while others are merely the work of criminal elements." The president added that the US has made it clear to China, as well as other state actors, that they are expected to adhere to international norms and rules. He further stated that there would be some tough conversations with them, as there already have been. China has been under suspicion for cyber attacks, leading to Pentagon warnings about the potential for a "digital Pearl Harbor." A US congressional report from last year labeled China as the most significant threat in cyberspace. The issue has become a contentious point between Washington and Beijing. On Tuesday, US Director of National Intelligence James Clapper testified before a Senate committee that cyber attacks and cyber espionage have replaced terrorism as the leading security threat for the country. A day earlier, US National Security Advisor Tom Donilon called on China to take action against cyber crime. Last month, a report from US security firm Mandiant claimed that a unit of China's People's Liberation Army had conducted data raids on the computer systems of more than 140 mostly US-based organizations. US state department officials have stated that hacking is discussed "in virtually every meeting we have with Chinese officials." In January, The New York Times reported that it had been targeted by cyber attacks from China following its report on the wealth of outgoing Premier Wen Jiabao's family.
The organization announced that it detected "restricted, focused assaults" and urged individuals to promptly update their software. Flash, a common tool for delivering multimedia content online, has been identified as having a vulnerability. This flaw allows hackers to gain control over a computer and it affects systems running Windows, Mac, and Linux. Users can verify if their Flash installation is up-to-date by visiting the Adobe website; the current latest version is 18.0.0.194. Individuals using the latest versions of Google Chrome and Microsoft Internet Explorer may find that Flash is updated automatically. Adobe's Flash software has a history of requiring security updates and is often viewed as a weak spot on many websites by security researchers. Alongside Java, Flash is frequently targeted by hackers using zero-day exploits, a term for unrecognized security gaps. This targeting is partly due to Flash's widespread use, according to Mark James, a security expert from ESET. "Given Flash's extensive use as a plugin, it's logical that it would be one of the most targeted applications for vulnerabilities," he stated. "If you aim to impact a large number of people, then you need an application that many users employ, and Flash is one of them." Security blogger Brian Krebs has disabled Flash entirely on his device as an experiment, suggesting on his blog that it might be worth reconsidering whether it's necessary to keep Flash Player installed at all.

The East Lancashire Hospitals NHS Trust and Lancashire Care NHS Foundation Trust have reported improvements in patient safety. Mrs. Williams was admitted to the hospital late on January 26, having been found unconscious and intoxicated near a canal in Burnley. The inquest revealed that hospital staff were aware of her suicidal tendencies, but Mr. Singleton emphasized that this information was not communicated clearly enough, and the cubicle door should have remained open. The Blackburn Coroner noted that not only was Mrs. Williams suicidal, but she was placed in an environment where she could carry out her intentions - a room with potential hanging points and ligatures. Mr. Singleton is preparing a Regulation 28 report, which allows coroners to highlight concerns to prevent future deaths, but he has already been encouraged by the way both hospital trusts have already addressed some issues. During an unscheduled visit to HMP Bristol in Horfield earlier this year, significant issues were uncovered. Nick Hardwick, the chief inspector of prisons, expressed concern over a resurgence of aimlessness within the prison following a favorable report in 2010. The National Offender Management Service acknowledged these concerns and pledged support to help improve the prison's performance. In their 2010 inspection, Her Majesty's Inspectorate of Prisons had commended the prison for its progress and described it as a well-managed facility with a clear vision. However, they noted that the advancements made were not sustained. The most recent inspection revealed that staff at the category-B prison, capable of housing up to 600 inmates, were perceived as dismissive towards prisoners. The report detailed instances of unprofessional conduct, such as the use of derogatory and abusive language, among staff members. Furthermore, fewer prisoners reported being treated with respect compared to similar institutions. The cells were found to be dirty, with damaged or missing furniture, offensive graffiti, and an infestation of cockroaches. The report also highlighted a shortage of clean clothes and adequate bedding for inmates. Inspectors also witnessed a prisoner being subjected to arbitrary punishment when a prison officer prevented him from consuming his full meals. The prison has since taken disciplinary action against the staff involved. Mr. Hardwick described the overall prisoner experience as poor and outlined key areas for improvement, including enhancing the prison environment, modifying the staff culture, and ensuring prisoners have access to meaningful activities that will prepare them for life after release. Positive aspects noted by the inspectors included the prison's security measures being proportionate and its ability to meet resettlement challenges. Michael Spurr of the National Offender Management Service explained that at the time of the inspection, the prison had been undergoing a significant period of restructuring and change. He also acknowledged the high turnover of prisoners as a challenging factor for the governor and her staff. "The prison and its staff will receive the support necessary to help improve performance and create a safe and constructive environment for the prisoners it houses," Mr. Spurr added. The governor of HMP Bristol, Andrea Albutt, was not available for comment.

Lewis Coaches, a Ceredigion-based company with 30 vehicles, is set to halt operations on Friday, potentially impacting 40 jobs. This marks the third bus company collapse in Wales within the past two months, following the administration of Wrexham-based GHA Coaches in July and the ceasing of Pembrokeshire firm Silcox Coaches in June after 134 years in business. The 701 commercial bus service from Aberystwyth to Carmarthen and Cardiff will no longer be in operation. Plaid Cymru's Mid and West AM, Simon Thomas, has written to the Welsh Government seeking assurances about the bus route, emphasizing the crucial role it plays in connecting communities and facilitating travel for workers, students, and tourists in the west. The company expressed its sadness over the closure on its website, expressing gratitude to its customers for their long-term support and hoping the transition won't cause inconvenience to anyone.

The tragedy occurred during a weekend visit, following the family's separation. The boys' mother, Claire Throssell, expressed her sentiments, stating, "The children's voices need to be heard." Over 40,000 people have endorsed a petition, backed by Women's Aid, demanding that the government and family courts take measures to prevent any further avoidable child deaths due to unsafe contact with a domestic abuse perpetrator. Throssell had previously alerted a judge about her ex-husband's potential to "either kill or significantly harm the boys." She described him as being on the brink, having attempted suicide a few months prior to the incident and being in an unstable state at the time of the visit. Throssell emphasized the importance of prioritizing children's voices in decision-making processes. Jack, tragically, never had an opportunity to voice his concerns until the fire, at which point he told the firefighters, "My dad did this, and he did it on purpose." The inquest into the boys' deaths revealed that they were enticed to their father's home with a new model train set, and that Sykes, who had recently parted ways with Throssell, intentionally set fire to the property. Both boys succumbed to their injuries in the fire, with Paul dying instantly, and Jack passing away six days later in the hospital. The coroner determined that both boys were unlawfully killed, and ruled a suicide in the case of their father.

Over the past five years in the United Kingdom, 272 organ donors, who had a history of cancer, contributed to transplants. These donations resulted in 675 recipients receiving a transplant. Notably, eye donation is a significant area where such donors have been able to make a difference. The data from NHS Blood and Transplant further reveals that 1,033 people who had experienced various types of cancer donated their eyes - though not other organs. Officials acknowledge a common misconception that cancer patients cannot be organ donors, but there are certain circumstances where it is possible. The Advisory Committee on the Safety of Blood, Tissues and Organs has emphasized that the risks of cancer transmission must be weighed against the risks of dying without a transplant. They assert that organs from deceased donors with some cancers may be safely used for transplantation. The risk of donor-transmitted cancer in the UK is currently estimated at 0.06%. John Forsythe, the associate medical director for organ donation and transplantation at NHS Blood and Transplant, encourages individuals not to let health conditions or past illnesses deter them from registering as donors. He stresses that they strive to minimize risks to recipients by thoroughly evaluating all potential organ and tissue donors. Approximately 70 cornea donations per week are required to meet the demand for sight-saving transplants. However, one in ten people on the NHS Organ Donor Register declines to donate their eyes. In 2010, Alison Cooney, an aspiring midwife, passed away at the age of 28, six weeks after being diagnosed with bowel and liver cancer. Despite the fact that her major organs could not be donated due to the aggressive nature of her illness, her corneas were donated and helped restore the sight of two people. "Apparently not many people donate their eyes," Ann Cooney, Alison's mother, noted, "but it is one of the most successful procedures. Even though it was initially difficult to accept what was about to happen, we had to focus on something good being achieved from something bad."
Shawn Tyson was convicted for the murder of friends James Cooper, aged 25, and James Kouzaris, aged 24, in Sarasota, Florida, on the 16th of April 2011. The appeal court confirmed his guilt but overturned his sentence due to his age at the time of the crime, as he was 16 years old. The case was sent back to a lower court for a fresh sentencing hearing. In his ruling, Associate Senior Judge James R. Case affirmed Tyson's guilty verdict "without discussion". However, given that Tyson was a juvenile homicide offender, his sentence needed to be reassessed, the judge stated. This was in line with a Supreme Court decision that mandatory life sentences without parole for juveniles violated a constitutional ban on "cruel and unusual" punishment. Mr. Cooper, from Hampton Lucy, Warwickshire, and Mr. Kouzaris, from Northampton, were fatally shot when they accidentally entered the Newtown area while intoxicated. Tyson, who was tried as an adult, was found guilty of first-degree murder in March 2012.

Chinese authorities accused GSK staff of bribing government and hospital officials in China earlier this month. The company declined to comment on whether the SFO's criminal investigation is linked to any specific incident. In a statement, GSK reaffirmed its commitment to upholding the highest ethical standards. GSK is currently under investigation in Poland and Iraq for similar allegations. If proven, these allegations could suggest a violation of both the UK Bribery Act and the US Foreign Corrupt Practices Act. It is illegal for companies based in the US or UK to bribe foreign government employees. The SFO has confirmed the opening of a criminal investigation into GSK's commercial practices and its subsidiaries. The agency also stated that whistleblowers are valuable sources of information and welcomes approaches from anyone with inside information on all its cases, including this one. In April, BBC Panorama received information from Jarek Wisniewiski, a former sales rep for GSK in Poland, who claimed that doctors had been paid to promote the company's asthma drug Seretide. Eleven doctors in Poland and a GSK regional manager were charged over alleged corruption between 2010 and 2012, according to BBC Panorama. Last July, Chinese authorities announced they were investigating GSK, detaining four Chinese GSK executives. GSK has previously confirmed that it informed the SFO, as well as the US Department of Justice and the US Securities and Exchange Commission, about the Chinese investigation.

Approximately 800 law enforcement officers have been educated to administer on-the-spot penalties, aiming to prevent first-time offenders from entering the criminal justice system. Restorative justice, a practice implemented in the county since early 2011, has so far been utilized on 190 offenders. This approach is invoked only if the crime's victim consents to its application. The alternative to conventional arrest and punishment procedures is grounded in three fundamental principles. Inspector Dominic Human stated, "Admit that your actions were wrong, make amends to rectify the situation, and re-enter society. This offers a one-time chance not to become a criminal. Often, a victim simply seeks an apology, and restorative justice provides that opportunity." Trained officers encourage victims of minor offenses to suggest suitable penalties, which are then evaluated for fairness. Inspector Human emphasized, "We do not act as judge and jury in any way. We merely provide a platform for an individual to immediately acknowledge their wrongdoing and express a desire to right their wrong." As part of the restorative justice initiative, youth offenders who damaged a house's windows agreed to clean all windows. A 16-year-old shoplifter, who stole goods valued at £3, was tasked with cleaning 100 shopping baskets, while a 12-year-old, who caused £75 worth of damage to a car, was required to send a written apology, with parents covering the cost of repairs. Inspector Human noted that the system significantly reduces "an immense amount of time" compared to arrest and punishment via the court system. "We can achieve the same results, in a very short time, at the crime scene. The goal behind introducing restorative justice is to prioritize the victim's needs. To date, we have saved over 1,000 operational policing hours, which we can then allocate to address other critical community issues." Cambridgeshire Constabulary plans to educate all officers in the application of restorative justice methods in the future.

The bank is burdened with bad loans and is considered the most vulnerable major EU bank. The Italian Prime Minister, Paolo Gentiloni, whose government is newly formed, faces pressure as private investors would bear any losses under EU bailout regulations. He characterized the move as a "precautionary measure," stating, "We believe it is our duty to take this measure to safeguard savings. Over a four-year period from 2010 to 2012, approximately 400 complete skeletons were unearthed during construction at St John's College. These remains, dating from the 13th to 15th centuries, were burials from the Hospital of St John the Evangelist. A comprehensive £1.2 million study aims to shed light on the health of urban impoverished communities during the epidemic. Approximately 1,000 body parts were also discovered, making it one of the largest medieval hospital burial grounds found in Britain. The majority of those buried at the site were ordinary townsfolk, not clerics, and included both men and women, as well as some children. The epidemic claimed millions of lives across Europe. Researchers from various disciplines, such as archaeology, history, and genetics, will collaborate to "directly test the effects of the Black Death on a medieval town population before, during, and after the epidemic." The Hospital of St John the Evangelist was an Augustinian charitable institution, offering care to the public from around 1200 to 1500. The research is supported by the Wellcome Trust and St John's.

In a preliminary study, 261 adolescent girls from three secondary schools participated in a six-lesson course on body image. This intervention significantly improved their body image and self-esteem compared to the standard curriculum, according to psychologists at King's College London. Approximately one-third of teenagers express dissatisfaction with their body, and this poor body image has been linked to depression, eating disorders, excessive use of cosmetic surgery, obesity, and unhealthy weight loss practices. A six-part program on positive body image for teenage girls was developed by Dr. Helen Sharpe and her team at the Institute of Psychiatry. The program addressed ideals of beauty, unhealthy peer interactions like "fat talking" or making derogatory comments about weight, and strategies to enhance mood and self-esteem. The program was delivered by teachers in three publicly funded girls' schools. Dr. Sharpe informed the BBC, "We have determined whether a series of lessons delivered by teachers to students in school are beneficial for improving body esteem, and we found this to be the case." The research, published in The British Journal of Psychiatry, suggests potential for addressing issues stemming from low body esteem, such as eating disorders. Dr. Sharpe expressed optimism, "As we continue with this research, we hope to make the program even more effective and, in turn, potentially reduce disordered eating behaviors like binge eating and harmful weight loss." Interventions delivered by teachers could reach a large number of students at a low cost, the researchers noted. They cautioned that some schools were relying on unproven programs that could be detrimental. Last year, MPs recommended that all schoolchildren participate in mandatory body image and self-esteem lessons. An inquiry by the All-Party Parliamentary Group on Body Image revealed that more than half of the public have a negative body image. Girls as young as five now express concerns about their appearance, the MPs' report stated, while cosmetic surgery rates have risen by nearly 20% since 2008.
Skiplagged, a website, discovers affordable flights by identifying journeys with stopovers at a traveler's desired destination city. Two corporations, United and Orbitz, have accused the site of "unfair competition" and aim to recover lost revenues. The site's creator, Aktarer Zaman, asserts that he is simply exposing the inefficiencies in airline ticketing. The legal dispute has been initiated in Illinois. Skiplagged operates by scanning extended flights that feature a stop in a significant city as part of a route to another destination. For instance, a flight from New York to Lake Tahoe with a stopover in San Francisco could offer a cheaper fare if a traveler intended to visit San Francisco rather than Tahoe. In certain instances, the site suggests, passengers can save up to 40% on ticket prices by employing this strategy. However, this trick only applies to one-way flights, as checked luggage would then proceed to the flight's final destination. Twenty-two-year-old Zaman, who developed the site, informed CNNMoney that Skiplagged has not generated any profits for him. He declined to provide specific comments on the case to CNN. Zaman has launched a fundraising campaign to accumulate funds for the legal battle against United and Orbitz. To date, he has raised $10,538 (Â£6,776) of the $15,000 goal. In their legal documentation, United and Orbitz claim that the site is "intentionally and maliciously" disrupting their business and causing breaches of contracts with partners. The documents further state that "logistical and public safety concerns" prohibit the use of "hidden city" tickets, and as a result, using Skiplagged violates these regulations. The two corporations are seeking damages of at least $75,000 in alleged lost revenue due to Skiplagged's operations.
The Langstone Memorial signifies the culmination of a four-year endeavor, initiated by a proposal to unearth the histories of six World War II servicemen. Investigations conducted by the Langstone Council unearthed details of an additional 10 men from the earlier conflict. On Saturday, the Right Reverend Richard Pain, the Bishop of Monmouth, dedicated the memorial, erected in May. The project commenced when two members of the Langstone Council sought to delve deeper into the lives of six men whose names appeared on a World War II memorial plaque in the village hall. Edward Jeffery, the chairman of the community council, not only unearthed the backstories of the original six, but also discovered an additional 10 men from the villages of Langstone, Llandevaud, Llanmartin, and the surrounding areas who perished during World War I. The memorial, constructed from granite to symbolize the area's quarrying history, features a time capsule filled by students from Langstone Primary School with items reflecting their lives, as well as a QR code that allows visitors to learn about the men whose names are inscribed on it.
The recent discovery of the Xi-cc++ particle, announced at a high-energy physics conference in Venice, provides new insights into the strong force that binds atomic centers. Previously, the existence of this particle was only theoretically predicted, but now it has been identified for the first time. The study was conducted at the LHCb experiment, led by Dr. Patrick Spradlin of Glasgow University, who stated that the discovery would "provide clarity on a longstanding puzzle and initiate an exciting new avenue of exploration." Dr. Paul Soler, also from Glasgow University, described the development as "a new frontier in understanding the strong force." The majority of visible matter consists of neutrons and protons, which are composed of three smaller particles called quarks. Quarks can be either light or heavy, and there are six different types. Previously detected particles contain at most one heavy quark. However, this is the first time researchers have confirmed the existence of a particle with two heavy quarks. Professor Guy Wilkinson of Oxford University notes an intriguing difference between the new particle and those previously discovered. "In contrast to other particles of this type, where the three quarks perform an elaborate dance around each other, a particle with two heavy quarks is expected to behave like a planetary system, with the heavy quarks acting as two stars orbiting each other, and the lighter quark orbiting around this binary system." The research team plans to measure the properties of the Xi-cc++ to understand how the new arrangement of quarks behaves and how the strong force holds the system together. They also anticipate finding more double heavy quark particles. Additionally, the particle has two positive charges, double that of the proton, and is four times heavier. A paper reporting these findings has been submitted to the journal Physical Review Letters. Follow Pallab on Twitter.

He stated that he pulled Laurene-Danielle Jackson, 19, from a crowded stairway, where she and Nabila Nanfuka, 22, were trapped and later found unconscious. Both young women died due to asphyxia. Oliveira, 25, testified in the Northampton County Court hearing that he attempted CPR on Jackson, but she had no pulse. No criminal charges were brought following the tragic deaths. Oliveira explained that a scuffle erupted around 3:15 GMT as people rushed to exit the nightclub after repeated announcements that coaches were leaving. He described the ensuing chaos as a "domino effect," with the altercation triggering a frenzied response from the crowd. Oliveira remembered carrying a semi-unconscious man back inside, then picking up another unconscious woman and carrying her outside. Nabila Nanfuka, from Neasden in north London, who was studying leisure and tourism at Northampton University, died at Northampton General Hospital on October 19. Olabanji Gellatly-Olanrewaju, a friend of Nanfuka, recalled seeing five people fall on top of her. He described how she attempted to push people off herself, but ultimately became motionless. He explained that the pressure was immense, and he struggled to slow down the crowd. Fitzgerald described his attempts to communicate with the crowd, but ultimately ended up being pinned against the wall. CCTV footage from inside the club was shown to the jury, and the inquest continues.
The 62-year-old man, who was under investigation for homicide through negligence following a fatal crash in Catalonia, has been acquitted due to insufficient evidence. The accident, which occurred on March 20 near Freginals, approximately 150km south of Barcelona, claimed the lives of 13 individuals, seven of whom were Italian. One of the victim's parents, Alessandro Saracino, expressed his shock upon hearing the news. In an interview with Italian newspaper La Repubblica, Mr. Saracino suggested that the families might consider lodging an appeal. Initially, Catalan police had suggested that a black box on board the vehicle indicated several sudden changes in speed before the crash. However, the court in Amposta ruled that there was no evidence of speeding and no mechanical fault. Furthermore, the court determined that the driver was not under the influence of drugs or alcohol. The majority of the passengers in the vehicle were exchange students, returning from a fireworks festival in Valencia. The victims ranged in age from 19 to 25, with seven being Italian, two German, one each from Romania, Austria, France, and Uzbekistan. The court's decision is open to appeal.
