Over the course of the next three years, resources will be distributed to approximately 70,000 non-profit organizations and 900 university research projects. In essence, cloud computing refers to the practice of storing data online rather than on local computers. This not only makes data more accessible but also offers non-profits the opportunity to access immense computing power that would typically be beyond their financial reach, save for the largest corporations. In a blog post, Microsoft's Chief Legal Officer, Brad Smith, explained the initiative: "Cloud services can unlock the secrets hidden within data, leading to new insights and breakthroughs, not just in science and technology, but also in addressing a wide range of economic and social challenges and the delivery of improved human services." The analysis of large datasets, often referred to as "big data," is seen as a significant opportunity for non-profits dealing with complex social issues that require significant processing power that cloud computing can provide. Microsoft's commitment isn't about providing a physical product or cash but rather access to servers and services that normally businesses would have to pay substantial fees for. Additionally, the funds will be used to improve "last mile" internet connectivity, with the aim of bringing countries with limited internet access closer to the advantages enjoyed by more developed nations, such as home broadband. Other companies, like Facebook, have similar goals. Facebook's Internet.org project is investing in connectivity technologies, such as drones, to bridge the "last mile," enabling what founder Mark Zuckerberg calls the "next billion" people to access the web. However, initiatives like these are not always met with positive reactions. Facebook's Free Basics scheme, which allowed certain mobile sites to be accessed for free, sparked controversy in India, with local businesses alleging Facebook was giving itself an unfair advantage over local competitors. Microsoft may face similar criticism - that the three-year donation is intended to secure the long-term integration of the recipients within the Microsoft cloud ecosystem. Despite this, Microsoft's CEO, Satya Nadella, has received widespread praise for his consistent desire to use Microsoft's resources to benefit developing countries, including his native India. Recently, he was a guest at Michelle Obama's State of the Union address, and this week, he is attending the World Economic Forum in Davos, emphasizing Microsoft's potential to provide computing power for initiatives beyond big business. The UK has issued a tender for a contract to educate Whitehall staff on trade policy matters. The aim is to provide these individuals with a comprehensive understanding of the UK's trade priorities, as well as those of key international partners, and to familiarize them with relevant terminology. This initiative arises from concerns about a potential skills gap in trade negotiations, given the UK's upcoming independence from the EU. In his recent resignation letter, the former UK ambassador to the EU, Sir Ivan Rogers, expressed concerns about a shortage of multilateral negotiating experience within the UK. For many years, the EU has managed trade negotiations on the UK's behalf, with a significant number of UK specialists in the field based in Brussels. The Foreign Office and the Department for International Trade are now searching for a supplier to deliver short-term training in international trade policy and negotiations to civil servants across various Whitehall departments. The successful bidder will be tasked with imparting a thorough understanding of the key aspects and terminology of international trade policy, as well as the trading priorities of the UK and its key partners. The goal is to prepare officials to support and implement the UK's international trade policy priorities and negotiation agenda, both domestically and abroad. The tender process is being overseen by KPMG, which manages the civil service's core curriculum on behalf of the Cabinet Office. The UK cannot initiate any trade deals until it has left the EU, and at present, the government is limited to exploring potential future opportunities. However, ministers have suggested that there is a long line of countries eager to sign bilateral agreements, including Australia, New Zealand, and Canada. US President Donald Trump has expressed his desire for an early trade agreement with the UK following Brexit, and a discussion on the matter is expected during his meeting with Theresa May this Friday. The Treasury has provided additional funding to the Department for International Trade and the Foreign Office to enhance the UK's trade promotion capabilities. Two months ago, a senior official stated that the civil service was committed to empowering existing staff for their new roles but may also consider recruiting talent from outside Whitehall. At the time of the June 2016 referendum, the UK had 45 trade policy experts; this number has since increased by 100.
In February 1957, 11-year-old Moira Anderson ventured out from her grandmother's house in Coatbridge to run errands, but she never returned. The main suspect in her disappearance and subsequent murder is Alexander Gartshore, a bus driver who was also a convicted paedophile and passed away in 2006. Recently, the police have been investigating an area near the Monkland Canal in hopes of locating her remains. On the day she vanished, 23 February 1957, Moira boarded a Baxter's bus driven by Gartshore during a heavy snowstorm. Later that year, he was imprisoned for raping a 17-year-old babysitter. In 1999, another convicted child abuser, James Gallogley, implicated Gartshore as Moira's murderer. Gartshore's own daughter, Sandra Brown, was certain of his guilt and advocated for him to be charged. In 2014, prosecutors made an unusual statement, asserting that if Gartshore were still alive, he would have been charged with the schoolgirl's murder. In 1957, a witness reported seeing a tall man carrying a large, heavy sack towards the canal the morning after Moira's disappearance, but this possible sighting was not pursued further. Four years ago, a grave in Old Monkland Cemetery in Coatbridge was exhumed as part of the search, but no evidence was found suggesting that Moira was buried there.
Charlie Naysmith discovered a 600g lump of ambergris in a patch of seaweed at Hengistbury Head, Bournemouth. Initially, he believed it to be a common stone, but upon research with his father, Alex, they concluded it was likely a rare form of sperm whale vomit. Ambergris, a substance found within the sperm whale's innards and later expelled, is highly sought after for its rarity and can float on the ocean for years before washing ashore. Mr. Naysmith, a resident of Christchurch, estimated the lump found by his son to be approximately 20 years old and suggested it could be worth up to £40,000. He stated, "It's very rare. You don't hear of it coming around very often and it's usually found in small amounts. In olden days, they used it in perfumes and some medicines. I'm not sure if it's necessarily worth that much these days, but hopefully it will be." Charlie has already planned to use the potential earnings to create a nature reserve for animals in his country. Ambergris, often referred to as "floating gold" by scientists and treasure hunters, has been mythologized for thousands of years. Thought to aid digestion, it is expelled from the sperm whale's abdomen while far from land. Hard beaks of giant squid, a primary food source for sperm whales, have been found within ambergris lumps. Initially, ambergris is a soft, foul-smelling matter that floats on the ocean. Through exposure to sunlight and saltwater over time, it transforms into a smooth, waxy lump with a sweet scent. While still used in perfumes, many perfume manufacturers now opt for synthetic versions due to the association with whaling and the vulnerability of the sperm whale species.
Edinburgh Airport experienced the most significant growth, followed by Aberdeen and Glasgow airports. In February, Edinburgh Airport served 607,723 passengers, marking a 7.8% increase compared to the same month the previous year. The increase in international passengers was 11.6%, with 269,235 people traveling, due to the addition of new easyJet routes and a second daily Lufthansa service to Frankfurt. Domestic travelers increased by 4.9%, with 338,488 individuals passing through Edinburgh Airport last month. The airport attributes the growth to an increase in passengers traveling to Heathrow with Virgin Atlantic Little Red and to Gatwick with British Airways. Gordon Dewar, the airport's CEO, stated, "February was another strong month for us, and we're pleased to see a healthy rise in our passenger figures." Aberdeen Airport reported a 6.5% increase in passenger numbers, with 255,416 people passing through last month. The airport saw growth in both fixed-wing and helicopter operations. Glasgow Airport saw a 6% increase in passenger numbers, with 448,465 people passing through last month. Len Allchurch, the younger brother of the legendary Wales international Ivor Allchurch, played over 600 League matches in a career spanning from 1950 to 1971. His impressive career included helping Sheffield United gain promotion to the First Division during the 1960-61 season. Allchurch was part of the Wales squad for the 1958 World Cup, although he didn't get to play a game. Cliff Jones, a former Swansea and Wales teammate, paid tribute to Allchurch, stating, "We grew up together and played our football together." Jones and Allchurch were both part of the same Swansea schoolboy team for a couple of seasons and later worked together in the docks. They were closely related in both their professional and sporting lives at Swansea Town. Jones described Allchurch as a very skilled right winger with great control, a wonderful temperament, and great company. The 1950s were a golden era for football in Swansea, with some of the finest talent in Welsh football history emerging from what was then known as Swansea Town. Alongside the Allchurch brothers, John and Mel Charles, and Spurs legends Cliff Jones and Terry Medwin all came through the club's ranks. John Charles was the only one not to play for his hometown team. Len Allchurch played 276 League games for Swansea Town before joining Sheffield United for £18,000 in 1961. He scored five goals in the last seven games of the season, helping John Harris' side gain promotion to the First Division. He left Sheffield in 1965 to join Stockport County, helping them win the Fourth Division title in 1966-67. In 1969, he returned to his home town club, which by then had become Swansea City. He made a further 71 League appearances for the Swans before retiring in 1971. Although in the squad for the 1958 World Cup in Sweden, Len did not get to play alongside his brother as Wales reached the quarter final before being knocked out by Brazil, with Pele scoring the goal. However, he did play in the 2-0 win
The 23-year-old 2012 Olympic silver medallist, Fred Evans, will not be participating in this year's games following investigations by the Home Office and Games officials. The specific reasons for his absence remain unclear, but Welsh officials have held extensive discussions with all relevant parties for several weeks. In April, Evans was ordered to pay a total of £1,228 in fines and costs due to his involvement in a nightclub assault in Birmingham. On the same day, British 100m champion Dwain Chambers, a member of England's 4x100m relay team, withdrew from the games to focus on the European Championships in August. Three-time world squash champion Nick Matthew will lead England's team at the opening ceremony in Glasgow on Wednesday, while rhythmic gymnast Frankie Jones will represent Wales. Evans' absence is a significant setback for Wales, who have already lost triathletes Helen Jenkins and Non Stanford, and cyclist Becky James due to injuries. Additionally, 800m runner Gareth Warburton was expelled for violating anti-doping rules. On Monday, weightlifter Faye Pittman and judo player Kyle Davies withdrew due to injuries. Evans, currently ranked number one in the world in his weight division, was considered one of Team Wales' top medal prospects. James Thomas, the Welsh boxing performance director, stated, "We were aware of his conviction earlier this year and we certainly do not condone such behavior, but we hoped Fred would continue boxing and improve his lifestyle through the sport." Wales team boss Brian Davies, who recently announced he was considering revising the team's medal target of 27 due to several high-profile withdrawals, expressed disappointment at the outcome. "Everything possible has been done to get Fred to the games, and we are bitterly disappointed," said Davies. "Fred is a world-class boxer and has handled this difficult situation with great discipline. We thank him for his professionalism and wish him all the best for his future aspirations."

The ATR 42-500 aircraft, initially procured to combat Afghanistan's illicit drug trade, ended up costing a tenth of its intended budget, with additional millions spent on upgrades. Despite this, the plane, as reported by the US Justice Department's Inspector General, remains idle in a storage facility in Delaware. The report suggests that the aircraft is unlikely to ever operate in Afghanistan, as the program for which it was acquired concluded in 2015. The plane was intended for an anti-drug initiative jointly led by the DEA and the Pentagon, but the costs of modifying and housing the aircraft skyrocketed, with $67.9m from the US Department of Defense being spent, more than four times the initial estimate. Aviation officials with the DEA are criticized in the report for failing to consider the time and expense required to establish a support infrastructure, including pilots, mechanics, trainers, and spare parts, before purchasing the ATR 500. The plane, purchased over seven years ago, remains inoperable, resting on jacks, and has never flown in Afghanistan. A DEA official told investigators that the plane, once prepared, would be deployed in the Caribbean and Latin America. However, the report notes that this was not the intended purpose of the funding. These revelations follow closely on the heels of accusations by a US government watchdog that a Pentagon agency squandered millions on misguided reconstruction projects in Afghanistan. Special Inspector General for Afghan Reconstruction, John Sopko, pointed out a program that spent $6m importing rare, blond Italian goats to boost the Afghan cashmere industry. He expressed concern over the ineffectiveness of oversight, stating that he could not verify whether the goats were not consumed. Last year, Sopko revealed that $43m was spent on constructing a vehicle refueling station in Afghanistan, 140 times the cost of an equivalent station in Pakistan. He suggested that fraud and corruption may have inflated the cost.
The Healthcare Environment Inspectorate (HEI) has issued a request after conducting an inspection of Western Isles Hospital in Stornoway, Lewis, in September. The inspectors noted a lack of records regarding the temperature at which breast milk was being stored and an absence of a staff policy for handling and storing it. However, the HEI stated that the hospital was otherwise clean and adhering to standards. The inspectors made only one requirement in relation to breast milk and no further recommendations. Jacqui Macrae, head of quality of care for HEI, commented: "This was a positive inspection, during which we found the ward environment and all patient equipment we inspected to be clean. We also observed good staff compliance with standard infection control precautions." NHS Western Isles chairman Dr Neil Galbraith said: "The board closely monitors the maintenance of cleanliness within our hospitals, given the strong link between cleanliness and patient safety. The board is pleased with the Inspection findings, which largely align with the Western Isles Hospital's own self-assessment report and offer external confirmation that our cleanliness and anti-infection measures are effective." Dr Galbraith further added: "The board acknowledges the single requirement arising from the inspection and will promptly produce the policy paper and implement the related practice."

In February, a film was recorded on land leased for grouse shooting in the Derbyshire Peak District. The conservation group Raptor Persecution, who published the footage on Tuesday, expressed concern and called for action. Derbyshire Police examined the footage but chose not to take further action. The National Trust, however, is taking the incident seriously and has launched an investigation. The footage was captured by birdwatchers with a digiscope about a kilometer away and was released following police investigation. A spokesperson for Raptor Persecution stated that while no illegal activity occurred, it seemed a decoy bird was made to resemble a male hen harrier, potentially to attract another hen harrier. The spokesperson explained that male hen harriers are territorial and may attack perceived intruders. While the bird was distracted, it was speculated that a gamekeeper was positioning himself to shoot from a hidden spot nearby. The RSPB expressed worry that the footage may have depicted an illegal attempt to target hen harriers or other birds of prey, as decoy hawks and owls can be used for the lawful control of crows. The Peak District National Park's chief executive, Sarah Fowler, described the video as "alarming and suspicious" and voiced support for the investigation. The Moorland Association denounces all wildlife crimes but maintains no crime was committed in this instance, adding that making assumptions about the clip's content or the intentions of its creators would be unwarranted. Derbyshire's Police and Crime Commissioner, Alan Charles, commented that a recent sighting of a man with a gun and a plastic model of a hen harrier bird on a Peak District grouse moor underscores the need to protect wildlife. Although the man was captured on camera by two observers, they were too far away to obtain high-quality evidence for a prosecution. According to the RSPB, hen harriers are the most intensively persecuted birds of prey in the UK, and their predation of grouse is a contentious issue on shooting moors. In 2015, five male hen harriers disappeared from sites across England, leading to the collapse of nests. The fate of these birds remains unclear.
In August 2014 and April 2015, Bronze Age artifacts were unearthed in Wenvoe, Vale of Glamorgan. Simultaneously, in Llanarmon-yn-Iâl, Denbighshire, an early medieval artifact, potentially part of a gilt bronze or silver brooch, was discovered. Additionally, an early medieval silver ingot was declared treasure in Trelawnyd and Gwaenysgor, Flintshire. A Bronze Age axe and spearheads, thought to have been buried during a ritual ceremony around 3,500 years ago, were found by Gwyn Rees. National Museum Wales has expressed an interest in purchasing these items. The brooch, discovered by Karina Kucharski, features a gold baseplate adorned with a spherical blue glass bead set within a gold collar. Dr. Mark Redknap of National Museum Wales commented, "Early medieval goldwork of this period is scarcely found in Wales. This new discovery substantially enhances our understanding of early medieval jewelry, its circulation, and the goldworking technologies employed during this time." The silver ingot was discovered by Francis Hodge, and Flintshire museums are eager to acquire it.

At conferences held by the Royal College of Nursing and the British Medical Association, concerns have been raised about the dire state of the National Health Service (NHS) in England. Delegates at both events detailed how the system's pressures have escalated to a point where patient safety is being compromised. However, ministers in England have refuted these claims, asserting that increased investment is making the NHS the most secure healthcare system globally. The conferences coincided this year, with both opening with grim warnings of mounting pressures. The RCN presented a report demonstrating that frail elderly patients are being moved at night and treated in corridors and storage areas. Meanwhile, doctors at their Belfast conference expressed concern about the NHS being critically short of beds and operating at full capacity. They cited OECD data showing that countries such as Belgium, France, Austria, Germany, and even Romania have more than double the number of beds per capita than the UK. In England, the number of hospital beds has decreased by more than a fifth over the past decade, leaving hospitals overcrowded according to BMA leader Dr. Mark Porter. He stated that the situation increases the risk of infections and decreases the likelihood of patients being placed on the correct ward. Dr. Michael Hardingham, an ear, nose, and throat surgeon from Cheltenham, went a step further, stating that patients are being harmed due to a lack of available beds. This view was echoed by the majority of doctors who passed a motion stating that the reduction in bed stock has gone too far. Nurses at the RCN conference in Glasgow shared examples of how the pressure on hospitals is forcing some sites to take drastic measures. These included instances where hospitals are being forced to take extreme measures due to the pressure. RCN general secretary Janet Davies described the NHS as being in a state of "endless winter," causing chaos. Emergency care nurse Janet Youd from West Yorkshire added that she could not guarantee the safety of care at the next emergency department a patient might need to visit. Despite these concerns, a spokeswoman for the Department of Health maintained that the NHS is performing well under the additional pressures it is facing. She reiterated that patient safety remains a top priority and that the goal is to make the NHS the safest and most transparent healthcare system in the

Helicopter traffic decreased by 13.1%, while fixed wing traffic experienced a 7.2% drop. Airport managing director Carol Benzie stated that all three sectors - helicopters, international, and domestic - experienced a decline in passenger traffic. The European Commission has announced a new defense plan, aiming to enhance spending on assets such as drones and helicopters, while supporting innovative technologies. The objective is to reverse a decade-long trend of defense spending cuts by EU members, equating to nearly 12% in real terms. This initiative comes amidst threats from US President-elect Donald Trump to reduce the US security commitment to NATO, and his comments during the election campaign that he would not defend NATO allies unless they contributed fairly to the alliance. There are concerns among some EU and NATO members regarding potential Russian aggression, Trump's perceived closeness to Moscow, and the uncertainty brought about by Brexit. The UK, traditionally a barrier to Franco-German initiatives aimed at giving the EU a greater security role, is leaving the EU. European Commission President Jean-Claude Juncker emphasized the need for increased investment and cooperation among member states. He stated, "To ensure our collective security, we must invest in the common development of technologies and equipment of strategic importance - from land, air, sea, and space capabilities to cyber security." Juncker further added, "If Europe does not take care of its own security, nobody else will do it for us." The new defense fund's "research window" will be expanded from the €25m initially proposed for the 2017 budget to €90m in 2020, covering areas such as robotics and encrypted software. Simultaneously, a "capability window" aims to mobilize €5bn annually from member states' funds for joint purchases of equipment like drones and helicopters, with the Commission acting as a sort of treasury to manage these procurement processes. Despite a decrease in defense spending, military cooperation among EU countries has not significantly deepened, according to the Commission's statement. It is estimated that this lack of cooperation costs the bloc between €25bn and €100bn annually. The BBC's Europe editor Katya Adler notes that while the EU has its own rapid reaction forces, there is little evidence of the unity among the 28 nations necessary to create a European defense union. The European Council, comprising the heads of state and government of the EU, is expected to discuss the defense plan later this month.

Five-time Grand Slam champion, aged 28, disclosed on Monday that she had tested positive for meldonium in January. "I awoke yesterday to a flood of love and compassion in my inbox," penned the Russian. "Your heartfelt messages brought a smile to my face." Sharapova, who may face a ban of up to four years, expressed her determination to return to tennis. "I wish I didn't have to endure this, but I must," she wrote. "I have been mostly offline, save for occasional searches for a vintage coffee table, but my friends compiled a collage of your beautiful messages and hashtags for me. I spent the afternoon perusing them, accompanied by my dog, who seemed puzzled as to why this was more important than the walk he was expecting." Meldonium, a drug initially developed to treat diabetes and heart-related conditions, is known to potentially boost stamina and endurance due to its ability to enhance oxygen flow to muscles. It was included in the World Anti-Doping Agency's (WADA) list of prohibited substances on January 1, 2016. Sharapova, who claims to have been using meldonium for health reasons for the past ten years, has seen some sponsorship deals either terminated or delayed. The International Tennis Federation announced that her provisional suspension will commence on March 12.
A pigeon was observed by staff at a car wash on Lower Hollin Bank Street, Blackburn, during Wednesday morning. It remains uncertain whether the bird had accidentally come into contact with paint or was deliberately painted. According to an RSPCA representative, "I've never encountered anything like this in 15 years of service." Insp Nina Small further commented, "Only his eyes were unmarred." The pigeon was found in a car wash, possibly attempting to clean itself. Despite a wash, its feathers still bore a pink stain. The amount of paint that came off its body was remarkable. If someone had intentionally painted the pigeon's feathers, Insp Small expressed concern for other birds and animals in the vicinity. She expressed hope that all the paint could be removed from the pigeon's feathers, allowing its release back into the wild. However, if the feathers have sustained permanent damage, its flight capabilities could be compromised. Additionally, there is a risk that, while trying to clean itself, the pigeon might have ingested potentially harmful paint.
The initiative, a collaboration between the Labour Party and the Labour Women's Network, aims to assist over 600 women in entering politics over the next five years. This program is a tribute to Jo Cox, the late MP for Batley and Spen, who was tragically killed in Birstall, West Yorkshire, in June. Known for her advocacy of international feminism, this mother-of-two left a significant impact. The program will offer training and mentorship to women, encouraging them to pursue roles as MPs, councillors, elected mayors, and other party positions. Rachel Reeves, a Labour MP, spoke about Jo's passion for women in leadership and politics, stating that she was the first chair of the Labour Women's Network. Reeves described Jo as offering support akin to having one arm around your shoulder and the other pushing you forward. She encouraged other women to participate in parliamentary selections and run for office. This initiative follows letters from numerous female Labour MPs urging party leader Jeremy Corbyn to address personal abuse during the leadership campaign. The Conservatives, SNP, DUP, Plaid Cymru, UKIP, and Green Party are currently led by women. Tracy Brabin, a former soap actress, has been chosen as the Labour candidate for the by-election resulting from Jo Cox's death, which will take place on October 20. This year has been challenging for many Labour women MPs, but the scheme aims to bring about changes in the political landscape.
Approximately 65% of five-year-old children from disadvantaged backgrounds performed poorly in vocabulary tests, compared to 38% of more affluent children, according to the 'Ready to Read' Northern Ireland report. This report, issued by the Read On Get On coalition, was based on data from 1,955 children in 63 council wards across Northern Ireland. The report advocates for increased investment in the early years workforce and additional support for parents. Save the Children, one of the coalition members along with organizations such as Libraries NI, Parenting NI, and Early Years, published the research. Fergus Cooper, head of the charity in Northern Ireland, emphasized the importance of parental involvement in fostering literacy skills. He stated, "Many parents are unaware of the key actions they can take to reinforce learning, and early language development is a crucial skill that leads to good reading, so we need to invest and engage much earlier." Black Mountain Primary School in west Belfast, with nearly 150 pupils, has been collaborating with the charity for several years. Parents are allowed to visit the school during the school day to read with their children. Stacey Brittan, whose son AJ attends the school, credits the school's approach for their improved reading skills. "I never enjoyed reading, preferring films over books, but we started reading at night, and now AJ can't wait to buy a new book and read it, so his reading has significantly improved," she said. Elaine Crossman also visits the school to read with her son Riley. "It's fantastic because, with my work schedule, I don't always get the chance to read at home, so it's great on the days when I can spend quality time with my child reading," she said. The school's principal, Jill Black, attests to the positive impact of parental involvement on reading skills. "We started encouraging parents to come into school about three years ago, and the enthusiasm for reading has particularly improved, particularly in Primary One and Primary Two," she said. Ms. Black also noted that the initiative has strengthened the relationships between teachers and parents, and improved pupils' reading test results. "The enjoyment parents derive from reading has a ripple effect on their children," she added. "We see a substantial increase in their achievement and att
The High Court dismissed an argument from Govia Thameslink Railway (GTR), Southern's parent company, claiming that the proposed industrial action would infringe upon customers' rights. This ruling cleared the path for the initial strike day to occur on Tuesday. Southern Railway has forewarned that the strike will result in a lack of service on most routes. The RMT union, which has been conducting strikes since the summer regarding the role of conductors, is also planning additional stoppages around the Christmas and New Year period. Aslef, who viewed the court decision as a victory, deemed GTR's action as an unnecessary waste of shareholders', passengers', and taxpayers' money. Mick Whelan, the general secretary of Aslef, stated, "Now the company should engage in constructive discussions with us to reach a mutually beneficial agreement, similar to the one ScotRail achieved with us earlier this year." A GTR spokesperson expressed disappointment with the decision, stating that the unprecedented strike action by Aslef would cause significant disruption to the public. The judge, Sir Michael Burton, acknowledged that the strikes would cause substantial disruption, but they were sanctioned through a lawful ballot and did not violate freedom of movement provisions. Southern Railway had argued that the consequences of the strike constituted a breach of its customers' rights to travel under EU law, but this claim was dismissed. Nick Herbert, MP for Arundel and South Downs, suggested that the government should explore alternative measures to counter unjustifiable strike action, possibly through legislative changes. The following are the scheduled strike dates: 00:01 Tuesday 6 December to 23:59 Thursday 8 December (RMT), 00:01 Tuesday 13 December to 23:59 Wednesday 14 December (Aslef), 00:01 Friday 16 December to 23:59: Friday 16 December (Aslef), 00:01 Monday 19 December to 23:59 Tuesday 20 December (RMT), 00:01 Saturday 31 December to 23:59 Monday 2 January (RMT), and 00:01 Monday 9 January to 23:59: Saturday 14 January (Aslef).
Thirty-one years ago, Katrice Lee mysteriously vanished from a shop situated within a German military base, where her father, a Teesside resident, was stationed. The case has been the subject of a recent debate in the House of Commons, with concerns raised about the handling of the initial investigation. Iain Wright, the Labour MP representing Hartlepool, expressed his belief that the family has endured an unacceptably long wait and called for immediate action. Meanwhile, Conservative MP for Gosport, Caroline Dinenage, criticized former Prime Minister David Cameron for declining to meet with the family. Katrice's parents, Sharon Lee and Richard Lee, as well as her sister Natasha, who reside in Gosport, hold the belief that Katrice is still alive and that she was abducted. Defense Minister Mark Francois has promised to meet with the family before Christmas. In his statement, he acknowledged the limitations of the situation, stating, "We do not possess a time machine, and we cannot revisit that day in 1981 when Katrice disappeared." However, he assured that the Royal Military Police are applying all modern investigative techniques diligently and are seeking advice from leading international experts. In essence, they are employing every practical means to uncover the truth about Katrice's disappearance. Katrice vanished on her second birthday, while her family was out purchasing party food. Her mother, momentarily stepping away from the checkout to retrieve crisps, returned to find Katrice missing.
In an interview with The New York Times, Mr. Varoufakis stated that he is unable to disclose a recording due to confidentiality regulations. This comes amid controversy surrounding his negotiating tactics during debt talks. Greece's administration has expressed concern about repaying €1.5 billion to the International Monetary Fund (IMF) by June 5 without a swift agreement. Mr. Varoufakis disputed allegations of verbal abuse and name-calling by his fellow finance ministers at a meeting in Riga in April. He asserted, "I categorically deny being called names, being labeled a time-waster, and all such claims with every fiber of my being." However, he mentioned that he had recorded the meeting but was bound by confidentiality rules to share the recording. In a subsequent statement, he did not dispute the report, instead emphasizing his commitment to maintaining the confidentiality of his discussions. Mr. Varoufakis was replaced as the lead negotiator following the meeting due to reports of a disagreement. He denied being sidelined. Greece's debt stands at €320 billion, with €240 billion received through a European bailout. Greece owes Germany €56 billion, representing 177% of the country's debt-to-GDP ratio. Since 2010, the Greek economy has shrunk by 25%, and unemployment stands at 26%. Greece is currently engaged in negotiations with the EU and IMF over economic reforms, which include pension reform, taxation, labor market deregulation, and the rehiring of 4,000 former civil servants. The Greek Prime Minister, Alexis Tsipras, is attending the EU Eastern Partnership Summit in Riga to discuss a debt deal with other EU leaders. The government has prioritized paying salaries, pensions, and general state expenses over the IMF repayment due on June 5. Nikos Filis, spokesman for the ruling Syriza party's lawmakers, stated, "If there is no deal by [June 5]... they won't get any money." Meanwhile, German Finance Minister Wolfgang Schaeuble did not rule out the possibility of a Greek debt default, according to media reports.
A hearing will decide if Sgt Bergdahl will face court-martial proceedings. If found guilty, he could be sentenced to life imprisonment. Sgt Bergdahl was freed in May after five years of captivity by the Taliban. His release, resulting from a controversial prisoner swap, ignited a heated political debate. The US Army announced the charges against Sgt Bergdahl at US Army Forces Command in Fort Bragg, North Carolina on Wednesday. Colonel Daniel King stated that the US Army Forces Command had reviewed the Army's investigation into Sgt Bergdahl's disappearance before bringing the charges. Col King stated that Sgt Bergdahl has been charged with one count of desertion with intent to shirk important or hazardous duty and one count of misbehavior before the enemy by endangering the safety of a command, unit or place. Conviction on the desertion charge could result in up to five years in prison, while a conviction on the misbehavior charge carries a maximum sentence of life imprisonment. The case will now proceed to a preliminary hearing to determine if he should stand trial. Col King compared this hearing to a civilian grand jury proceeding. The date for the hearing has not been set. Fellow soldiers claim that Sgt Bergdahl intentionally left his unit while deployed in Afghanistan in June 2009. He was captured and held by the Taliban for five years, before being handed over to a team of US special forces soldiers last May. In exchange for his release, five senior Taliban commanders held at Guantanamo Bay were transferred to the custody of the Gulf state of Qatar, which facilitated the deal. His release outraged Republicans and some Democrats, who argued that the prisoner swap could potentially endanger American lives. Top Congressional Republicans contended that the deal violated US law and amounted to negotiating with terrorists - allegations denied by the Obama administration. They also objected to the fact that Congress was not notified of the deal. His family has received death threats, and a welcoming party in his hometown in the state of Idaho was cancelled due to safety concerns.

The Historical Institutional Abuse (HIA) Inquiry proposed a state apology and financial compensation for the victims. However, the collapse of Stormont in January stalled the process. A representative stated that Secretary of State James Brokenshire has not made any decisions regarding the issue. The government representative further clarified that the matter remains a devolved issue, and that compensation for abuse victims is one of the reasons Brokenshire is determined to reinstate the Northern Ireland Executive as soon as possible. The secretary aims to provide a response to Sir Anthony's (HIA) report at the earliest opportunity. Margaret McGuckian, founder of Survivors and Victims of Institutional Abuse (Savia), deemed the comments as evasive and accused Brokenshire of neglecting the victims. McGuckian claimed that during a meeting with the head of the Civil Service, Brokenshire was compelled to acknowledge that the only viable solution is for the secretary of state to introduce emergency legislation. The group will now escalate their protest to Westminster. Mike Nesbitt, a Ulster Unionist MLA and former Victims Commissioner, expressed confusion over Brokenshire's decision-making. Nesbitt pointed out that Brokenshire has taken legislative action from Westminster in the past when necessary, but is now denying compensation to victims. Nesbitt argued that the requested interim payment is minimal and would not significantly impact the executive budget, yet victims continue to suffer. He asserted that the leaders of the five main Northern Ireland political parties concur that action should be taken to begin addressing the needs of victims. Previously, there have been demands for Brokenshire to act on compensation payments for abuse victims. In July, former first minister Peter Robinson suggested that there would be minimal opposition from parties at Stormont if Brokenshire initiated compensation payments immediately. Sir Anthony Hart, the judge who led the HIA Inquiry, recommended compensation, a memorial, and a public apology to abuse survivors. Hart suggested a tax-free lump sum payment for all survivors, including those from institutions not covered by the inquiry.
In a recently published report, the Conservative Police and Crime Commissioner (PCC), Adam Simmonds, detailed his proposed plans. These plans involve hiring a chief of staff and four assistant commissioners, resulting in a total of 14 positions. Previously, the police authority had seven staff members, meaning there would be an additional 10 positions under the new commission. The report emphasizes Mr. Simmonds' intent to advocate for the interests of the local community. Three new departments will be established: one focusing on drug eradication, another on faith-based and neighborhood initiatives, and a third dedicated to the protection and security of the county. The commission will continue to oversee targets and set budgets, as the previous police authority did, but will also assume new responsibilities in prevention and care for crime victims. The budget for Northamptonshire Police stands at £120.55 million for the 2012-13 fiscal year, a decrease of £2 million compared to the previous year. Over the next four years, savings of £20 million must be achieved. On Thursday, Mr. Simmonds was unreachable for comment regarding these plans. Paul Varnsverry, a Liberal Democrat election candidate, expressed interest in observing the evolution of these ideas. Jim MacArthur, who represented UKIP in the elections, initially found the plans to be complex. John Norrie, an Independent candidate in the PCC election, expressed a desire for more caution in managing taxpayer funds, believing that the role was intended for a single individual with a few staff. The Labour Party regional office was not reachable for comment. A spokesperson for the commissioner stated that staff would be consulted about the plans within the first four months of the next year.

Princess Cruise Lines, a subsidiary of Carnival Corporation, the world's largest cruise company, faces charges for illegally discharging oily waste off the coast of England in 2013. The company allegedly attempted to conceal its waste dumping activities. They have agreed to plead guilty to seven charges, which pertain to stops at ports in nine US states and two territories. The charges are linked to the Caribbean Princess cruise ship, which the authorities claim had been making illegal discharges since 2005, a year after the ship began operations. The discharges were facilitated using equipment, such as a "magic pipe," to bypass pollution-prevention tools that separate oil and monitor oil levels in the ship's water, according to the authorities. The investigation was initiated after an engineer reported an illegal dump off the coast of England in August 2013. The engineer resigned from his position when the ship reached Southampton. The ports implicated in the charges are located in Florida, Maine, Massachusetts, New Jersey, New York, Rhode Island, South Carolina, Texas, Virginia, the U.S. Virgin Islands, and Puerto Rico. Jeremy Smart, head of enforcement at the Maritime and Coastguard Agency, stated that the case sends "a clear message to the industry that such pollution practices will not be tolerated anywhere in the world." The US Justice Department also announced that the fine is the largest-ever criminal penalty for deliberate vessel pollution.
The company's current deep-diving vehicle, recently tested in the Bahamas, reaches a depth of 1km (0.6 miles). However, it serves as a prototype for a future vessel capable of descending 11km (7 miles) to the bottom of the Mariana Trench. The core element of the design is a 15cm (6in) thick glass sphere, which is being fabricated by Rayotek Scientific in San Diego. This sphere is intended to protect the three-person crew from the intense pressures of the deep while offering them a panoramic 360-degree view during the descent. Triton, the company behind this project, has commercial ambitions: they plan to sell tickets to individuals seeking the thrill of diving to the deepest point in the ocean. Bruce Jones, Triton's CEO, stated, "There are thousands who climb Everest, and Richard Branson is taking deposits for trips into near-Earth orbit. We believe we can secure $250,000 from the adventurous few to join the exclusive group of people who've reached the ocean's deepest spot." The company aims to have the craft ready within the next two years.
Approximately 200,000 individuals attended the Lumiere event in 2015, which featured 29 pieces of art illuminating Durham over a span of four nights in November. A report commissioned by the county council indicated that this event generated nearly £4 million more than the 2013 event. The council's cabinet members will be asked next week to approve a commissioning fee of £600,000 to bring back Lumiere in 2017. The remaining estimated £1.8 million cost of staging the festival will be covered by an Arts Council England grant, sponsorship, trusts, and donations, according to the authority. Council leader Simon Henig stated that the 2015 event was commissioned based on its "sound economic impact," demonstrating a "clear and significant return on our investment" for the county, businesses, and residents. Some of the increase in revenue was attributed to "greater scrutiny of visitor spend." Adam Deathe, business engagement manager at Durham Business District, confirmed that hotels, restaurants, and shops all benefited as visitors came from the North East and beyond. "Lumiere draws national attention to Durham, with hotels being booked up months in advance as people come for at least a couple of days," he said. "Often, they use the city as a base and explore the wider area as well." First held in Durham in 2009, the festival has returned every other year, while January 2016 saw organizers, Artichoke, hold the first Lumiere London.

"It's time we discuss other topics," he stated. "We should be addressing societal inequalities and broader global issues," he added. However, he noted that within the Church of Ireland, and indeed the Anglican communion, there has been an almost exclusive preoccupation with the issue of human sexuality in the public eye. Last month, Ireland made history by passing a referendum to legalize same-sex marriage with over 62% of the vote in favor of amending the constitution to allow for marriage equality. Ireland is now the first nation worldwide to legalize same-sex marriage via a popular vote.

The inaugural review, titled "Translating Learning into Action," was carried out by Queen's University and the NSPCC. Dr. John Devaney, the research lead, stated that the findings indicated Northern Ireland's child protection system was effective. He emphasized, "We possess a robust children protection system." The report will be unveiled later today. Researchers analyzed 24 reports regarding children who either passed away or sustained severe injuries. Dr. Devaney noted that in the majority of cases, the deaths were unforeseeable. However, he suggested measures such as offering family support at an earlier stage and ensuring the availability of resources for professionals to maintain longer-term involvement with families to ensure sustained improvements. The report also revealed an increase in the number of children on protection registers compared to five years ago, which the authors attribute to a more robust protection system. The report drew several conclusions: - The rate of non-accidental child deaths in Northern Ireland has decreased due to a strong child protection system. - The majority of the 24 case reviews commended the dedication and professionalism of individual staff working with the families under review. - The children in these reports were among many living in similar circumstances, known to professionals, and it was unlikely that the children who died or were seriously injured could have been identified as being at a higher risk. - Services need to intervene at an earlier stage with families to prevent problems from becoming deeply entrenched. - Services need to maintain longer-term involvement with some families to ensure that improvements in parenting are sustained in the long term. The report's authors recommended therapeutic services be provided to address the psychological harm caused by poor parenting. They also suggested that professionals be given opportunities to meet more frequently to coordinate assessments and interventions with children and families. The authors further stressed that senior managers across organizations must take responsibility for managing the workloads of individual professionals to ensure they are manageable and appropriate for their level of experience.
Irish Water has announced that it will take until Thursday to restore water supply to approximately 50,000 homes and businesses, following a burst pipe on Friday morning. The reservoir levels have dropped critically due to the incident, and the repair process has proven more challenging than initially expected, largely because of the age and type of the pipe, as well as the complexity of the repair. The water main supplying the Staleen Water Treatment Plant is situated four meters underground and is a 50-year-old pipe that has warped. The Staleen Water Treatment Plant provides drinking water to Drogheda and its surrounding areas, as well as parts of south Louth and east Meath. In response to the remaining water shortage, Irish Water and Louth County Council reinstated water rationing in certain parts of Drogheda at 8:00 local time on Monday. They have also supplemented the water supply by transporting treated water from neighboring areas using large tankers, with water tankers and containers being mobilized from across Ireland and Northern Ireland. The treated water is being injected directly into the water network to increase the available water for the rotating water rationing schedule. This approach aims to ensure that all locations have water for specific periods, with the schedule being posted on the websites of Louth County Council and Irish Water and updated as necessary. Temporary water stations have been established at various locations in east Meath and Louth, and consumers are asked to bring clean containers and boil water taken from these stations before use as a precaution. Irish Water's Northern Ireland counterpart, NI Water, has pledged to continue collaborating with Irish Water and providing support until the incident is resolved.

Archaeologists uncovered human remains following the discovery of a skeleton during construction preparations for a new classroom at St Mary's Primary School in Leith. Nine coffins were outlined in the school grounds. Experts asserted that this finding is indicative of Leith's robust archaeological past. Richard Lewis, the culture convener for the City of Edinburgh Council, stated, "The data emerging from the site is proving to be more crucial than initially anticipated. The school community has expressed keen interest in the excavation and the disclosures. As the excavation progresses, we can expect to uncover more about the previous inhabitants of Leith." John Lawson, the City of Edinburgh Council's archaeologist, added, "Leith and its vicinity boast a rich archaeological history. These excavations have exposed what seems to be a complex cemetery, dating from the 15th to the 17th centuries, containing at least nine graves, including those of adults and children buried in coffins. The discovery of these burials in this location, while a possibility, is still unexpected. Further excavations will provide additional evidence to help us accurately date these burials and their relationship to Leith's history." The area with the remains has been cordoned off, but the school remains operational. The new classrooms will still be constructed, but their opening has been delayed until after the end of June due to continued archaeological work.

Michael Martin's 19-year-old son, Chris Martin, was a healthy university rugby player at University College Cork. Tragically, two days after Christmas 2015, he woke up struggling to breathe and passed away as his family attempted to seek medical help. His father emphasized the importance of managing asthma effectively, stating, "In a million years, I never thought asthma would kill him. I never thought asthma would kill anyone, to be honest." However, asthma causes an average of one death per week in the Republic of Ireland, according to the Asthma Society of Ireland. The organization aims to dispel the notion that only those with severe asthma are at risk of death. Mr. Martin has participated in the society's latest awareness campaign, which airs on Irish TV and social media from Saturday. Chris was diagnosed with asthma when he was around four years old and had never been hospitalized due to the condition. He was an active teenager involved in various sports such as surfing and mountaineering. "Chris always carried his reliever inhaler and used it during rugby games," Mr. Martin said. "However, he would often forget to take his preventative medication. He might take it on a Monday or a Tuesday but then forget to take it on a Wednesday or Thursday." Mr. Martin explained that his son was unaware his asthma was not under control or that he was at risk of a severe attack. On the night of December 26, 2015, Chris went to bed as usual, but woke up at 6:00 the next morning unable to breathe. The family tried to get him to a doctor for a nebulizer treatment, but Chris collapsed as he tried to get dressed and passed away in front of his family. Averil Power, CEO of the Asthma Society of Ireland, stated, "Unfortunately, Michael's story is not unique. We have been contacted by the families of other young people who died of asthma in the past 18 months, aged 7, 16, 19, and 21. Most were fit and active young people who had no idea they were at risk of dying from an asthma attack." North Wales Police oversees the entire region commonly recognized as North Wales, encompassing the counties of Anglesey, Gwynedd, Conwy, Denbighshire, Flintshire, and Wrexham. With a population of approximately 675,000, the region is predominantly rural and boasts the Snowdonia National Park, home to Wales' highest peak, Snowdon. Major transportation routes like the A55 link the area to cities such as Manchester, Liverpool, and Birmingham, and the port of Holyhead on Anglesey, which offers ferry services to Ireland. Since 2009, Mark Polin has served as the force's chief constable. North Wales Police employs 1,454 officers, 888 staff, and 232 community support officers. The total budget for 2016/17 stands at £141m, with a projected savings of £4.54m between 2017/18 and 2019/20. The police precept for Band D properties amounts to £240.12, making it the highest in Wales, compared to £220.06 in Gwent, £207.85 in South Wales, and £200.07 in Dyfed-Powys Police force areas. During the year up to September 2015, there were 36,800 recorded crimes in North Wales, marking a 1% increase from the previous year. This represented the lowest increase in recorded crime in Wales for that period. Incidents of violence against the person rose by 17%, while all but one police force saw increases in this category. Annually, approximately 90 heart surgeries are performed by the Belfast Health Trust, with an additional 40 taking place in either England or Dublin. The health board is now mandated to conduct a consultation on the most effective way to address these pediatric cases elsewhere. Health Minister Edwin Poots has suggested that all possibilities should be explored, including a pan-Ireland service. Should such a solution prove unfeasible, children would need to travel to England. As the demand for this intricate service escalates across the UK, Northern Ireland is under pressure to demonstrate its ability to offer the necessary surgical support. Although the review did not identify any immediate safety issues, it determined that the service within the Belfast Health Trust is no longer financially viable. The Children's Heartbeat Trust has expressed disappointment and concern over this development. Spokesperson Sarah Quinlan, representing around 1,000 families who utilize the service, expressed that if the review found the service to be safe, there would be no reason for it to cease operation. After a discussion with the Public Health Agency, the trust informed the BBC that if the service at the Royal Belfast Hospital for Sick Children were to close, more families would be compelled to travel to England. Ms. Quinlan stated that an all-Ireland center would be the ideal solution and that this option would be widely supported.

In a speech to the David Hume Institute, Tricia Marwick advocated for a comprehensive review of the committee system's functioning. She also suggested that committee conveners be elected by their fellow members. Currently, the Scottish Parliament consists of 17 committees, with 132 positions distributed among 80 MSPs. Marwick proposes reducing the number to 10 or 11. She questioned the effectiveness of the current committee structure, stating, "Has our current committee structure served us well enough? Why has no committee proposed any committee legislation since 2002, apart from procedural legislation? Why have we conducted almost no post-legislative scrutiny?" Marwick proposed larger committees to provide a better overall policy alignment, bringing together subjects that may function better under a single, larger committee. These larger committees would focus on policy areas that are crucial to the parliament. She suggested that these committees could divide into smaller subgroups for more flexible discussions, requiring only the necessary members to participate at a given time. Marwick also supported the introduction of elected committee conveners as part of a cultural change within Holyrood. While parties would still have a proportional share of committee conveners, these conveners would be directly accountable to parliament due to a secret ballot of all MSPs. She expressed a desire for more powerful conveners with a stronger voice, not influenced by any government's legislative agenda. Holyrood's committees have been criticized for their inability to effectively scrutinize the Scottish government, with opposition MSPs accusing the SNP administration of stifling dissent.

In an open missive, a group of security and foreign policy specialists express their viewpoint that the agreement "fails to meet the administration's own benchmark of a 'satisfactory' accord." They advocate for reduced compromises on international nuclear inspections and research and development activities. The publication of this letter coincides with the approaching deadline for a final agreement. U.S. Secretary of State John Kerry and Iranian Foreign Minister Mohammad Javad Zarif are scheduled to travel to Vienna on Friday to participate in the negotiations, which may extend beyond June 30th. The P5+1 (United States, United Kingdom, France, China, Russia, and Germany) seeks to restrict Iran's sensitive nuclear activities to prevent it from rapidly developing a nuclear weapon. Iran, aiming to have sanctions lifted, maintains its nuclear work is peaceful. The open letter, published by the Washington Institute for Near East Policy on Thursday, bears the signatures of a bipartisan group of U.S. diplomats, legislators, policymakers, and experts who have been meeting regularly over the past three years to discuss Iran. The letter states, "Most of us would have preferred a stronger agreement." It continues, "This agreement will not prevent Iran from acquiring nuclear weapons capabilities. It will not necessitate the dismantling of Iran's nuclear infrastructure." However, it will reduce that infrastructure for the next 10 to 15 years. It will also implement a transparency, inspection, and consequences regime with the aim of deterring and dissuading Iran from building a nuclear weapon. The letter implores President Obama to demand that Iran disclose information about nuclear weapons research suspected by Western powers and grant access to military sites for International Atomic Energy Agency (IAEA) inspectors. Furthermore, the president is urged to set stringent limits on the research and development of centrifuges used to enrich uranium to prevent "rapid technical upgrade and expansion" of Iran's enrichment capacity at the end of any deal. Enriched uranium can be utilized for both nuclear energy and weapons. A senior official from the Obama administration confirmed that they had received the letter, stating that it generally aligns with the U.S. negotiating position within the negotiation room.
William Matchett, a former officer of the Royal Ulster Constabulary's Special Branch, has provided counsel on policing in Iraq and Afghanistan, and authored a book detailing the Special Branch's role during the Northern Ireland Troubles. He notes that the current form of terrorism threatening the UK differs significantly from the one faced during the Troubles. He explains that the new threat, as demonstrated in England, is characterized by rapid and dynamic attacks using vehicles and knives, which can result in fatalities within seconds. Unlike the Troubles, where attacks were swift and focused on a single target, the recent attacks in England have shown that perpetrators linger to cause maximum casualties. In light of this evolving threat, Matchett suggests that it has become challenging to maintain public safety without more police officers carrying sidearms. This extends from beat officers and community officers to undercover officers, both on and off duty. Sadly, seven people lost their lives and 48 were injured in the recent terror attack in London. The attackers, armed with vehicles and knives, were shot by police from an armed response unit just minutes after striking pedestrians on London Bridge and stabbing people in Borough Market. An off-duty policeman, who tackled one of the attackers, was critically injured and remains hospitalized. Another officer, armed only with a baton, was seriously injured but is now in stable condition. While all police officers in Northern Ireland are armed on duty and carry personal protection weapons off-duty, Cressida Dick, commissioner of the Metropolitan Police, believes that this should not be the norm for the rest of the UK. She argues that the public and officers are not comfortable with the idea of every police officer being armed, and that arming officers may not necessarily provide increased protection for both the public and officers. The Director-General of the International Atomic Energy Agency (IAEA), Yukiya Amano, stated that Iran had provided environmental samples from the Parchin military complex, which he visited on Sunday. Amano noted that significant work still needs to be done before the investigation can be concluded by the December deadline. Western nations have long suspected that nuclear weapons research was conducted at Parchin, but Iran maintains its nuclear program is entirely peaceful. The conclusion of the IAEA's investigation is essential for the execution of the comprehensive agreement reached in July between six world powers and Iran, under which Iran will restrict its sensitive nuclear activities in exchange for sanctions relief. The Parchin facility, located south of Tehran, is primarily used for research, development, and production of ammunition, rockets, and explosives. Reports of its potential involvement in Iran's nuclear program emerged in 2004, following claims of a large explosives containment vessel being built for hydrodynamic experiments, which the IAEA considers strong indicators of possible nuclear weapon development. During a 2005 visit, no unusual activities were observed, and environmental samples did not show signs of nuclear material, yet suspicions remained. Until recently, the Iranian government had consistently rejected IAEA requests to revisit Parchin, and extensive renovations, demolitions, and new constructions were observed. On Sunday, Amano, along with the head of the IAEA's Department of Safeguards, Tero Varjoranta, was given a tour of Parchin. At a news conference in Vienna on Monday, Amano disclosed that environmental samples were collected from areas of interest at Parchin last week, with Iranian experts participating in the sample-taking process. He did not specify whether IAEA inspectors were present during the procedure but emphasized that the process was conducted in a manner that did not compromise verification procedures. Amano confirmed the integrity of the sampling process and the authenticity of the samples, stating they were collected under the IAEA's responsibility and monitoring. The collection of samples at Parchin by Iranian experts is thought to be part of a confidential "road map" agreement signed by the IAEA and Iran in July.

The poll conducted by YouGov for ITV Cymru Wales and Cardiff University suggests that in a general election, support stands at 29% (an increase of 6% from July), with Labour at 35% (+1), Plaid Cymru at 13% (-3), UKIP at 14% (-2), and the Lib Dems at 7% (-1). This is the first Welsh poll since Theresa May became Prime Minister. According to political analyst Prof Roger Scully, the results indicate a "honeymoon period" for May as a new party leader. If the current Westminster constituency boundaries were applied, the only change would be Labour losing Ynys Mon to Plaid Cymru, resulting in Labour holding 24 out of the 40 Welsh seats, the Conservatives 11, Plaid Cymru four, and the Liberal Democrats one. However, a new Parliamentary map proposed by the Boundary Commission would reduce the number of Welsh MPs to 29. Under these arrangements, Labour would only narrowly win the majority of Welsh seats. The assembly election projections show Labour losing three seats, giving them 26 AMs, and the Conservatives gaining three to once again become the largest opposition party in Cardiff Bay, with a 14-strong group. The projections maintain the same number of AMs for the other parties as in May's assembly election - 12 for Plaid, 7 for UKIP, and 1 for the Liberal Democrats. Prof Scully notes a "significant lift" in the Conservative fortunes indicated by the poll, which he suggests mirrors trends seen in recent Britain-wide polls. He compares this to the short-lived "Corbyn Bounce" following Jeremy Corbyn's leadership victory last year. The poll had a sample of 1,001 adults and was conducted online by YouGov from 18-21 September 2016.
Pauline McKee, a grandmother of thirteen, was playing the Miss Kitty slot machine at a casino when she unexpectedly won $1.85. However, a message on the screen announced a bonus worth an astounding $41,797,550.16. The casino refused to honor this win, claiming it was a computer glitch. McKee, who had hoped to use the winnings to assist her family financially, sued the casino. The Iowa Supreme Court eventually dismissed her case. "From the start, I had my doubts because that's an enormous amount of money for a penny machine," she told the Chicago Tribune. The Iowa Racing and Gaming Commission (IRGC) investigated the matter, securing the machine and sending it to an independent testing laboratory. Investigators found that the software was programmed to allow a bonus of up to $10,000, but they couldn't determine how the multi-million dollar bonus message had appeared. The machine's manufacturer, Aristocrat Technologies, stated that they had been aware of this type of error in the display and suggested that component degradation over time might increase the likelihood of this rare occurrence. The company had alerted casinos to the glitch in 2010 and recommended disabling the bonus feature as a precautionary measure. It appears the casino did not implement this measure. The IRGC concluded that the bonus display was not valid and that the slot machine game malfunctioned. McKee filed a lawsuit against the casino in 2012, claiming breach of contract and consumer fraud. The court sided with the casino in 2013, but the case continued as McKee's lawyers argued that the IRGC had no jurisdiction in disputes between casinos and their patrons. They also questioned whether the machine had truly malfunctioned, as the IRGC had concluded, and whether McKee had actually entered into a contract when playing the game. Similar cases involving erroneous bonus displays have occurred in the US. In 2009, a player won a bonus of $1m on screen, only for a Mississippi court to dismiss the claim due to game rules limiting payouts to $8,000. McKee's legal battle came to an end on 24 April when the Iowa Supreme Court rejected her case.

She noted that when faced with resistance from the two OAPs, McLeod gave up and was escorted out of the house without causing injury. Defense counsel Matt Jackson expressed McLeod's deep regret for his actions, stating that forcing his way into the house was unforgivable.
Some children are gaining improved access to educational opportunities, according to an analysis by Alan Milburn and others in the Social Mobility and Child Poverty Commission. This report shares similarities with concerns raised by Ofsted in their annual report and aligns closely with the government's policy on school improvement - the controversial notion that transforming schools into academies is the primary solution for success. However, the evidence suggests a more complex picture, as schools have converted under various circumstances. The report raises a significant concern about the pay for new teachers, warning that it is lagging behind other graduate jobs in the UK and those in similar developed economies. The median starting salary for top graduate jobs this year is £30,000, while in London it's £27,800 and around £5,000 less in other parts of the country for teachers. The Commission has urged an increase in new teachers' wages. This call for higher teacher salaries comes as the body responsible for advising the government on school teachers' pay begins to consider the numerous submissions it receives during this time of year. The teaching unions, in unison, are advocating for pay rises exceeding the average 1% increase in public pay set out by George Osborne's recent spending review. They cite difficulties in recruiting teachers and anecdotal evidence from head teachers that it is becoming increasingly challenging to secure high-quality candidates, particularly in areas where they are most needed. The government may not be inclined to yield on pay, potentially facing growing discontent within the teaching profession. However, they are planning to recruit an elite group of 1,500 teachers for underperforming schools. Many believe these teachers will need to be deployed in groups of schools to be effective. Additionally, ministers will be emphasizing the long-term benefits of the profession, such as job satisfaction and potential for long-term pay growth. International research indicates that teachers taking on more responsibility can expect to be paid reasonably well in the long term compared to other countries. However, this may not be sufficient to prevent the pay review body from repeating its warning from the last report that there are growing challenges in recruiting enough teachers to meet the rising pupil numbers. It's unlikely the government will go as far as the unions would like and recommend an exception to public pay restraint for teachers.
A study conducted by Leicester University revealed a decreasing trend in infant mortality rates throughout the United Kingdom, with Scotland leading the charge among the nations. The rate of stillbirths and deaths of babies within 28 days in Scotland is 4.72 per 1,000 live births, which is slightly higher than the 4.3 rate in Nordic countries, renowned for their exceptional neonatal survival rates. In 2015, the overall rate for the UK for stillbirths and deaths of babies within 28 days of birth was 5.61 per 1,000 live births, a decrease from 6.04 in 2013. In Scotland, the rate has dropped from 5.43 to 4.72, the lowest among all the UK nations. Mary Ross-Davie, the Royal College of Midwives Scotland director, deemed the report positive news for Scotland. She attributed the improvement to efforts focusing on discouraging pregnant women from smoking, encouraging them to monitor fetal movements, and implementing schemes for midwives and obstetricians to consistently measure the growth of babies and respond promptly to any noted issues. Ross-Davie suggested that Scotland began addressing this issue earlier than other UK countries, having established a national stillbirth group in 2013 and the Maternity Care Quality Improvement Collaborative since then. Ross-Davie acknowledged that deprivation plays a significant role in stillbirth and neonatal death rates. She emphasized the need for continued efforts to further reduce these rates in Scotland. Ross-Davie noted that Scotland has looked to Scandinavia for inspiration in reducing stillbirth rates, highlighting the more equal society in Scandinavian countries, which results in lower levels of deprivation. Professor Elizabeth Draper, the report's author, confirmed an 8% decrease in stillbirth rates across the UK. She explained that while the decrease in Scotland has been similar, the initial rate was lower, resulting in a lower stillbirth rate in Scotland compared to the rest of the UK. Draper's ultimate goal is to substantially reduce stillbirth rates in all four countries of the UK, aiming to match the Nordic levels of stillbirth.

A young man in his twenties is currently hospitalized in Multan, suffering from burns covering half of his body. According to his family, he and the woman had been in a relationship, but he did not wish to marry her. The woman claims she acted in self-defense following an attack. Although acid attacks are relatively common in Pakistan, with hundreds reported annually, it is uncommon for men to be the victims. The incident in Multan occurred during the early hours of Tuesday. Local journalists report that the man was seen stumbling out of the woman's house, screaming and covered in acid. A local police official, speaking to the AFP news agency, stated that the man had gone to visit the woman as usual, but she threw acid on his body after he again refused to marry her. The man was able to turn, so the acid primarily burned his back, sparing his face, as per the officer. Pakistani broadcaster Dunya TV indicates that women are the victims in approximately 80% of acid attacks in the country. Experts suggest that acid is easily accessible in South Asia, and many victims are attacked after rejecting suitors or due to family disputes. A salon in the area is assisting victims of acid attacks.
