Located on the Great Silk Road trade route, Armenia has been impacted by various cultural influences and empires throughout its history. Gaining independence from the Soviet Union in 1991, Armenia was soon embroiled in a violent conflict with Azerbaijan over the predominantly Armenian-speaking region of Nagorno-Karabakh. One of the earliest Christian civilizations, its first churches were established in the 4th century. Throughout subsequent centuries, Armenia experienced frequent shifts in control between Byzantine, Persian, Mongol, Turkish, and independent rule. The current population is approximately 3.1 million, living across an area of 29,743 square kilometers (11,484 square miles). The primary languages are Armenian and Russian, with Christianity as the main religion. Life expectancy is 71 years for men and 77 years for women. Major exports include diamonds, machinery, and foodstuffs. Serge Sarkisian, who served as president from 2008 to 2018, previously held positions in the defense committee of the self-proclaimed Nagorno-Karabakh Republic, as well as Armenia's minister of defense, minister of national security, and head of the presidential staff. In 2009, Sarkisian signed a landmark agreement to re-establish diplomatic relations with Turkey, but the agreement's ratification was suspended after a few months due to pressure from nationalists on both sides. Television is the primary medium in Armenia, with around 25 private stations and two public networks. Few Armenians rely on newspapers as their primary news source, as print runs are typically small (a few thousand copies) and most titles are owned by wealthy individuals or political parties. A media law prohibits censorship, but libel and defamation can result in prison sentences for journalists. Key historical events include: - 1915-1917: Between 600,000 and 1.5 million Armenians are killed or deported from their homeland in Anatolia to present-day Syria, a event that Armenia recognizes as genocide, while Turkey does not. - 1918: Independent Armenia emerges following the defeat of the Ottoman Empire in World War I. - 192
A Malayan tapir named Nias was born at Port Lympne Reserve, located near Hythe, to parents Malacca and Hunter. The Malayan tapir population is now estimated to be between 1,500 and 2,000 individuals. Adrian Harland, the reserve's director, expressed his excitement, stating, "Nias' birth is terrific news for the Malayan tapir population and our diligent hoofstock caretakers. We're incredibly proud of our breeding success with this species." The Malayan tapir, scientifically known as Tapirus indicus, is also known as the Asian tapir and is the largest of the four tapir species. It is related to both horses and rhinoceroses. This "odd-toed" ungulate has four toes on each front foot and three toes on each back foot, each ending in a hoof. The species typically has a gestation period of around 13 months and is born with white spots and stripes on black skin. As they mature, they become darker, providing camouflage in the moonlit forests of their natural habitat. Nias is the 11th Malayan tapir born at Port Lympne Reserve since 1989, making it one of Europe's most successful breeders of the animal. To stay updated, follow @BBCNewsbeat on Twitter and Radio1Newsbeat on YouTube.
In the wake of recent acid attacks in London, which targeted delivery riders on bikes and mopeds, over 70 Deliveroo riders have expressed safety concerns, refusing to complete deliveries. To address these issues, Deliveroo is introducing a new app feature that enables riders to report security concerns, as well as trialing helmet cameras. Last month, two Deliveroo and UberEats riders were among five individuals injured in north London within 90 minutes, after moped riders hurled acid in their faces. Since all Deliveroo drivers are self-employed, they are not eligible for sick pay if attacked. Additionally, they use their own vehicles for deliveries. Deliveroo has updated its app, allowing riders to report specific delivery location safety concerns, which will then be shared with the company's headquarters and local authorities. The company is also conducting a trial of helmet-mounted cameras to aid riders in gathering evidence and providing information to the police. This trial is being held in Hackney, east London, an area that has become a problematic spot for riders. Deliveroo is hiring 50 new staff members with a focus on rider safety. Delivery rider Jabed Hussein, a victim of the acid attacks last month, told the BBC's Victoria Derbyshire program: "It felt like being set on fire, I was screaming on the street. This job should not put one's life at risk. Drivers I know are now quitting at 10 PM, it's too dangerous after that. Most of them won't work after this, whereas we used to work until 1 AM. Now, I feel I'm working in some sort of jungle, like anything can attack me, I'm not safe." Deliveroo managing director Dan Warne stated: "The safety of our riders is our top priority, and it's our responsibility as a company to intensify our efforts so they can feel safe at all times." Tune in to the BBC's Victoria Derbyshire program weekdays between 9 AM and 11 AM on BBC Two and the BBC News channel.
On January 31st, the body of India Chipchase, aged 20, was discovered at a residence situated on Stanley Road, Northampton. Edward Tenniswood, a 52-year-old resident of the same street, has been charged with her murder in Birmingham Crown Court. Christopher Donnellan QC, the prosecutor, stated that Miss Chipchase's body was found on a mattress within Tenniswood's home. In the early hours of 30th January, it was alleged that Tenniswood approached NB's cocktail bar in Northampton while India was in a heavily intoxicated state, barely able to stand. Witnesses claimed that Tenniswood seemed to recognize her and promised to safely escort her home, but he did not follow through on his word. According to the prosecutor, India's death was not an accident but the result of Tenniswood raping and strangling her at his residence on Stanley Road. The prosecution argued that when she resisted, he tightened his grip around her throat, causing her to sustain injuries to her head and face. After her death, Tenniswood reportedly cleaned up the scene, re-dressing her, covering her with a sheet, and removing her belongings. It was also revealed that Tenniswood wore clear plastic vinyl gloves during the incident, which were later found in a trash bag. The gloves contained India's blood on the outside and Tenniswood's DNA on the inside, suggesting he was aware of the nature of his actions. The prosecutor suggested that Tenniswood's motive was likely sexual, and when India resisted him, he was determined to have sexual intercourse. The court heard that Tenniswood checked into the Ibis Hotel in Mare Fare around 21:30 GMT on 30th January. Miss Chipchase's body was discovered inside Tenniswood's home at approximately 15:30 the following day, and he was arrested three hours later. When Tenniswood was apprehended, police noticed a scratch on his neck and other injuries on his body. The trial is ongoing.

In 2015, the British Film Institute (BFI) reported a 17% increase in box office revenue, amounting to £1.24 billion. This surge in earnings was accompanied by a 9% rise in cinema admissions, with a total of 171.9 million tickets sold. Additionally, the expenditure on film production reached £1.41 billion, marking the second-highest amount ever recorded. Notably, this is the first year since 2012 that both admissions and box office revenue have increased. Five films surpassed the £40 million mark at the UK box office in 2015, compared to only one in 2014. Star Wars: The Force Awakens, released in December, became the UK's highest-grossing film of all time with earnings of £114 million. Spectre, the latest installment in the James Bond franchise, followed closely behind with £94.7 million, while Jurassic World took third place with £64.5 million. The Avengers: Age of Ultron earned £48.3 million, and Minions was fifth with £47.7 million. The BFI also noted that UK independent films held a 11% market share in 2015, which is the third-highest on record. Eight of these independent films grossed more than £5 million at the box office. The highest-grossing independent film was Tom Hardy's Legend, which earned £18.4 million. The Second Best Exotic Marigold Hotel came in second place with £16 million. Despite the UK's film production spend figure decreasing by 6% compared to the record-breaking total of £1.49 billion in 2014, Amanda Nevill, the BFI's chief executive, expressed optimism. She stated, "With spend from film production over £1.4 billion, audiences flocking to cinemas, and UK box office topping £1.2 billion for the first time, today's numbers show a golden age of British film continuing - and underline film's important contribution to the UK economy." Her compatriot Shelly-Ann Fraser-Pryce, aiming to become the first woman to win three consecutive golds in the event, placed third with a time of 10.86 seconds. Tori Bowie of the USA took the silver medal in 10.83 seconds, while Dafne Schippers of the Netherlands, who was second to Fraser-Pryce at the 2015 World Championships, finished fifth in 10.90 seconds. Thompson expressed her surprise upon crossing the finish line, stating, "I didn't know how to celebrate when I saw I was clear." Fraser-Pryce, on the other hand, was proud that the 100-meter title remained in Jamaica, saying, "I'm on the podium with my training partner. I'm proud of Jamaica." Despite failing to make the final, twenty-year-old Desiree Henry of Britain was content with her personal growth, having finished fourth in 11.09 seconds in her semi-final. Asha Philip also did not advance, placing eighth in 11.33 seconds in her race. Henry reflected on her experience, saying, "I guess it wasn't the Olympic ending I was really hoping for in terms of trying to make the final. But I'm proud of myself for growing as an adult and believing that I could run up against these girls and really trying to make it to the final." Philip, too, expressed her disappointment, stating, "I'm not doing as well as I want to be doing, but I'm still at the Olympics and I've made a semi-final. I am bitter inside because I came here to at least run a personal best."
A convoy composed of buses and ambulances has arrived in Zabadani to transport fighters, along with some civilians, to Beirut. This evacuation is part of a ceasefire arranged in September, covering Zabadani and two besieged towns in the north, under attack from rebel forces. Approximately 300 families from these towns are also being granted safe passage. On Monday, at least 14 people lost their lives, and 90 were injured in two suicide bombings in the central city of Homs. Reports indicate that the explosions occurred in the Zahraa neighborhood, predominantly inhabited by members of the Alawite sect, to which President Bashar al-Assad belongs. Zabadani has been under siege by pro-government forces, backed by the Lebanese Shia militant group Hezbollah. In contrast, the towns of Kefraya and Fuaa, located in the northern province of Idlib, have been under siege by Sunni rebels. The families from Kefraya and Fuaa are being transported to Turkey, from where they will proceed to Lebanon. The International Committee for the Red Cross (ICRC), the Lebanese Red Cross, the Syrian Arab Red Crescent, and the United Nations are all participating in this operation. The evacuations on Monday represent the latest in a series of similar moves, as Zabadani is the last significant rebel stronghold along the Lebanese border. Despite holding on, the rebel fighters faced almost certain defeat. Localized truces were proposed as one of the few potential solutions to the violence and stalemate, but they have failed to generate any meaningful progress, according to correspondents.

The party leader, Kirsty Williams, described this situation as both shocking and disappointing. The Welsh Government, however, attributes this to an increase in cancer diagnoses, stating that more people are surviving the disease. The target set by the Welsh Government is for 95% of patients initially suspected of having cancer to begin definitive treatment within 62 days of diagnosis. In June 2013, Health Minister Mark Drakeford emphasized that the key to reaching this level was ensuring patients received their first hospital appointment within 10 days of referral. Between October 2013 and September 2015, more than 22,000 people were referred by their GP with suspected breast cancer. Of these, 2,249 were diagnosed, and 92.3% of these began definitive treatment within 62 days. Betsi Cadwaladr and Cardiff and Vale health boards did not provide specific details, but Betsi Cadwaladr did mention that the average wait for a cancer specialist appointment at Ysbyty Glan Clwyd and Ysbyty Gwynedd was 21 days. Powys Teaching health board refers its patients to hospitals in neighboring health boards. The latest figures for the three months to December show that 83.7% of cancer patients started treatment within 62 days. Kirsty Williams commented, "It's absolutely desperate for individuals who spend that time worrying about their health and future. But it also shows us we have a systematic problem in how we can ensure we have enough capacity within cancer services to see people within a timely fashion." A Welsh Government spokesman responded, "For many women referred to hospital with suspected breast cancer, a one-stop diagnostic clinic takes place at the same time as their first outpatient appointment, which enables them to start treatment immediately." The Ministry of Justice (MoJ) has announced that the Grade II listed prison, closed in 2013, will be utilized as a venue for the town's Year of Culture events in 2016. The Year of Culture initiative aims to bring together the town's arts groups and establish it as a cultural hub. Alex Brennan of organizers Reading UK expressed excitement, stating it would be a "wonderful" addition to the Year of Culture lineup. The MoJ plans to collaborate with a third party to prepare the venue for an autumn season of art, literature, and music, with no additional cost to the taxpayer. Rob Wilson, MP for Reading East, believes using the prison will help highlight the town's cultural and historical importance. Mr. Brennan added, "It holds a significant place in Reading's history. I hope it will become a unique and remarkable venue for cultural events." Notably, the prison served as the inspiration for Oscar Wilde's poem The Ballad Of Reading Gaol. Part of the prison stands on the site of a Scheduled Ancient Monument. The prison has also housed notable figures such as Roderick Maclean, who attempted to assassinate Queen Victoria in 1882, Amelia Dyer, convicted of baby farming murders in 1896, and Oscar Wilde, the playwright jailed after his 1895 trial for indecency. Arthur Griffith, a leading Irish republican, was also interned in the prison in 1916. In November 2015, the MoJ revealed plans to sell the site for housing. Archaeological investigations are set to commence before a development plan is finalized. Reading Borough Council has expressed interest in taking over the site, criticizing the MoJ for delaying decisions regarding its long-term future. In the 2014/15 financial year, the empty jail cost the MoJ approximately £262,000, covering security and utility bills.
Sgt Craig Nicholas Jones contended that he was unfairly convicted by magistrates due to the malfunction of the lights on two 50mph signs on the A55 at Old Colwyn, Conwy. However, judges at the High Court in London determined that the signs would have been visible in the headlights of the unmarked police car. As a result, the 90-day driving ban initially imposed by magistrates has been reinstated. Sgt Jones was on duty when his vehicle was recorded doing 98mph on the road between Llanddulas and Colwyn Bay on 1 April, 2009, according to the court. Sgt Jones's legal team argued that since the internal lights on the two 50mph signs were not functioning, he should have been acquitted at the magistrates' court. They cited a "clear and established policy" at the time not to enforce the speed limit when the signs were not functioning properly. However, Mr Justice Charles and Mr Justice Wyn Williams opined that a decision not to prosecute Sgt Jones would have undermined the integrity of the judicial system. The two judges further stated that although the lights were broken, the signs were "properly placed and fully complied with the relevant regulations". They also emphasized that minor infractions of the regulations did not affect the legality and enforceability of the speed limit. Consequently, Sgt Jones's appeal was dismissed. The 90-day driving ban, which was initially imposed at Llandudno Magistrates' Court in December 2009 and suspended pending the High Court appeal, will now commence on 31 January.

Deborah Streatfield from MyBigCareer asserts that it is fundamentally unfair for students aiming to study medicine to be required to pay for work experience that aids their university applications. This work experience is being provided by the Chartwell Trust, which runs a private hospital in Essex, and is marketed as "Chartwell Work Experience For Medical School Applications." This program offers an in-depth look into hospital work and healthcare, allowing students to shadow staff and gain valuable medical work experience to support their UCAS applications. The brochure, distributed to schools and aimed at students aged 15 and above, promises good supervision from motivated staff, hospital rotations, and interview training by qualified doctors. The trust also operates care homes, and the brochure mentions rotations through these facilities for children with learning difficulties and the elderly with dementia. Mrs. Streatfield's charity aims to provide young people with better career advice, believing that improved social mobility can be achieved through better-informed youth. However, she believes that selling work experience in this manner creates a barrier to accessing competitive courses like medicine. "Work experience is crucial when applying for medicine, and many disadvantaged students lack familial connections to leverage. This scheme is completely unfair," she stated. MyBigCareer plans to pilot unpaid work experience placements with 43 GP practices in Hackney and the City of London. In response, the directors of Chartwell Trust Care stated that the placement offers one-on-one meetings with senior management, consultants, surgeons, and directors to provide an insight into healthcare. The fee charged covers administrative costs and coaching to boost the individual's confidence and self-esteem for their career in healthcare. There have been specific concerns about the need to attract a more diverse range of applicants to medicine, with warnings that it tends to draw too many recruits from wealthier backgrounds. Research from the Medical Schools Council in December showed that over half of UK secondary schools and colleges had not provided a single applicant to medicine in recent years.

Photos of haphazardly parked bicycles in Cambridge have been shared to encourage people to reconsider their parking and security habits for bicycles. The police force emphasized that such scenes in their #badlyparkedbike campaign are not rare occurrences. According to statistics, approximately 58% of residents in Cambridge cycle at least once a month. Inspector Matt Johnson of Cambridgeshire police stated, "Although the images may seem surprising, such parking incidents are not infrequent in Cambridge. This type of parking creates an obstruction, making it difficult for others to unlock their bikes and, in some instances, leading to injuries as people attempt to remove bikes. Whenever possible, utilize a designated bike rack as these are designed to prevent theft when coupled with a strong lock." While several individuals agreed that poor parking is potentially hazardous, others pointed out that the crackdown has underscored the need for more bike racks. Al Storer, one of the users, remarked, "All [it]... truly illustrates areas where the available bike parking is extremely inadequate." Inspector Johnson acknowledged the extensive cycle parking facilities across the city but admitted that the issue of abandoned bicycles occupying spaces persists as an ongoing concern.
The UK Intellectual Property Office (IPO) has upheld Swatch's opposition to Apple's use of the name "iWatch" in the UK, a decision made in April following a hearing. Swatch argued that the name was too similar to their existing trademarks, "iSwatch" and "Swatch." As a result of this ruling, Apple can only use the "iWatch" name in the UK for specific products, such as computer software and accessories, but not for computer hardware, monitors, audio and video devices, or cameras. This decision underscores the challenges global brands face when launching new products internationally. Trademarks are territorial, requiring registration in each country of interest. If Apple had not been able to secure the "iWatch" trademark globally, it would have significantly impacted their decision not to use the name for their smartwatch, according to trademark lawyer Sharon Daboul at law firm EIP. Swatch has chosen not to comment on the IPO's decision.

The park staff stated that her condition was beyond saving. A £200 reward has been offered for any substantial information leading to a successful prosecution. Further updates on this and other local news from East Yorkshire and Humberside will be shared. Photographer Joanne Coates has been documenting the lives of Scotland's fishing industry workers for the past three years, portraying the salt-soaked men who endure long hours at sea. For Coates, whose childhood was spent in rural northern England, these images are a continuation of her visits to the sea. Every Sunday, she and her brother were taken to Scarborough, Whitby, and Bridlington, offering her a glimpse into her future subjects. "Rubber boots here, nets there, snippets of seafarers' conversations," says Coates. "The worn sweaters, the big smiles. I can't explain the connection, but it was there." These photographs depict a world of arduous work and lengthy days, marked by grime-stained overalls and gray skies. It's a perilous occupation. In the first six months of 2016, more lives were lost at sea than in the entire previous year, according to the Fishermen's Mission charity, which reports an annual average of 15 fishermen either killed or seriously injured. In Orkney, where Coates took these images, fishing is deeply ingrained in the lives of many men. She captures the inshore fishermen of the Orkney Fishermen's Society, a cooperative founded in 1953, focusing primarily on brown crab and lobster. This group collaborates closely with researchers to preserve a sustainable fishing ground, as Orkney is also home to Europe's largest lobster hatchery, which annually releases over 100,000 lobsters. Coates' evocative photographs chronicle the daily lives of these men as they navigate an uncertain future.

On the 5th of April, Dennis Green, aged 81, and his wife Doreen, 80, tragically passed away in a two-vehicle accident at Gate Helmsley, close to York. The couple were fondly remembered as "cherished and deeply loved parents, grandparents, and great-grandparents." In a statement issued following the collision, their family expressed, "Our hearts are heavy with loss, words cannot fully express our sorrow." Relatives stated that Mr. and Mrs. Green, residents of Strensall in York, were avid sequence dancers, took regular holidays, and led an "active and vibrant social life." They were well-regarded and respected figures in their community where they had resided for over 50 years. Their absence will be deeply felt not only in the village but also amongst their extensive circle of friends. The family revealed that the day prior to the unfortunate incident, they had celebrated their 60th wedding anniversary. A family gathering had been planned for the weekend, but fate had other plans. They lamented, "The void left by their passing will be immense within our close-knit family, but we will find solace in each other and in the cherished memories we share of them." They found some comfort in the knowledge that they spent their lives together. The accident occurred around 1:20 PM BST on Northgate Lane, Gate Helmsley. At the time of the collision, Mr. Green was driving their Citroen Xsara, and his wife, Mrs. Green, was a passenger. The other vehicle involved was a Land Rover Defender, driven by a man in his 20s. He was treated for minor injuries at the scene.
The Hamilton Flute Band, established in 1856 and one of the oldest in Northern Ireland, played a significant role during World War I. Serving as the nucleus of the 10th battalion of the Royal Inniskilling Fusiliers' regimental band, they participated in the Battle of the Somme, where their bass drum was even used as a communion table. Tragically, Lance Corporal Joseph Scott from Moore Street and Rifleman George Mills from Pine Street lost their lives on the first day of the battle, July 1st, with many more casualties following. Following the Armistice, the surviving members worked to rebuild the band, a task made challenging by the loss of most of the instruments during combat. The bass drum, which had served through the war and was used as a communion table on battlefields, was eventually returned and now hangs in the Chapter House of St Columb's Cathedral. Robert McGonagle, secretary of the Hamilton Flute Band, praised the courage of the band members, expressing wonder if such dedication would be seen today. He commissioned a special piece of music to honor the band's role in the Battle of the Somme, including their march to war and their harrowing experience over the trenches. In remembrance of those who lost their lives, a special drum service will be held at St Columb's Cathedral this year, alongside the act of remembrance at the war memorial in Derry. The piece of music, specially composed for this occasion, will be a fitting tribute to the Hamilton Flute Band's remarkable story. To learn more about this historic band, visit the linked article.

Notable figures such as Cleo Rocos, Kerry Katona, Denise Welch, Christopher Maloney, Vanessa Feltz, and Kym Marsh were among those expressing their condolences. The tour was named after a mix-up on this year's Celebrity Big Brother, where Gest was a contestant. Pollard was moved to tears, believing Gest, who was ill at the time, had also died. The "Alive With Soul" tour consists of nine dates, starting on 1 July at York's Barbican Centre and concluding in Nottingham on 13 July. Tributes will also be paid on the tour to singer Billy Paul, who passed away earlier this week. Handi added that the artists, who had become like a family due to their close working relationship, were determined to go ahead with the tour to remember David through their music. Gest passed away at the Four Seasons hotel in Canary Wharf, London, earlier this month.
Governor Jerry Brown and other prominent legislators initially incorporated the proposal into a climate change bill, but were compelled to step back due to escalating resistance. State senate leader Kevin de Leon, who endorsed the reduction, alleged that oil corporations were employing "intimidation tactics." The leaders have declared their intention to advance other reforms, such as enhancing the use of renewable electricity. "I'd argue that oil has won the minor battle, but they've lost the larger war," Mr Brown stated. "I'm more resolved than ever to ensure our regulatory system serves the people of California - purifying the air, decreasing petroleum usage, and fostering green jobs." The plans to mandate a 50% decrease in petroleum usage in vehicles by 2030 faced intense opposition from business associations and oil companies, who forecasted adverse effects on California's economy. Mr De Leon asserted that the industry had a "selfish agenda" and accused it of constructing a "multibillion-dollar smokescreen" to dissuade lawmakers from endorsing the legislation. Republicans, along with many moderate Democrats, had resisted the proposal. Catherine Reheis-Boyd, president of the Western States Petroleum Association, applauded Wednesday's decision to lower the emissions target from the bill. "Today's announcement was an acknowledgement that California's energy future, economic competitiveness, and environment are intertwined," she added. California ranks as the second-largest emitter of carbon dioxide from burning fossil fuels among U.S. states. Mr Brown intends to proceed with the two remaining components of the climate change bill, focusing on increasing renewable electricity use and improving energy efficiency in buildings. This issue has been a significant focus for the governor during his final term. He is expected to attend the United Nations climate change conference in Paris in November.

Despite uncertainties surrounding the statistics due to the absence of a specialized pediatric brain injury facility, Brain Injury Matters emphasized the importance of early detection, as symptoms may not be immediately apparent. Instead, they gradually manifest as the brain develops. One mother shared her experience; her eight-year-old son, Eric, had a kidney infection that evolved into encephalitis, leading to brain swelling. She described his symptoms as a sore head, sensitivity to light, and vomiting. Hospitalized first at the Causeway Hospital and then the Royal Victoria Hospital, Eric emerged a changed boy due to the infection. A year later, the family is advocating for increased awareness alongside Brain Injury Matters. Janet noted that her son now tires easily, both physically and mentally, and struggles with concepts of time. He was previously a bright child but now requires assistance due to dyslexia and difficulty focusing. Despite these challenges, she fondly refers to him as "great." Acquired brain injuries have become more common due to advancements in medical care, leading to reduced mortality rates. These injuries can stem from road accidents, falls, infections, lack of oxygen, or strokes. Although they can affect individuals of all ages, they tend to peak in early childhood (up to age 4), adolescence (ages 15-29), and more frequently in males than females. Fiona McCabe, the CEO of Brain Injury Matters, revealed that the charity is assisting an increasing number of families. Based on evidence from the UK and anecdotal knowledge, she suspects there could be around 1,000 new cases of brain injury in children in Northern Ireland each year. Without a clear understanding of the collective number of children with brain injuries in Northern Ireland, it is challenging to allocate resources effectively. This challenge is exacerbated by the annual increase in cases. Eric's mother shared that her family is receiving support from both Brain Injury Matters and the Child Brain Injury Trust. She expressed gratitude for the assistance at home, particularly with the other children, who also struggle to adapt to the changed Eric.

The Budgetary Control Committee of the parliament is questioning the allowances given to Martin Schulz's advisor, Markus Engels, according to documents obtained by the BBC. In 2012, it appears that Mr. Engels was allowed to claim a residence in Brussels while residing in Berlin. There has been no response from Mr. Schulz's SPD party when approached by the BBC. Both Germany's Spiegel magazine and Stern magazine have stated that travel arrangements like those for Mr. Engels are common practice in Brussels. The EU anti-fraud office, Olaf, has confirmed it is examining media reports concerning the employment conditions of certain European Parliament staff members, without naming them. Olaf will assess these reports to determine if they fall within its jurisdiction and if there is enough suspicion of fraud, corruption, or illegal activity affecting the EU's financial interests for an investigation to be launched. Once analyzed, Olaf will decide whether or not to initiate an investigation. Mr. Schulz served as the president of the European Parliament from 2012 until this year, when he stepped down to run for the German general election in September. Under his new leadership, the center-left SPD, a junior partner in Chancellor Angela Merkel's governing coalition, has seen a rise in poll ratings. Mr. Engels now manages Mr. Schulz's election campaign. A letter, reprinted by Germany's Spiegel magazine, shows that in January 2012, Mr. Schulz requested the European Parliament's then Director-General for Communication, Juana Lahousse-Juarez, to authorize a "long-term mission" for Mr. Engels to support his activities as EP president in Berlin. Unpublished documents from the EP's Budgetary Control Committee seen by the BBC question why Brussels was chosen as Mr. Engels' place of employment, which entitled him to daily allowances, expat allowance, and travel costs, despite no travel costs occurring. The documents also inquire about the selection process for the post and who made the decision regarding Mr. Engels' place of employment. A formal response to the committee's questions is expected early next month.

Teachers, carrying banners and voicing chants, walked from Llandrindod High School to County Hall. The National Union of Teachers (NUT) asserted that the strike was undertaken to safeguard the education rights of children, whose right to a quality education is allegedly being eroded. Arwel Jones, the cabinet member for schools at Powys council, expressed disappointment over the union's actions. The council is currently considering proposals to reorganize secondary schools in the mid and southern parts of the county. Councillor Jones stated that schools are confronting "severe challenges," and that the strike would not derail their plans but potentially disrupt the learning process of schoolchildren at a critical juncture. A total of 19 Powys schools have been impacted by the strike; six are fully closed, and 13 are partially operational. Cai Jones, the NUT organizer, lauded the turnout for the march, noting that parents and children joined the teachers. He stated, "We walked through the town center, and the community rallied behind us. People emerged from their shops to express their support, and cars honked as they passed." The teachers presented a list of their demands to Powys council at County Hall and convened at Llandrindod Pavilion later to deliberate on the changes they wish to implement.
Eleven care workers are set to be sentenced for the mistreatment and neglect of five patients at Winterbourne View, with additional allegations of abuse against former patient Ben Pullar by the BBC. The National Autistic Society has urged for a thorough investigation into the case, but police have declined to reopen it. Mr. Pullar, who is autistic, bipolar, and has severe learning difficulties, spent nearly a year at Winterbourne View from July 2009. Nurse Maxwell Nyamukapa was suspended and later reinstated following the incident. Despite multiple attempts, Mr. Nyamukapa has declined to comment. The police classified the incident as an assault, stating that the nurse acted instinctively and in self-defense. Sarah Lambert from the National Autistic Society stated, "Any disabled person living in residential care who sustains serious injury must have their case fully investigated. Abuse of those with autism and other disabilities by those who are supposed to care for and protect them is utterly abhorrent and is a disability hate crime." She further emphasized, "The police must reopen this case in order to send a clear message that any abuse by support staff is not only totally unacceptable, but a serious criminal offence." Daily care notes from Mr. Pullar's time at the hospital indicate that he had an accident involving his teeth just weeks after his arrival. His family was told at one point that he had bitten the floor, and he was taken to Bristol Dental Hospital where the alarm was raised. Internal notes obtained by Inside Out West suggest that the nurse was bitten and retaliated in order to remove his fingers, and later, that he had pushed and pulled to get them out. South Gloucestershire Council has declined to comment on Mr. Pullar's case. At the time, Castlebeck was the hospital's owner. The new board and management are currently reviewing what happened at Winterbourne View, and they have referred Mr. Nyamukapa twice to the Nursing & Midwifery Council, which declined to comment. Inside Out West airs on BBC One West on Mondays at 7.30pm and is available for seven days after on the iPlayer.
The 21-year-old has netted five goals from nine starts in the Championship this season, with two of those coming against Nottingham Forest on Tuesday. Manager Sean Coyle expresses his belief that the player will eventually become a reliable Premier League performer, stating, "I think he has the potential to be a genuine Premier League player." Coyle further mentions that players like Sam Gallagher and Tommie Hoban are the type of players he would consider investing in. Gallagher had 18 appearances in the top-flight for Southampton during the 2013-14 season, 15 of which were as a substitute, and scored in a win against Norwich. However, a severe knee injury hampered his progress, and he failed to score in 15 games during his loan spell at MK Dons last season. When asked if he would be interested in signing Gallagher permanently, Coyle responded, "I'd certainly like to - Tommie Hoban is another player I would like to sign." Coyle adds that Gallagher is young and has much to learn, but he tries to offer guidance and share his experience with the player daily. Gallagher is receptive to this advice, demonstrating his willingness to learn and improve.
In August, Denis Pitner, a Croatian tennis official, was given a one-year ban for disclosing a player's fitness status and unauthorized access to a betting account. Despite being cleared by the US Open on July 13 and receiving his credential, the USTA was unaware of the ban until it was brought to their attention. On Friday, the USTA acknowledged a flaw in their process as the reason for the oversight. Following The Guardian's revelation that Pitner had officiated while banned at the 2015 US Open, the USTA issued a statement expressing shock at the discovery. Last month, the BBC and Buzzfeed reported evidence of widespread suspected match-fixing in tennis. In response, the sport's governing bodies have announced that an independent review panel will conduct a thorough investigation into corruption allegations in tennis, which is expected to take at least a year. Pitner also officiated at the ATP Qatar Open in Doha in January, and the USTA has pledged to cooperate with the review panel to prevent any future occurrences. The USTA has emphasized that they take this matter very seriously and have made the investigation of the error their top priority.
The North Wales Fire and Rescue Authority suspended plans to eliminate one of Wrexham's fire engines in March, postponing the decision until after the May elections. The newly formed authority has not ruled out the possibility of this cut as they establish their budget plan for the following three years. Deputy Chief Fire Officer Dawn Docx stated that any cuts would be a last resort. Although no plans have been proposed to reduce front-line services, the Fire Brigades Union (FBU) expresses concern that the fire engine may face elimination as the authority attempts to manage budget pressures. Cerith Griffiths, from the FBU, warned that the elimination of the second engine would force firefighters to violate safety protocols to save lives. Under the service's safety rules, two fire engines are required at a house fire incident, known as a predetermined response. Mr. Griffiths explained that if the second engine were cut, firefighters would have to decide whether to wait critical minutes or enter a burning building without backup, potentially putting their own lives at risk. He referenced the Grenfell Tower disaster in London, stating that firefighters broke numerous protocols there, and if the second engine were unavailable, they should not enter such a property. Mr. Griffiths further explained that firefighters often push boundaries to save someone, but waiting half an hour for the second engine could be detrimental in a situation where every second counts, especially for those trapped. He emphasized that this could put lives in danger. In March, the fire authority withdrew proposals to eliminate one fire engine and 24 firefighter posts to bridge a £900,000 funding gap by 2020 due to public opposition. The station is the only one in north Wales equipped with three fire engines - two full-time and one part-time engine. The new authority is now preparing to establish its own budget plans and is seeking public input before publishing its draft financial strategy in November. Ms. Docx stated that no specific proposals have been made, but the service is exploring ways to save money without affecting front-line jobs. She suggested that a fire engine might be removed from a station, meaning crews would have to wait "slightly longer" for backup. Ms. Docx clarified that this would require a judgment call - whether to wait for the second fire engine or to proceed
On Tuesday morning, vandals allegedly defaced a national park with red spray paint, inscribing the phrase "[expletive] law," as stated by the National Park Service. To remove this graffiti, crews are employing a safe gel-type architectural paint stripper suitable for historic stone. The desecration of US Civil War memorials has ignited a contentious debate about race in the United States. Additionally, undecipherable graffiti was discovered, written in silver paint, on a nearby sign guiding tourists to the Smithsonian Institution on Washington's National Mall public park. Abraham Lincoln, who led the northern Union government to victory over the secessionist Confederacy and issued the Emancipation Proclamation in 1863, freeing African-American slaves, was assassinated in Washington just five days after General Robert E Lee surrendered, thereby ending the war. The statue of General Lee has become a focal point of controversy, following violent clashes between white supremacists and anti-racist protesters in Charlottesville, Virginia, over the removal of the monument. President Trump faced widespread criticism in the US media for failing to immediately condemn the violence, particularly from the Ku Klux Klan and neo-Nazi groups. In response to a 2015 hate attack in South Carolina, several cities across the US, including Charlottesville, have announced plans to remove statues of Confederate leaders. Following the weekend's violence, Baltimore, Maryland; Lexington, Kentucky; Memphis, Tennessee; and Jacksonville, Florida have also announced proposals to remove controversial monuments. On Monday, protesters in Durham, North Carolina, forcibly removed a bronze statue of a Confederate leader, with police recording the event. Durham County Sheriff Mike Andrews later stated that the police are reviewing the video to identify and prosecute those responsible for the statue's removal.
A woman, mother of ten, originally from east Belfast and a Protestant, converted to Catholicism following her marriage to Arthur McConville. After facing intimidation in east Belfast, the family relocated to west Belfast and settled in Divis Flats on the Lower Falls Road. Tragically, her husband passed away in 1971. In December 1972, she was abducted by the IRA from their home. Initially, there were rumors that she had been seen helping an injured British soldier by neighbors, while others accused her of being an informer. However, these allegations were disproven during a Northern Ireland Police Ombudsman investigation. Last year, one of Mrs. McConville's daughters, Agnes, shared her account of her mother's abduction in a BBC documentary for the first time. She recalled hearing her mother's terrified screams as she was forcibly taken from their home by several men and women and thrown into the back of a van. "That was the last time we saw her," Agnes said. It is believed that Jean McConville was held at multiple houses before she was shot. Her disappearance garnered significant media attention in the lead-up to Christmas, with her children appealing for information about their mother. However, their pleas remained unanswered. Within the community, republicans claimed she was merely laying low, and her story gradually faded from the headlines. In 1999, the Independent Commission for the Location of Victims' Remains was established, aiming to locate the remains of those who disappeared during the Troubles. The IRA admitted in that year that it was responsible for the murders and secret burials of nine of the Disappeared, including Jean McConville. Her body was eventually found on Shelling Hill Beach in County Louth, Republic of Ireland, in 2003. Irish police confirmed that she had died from a gunshot wound to the head. In the aftermath, the IRA issued an apology for the pain it had caused the families of the Disappeared, acknowledging that their suffering had persisted for far too long.
